## 2021 AIRWAY AND BREATHING

### Lecture Notes


#### Assessment Frameworks

The approach to paediatric emergencies relies on two primary assessment frameworks that guide initial evaluation and management. The ABCCCD framework systematizes the assessment in order of priority: A stands for Airway, B for Breathing, C for Circulation, followed by a second C for Coma, another C for Convulsion, and finally D for Dehydration. This hierarchical approach ensures that life-threatening airway and breathing problems are addressed before other conditions.

The 3TPR framework focuses on specific assessment factors, particularly in infants and young children. The three T's represent Tiny baby assessment, Temperature, and Trauma, while the three P's stand for Pallor, Poisoning, and Pain. This framework helps clinicians consider age-specific vulnerabilities and environmental factors that may contribute to the child's condition.

#### Breathing Assessment

Determining whether a child is breathing requires a systematic approach using the Look-Listen-Feel technique. Looking involves observing the chest to see if it is moving or if the child is active, talking, or crying—signs of adequate ventilation. Listening means paying attention to breath sounds from the nose or mouth. Feeling involves placing your hand at the child's nose or mouth to detect breath movement. This three-pronged assessment provides rapid, reliable confirmation of breathing status.

A child in respiratory distress will show multiple warning signs that require immediate attention. These include increased work of breathing, visible fatigue and distress, fast breathing measured by an elevated respiratory rate, and difficulty with normal activities such as talking, eating, or breastfeeding. Physical examination may reveal severe chest wall indrawing, where the chest wall retracts with each breath. Auditory signs include grunting—a short noise on expiration that indicates low oxygenation—and head nodding that occurs with each respiration, particularly in severely distressed infants.

Abnormal respiratory sounds carry specific clinical meanings. Stridor is a high-pitched, squeaky noise heard during both the inspiratory and expiratory phases of breathing, indicating upper airway obstruction from airway narrowing. Grunting, beyond the context of distress, generally indicates low oxygenation and requires urgent intervention. Wheeze indicates obstruction of the lower airway. Stertor or snoring sounds represent obstruction at the level of the oropharynx and tongue, distinct from the upper airway obstruction that produces stridor.

Central cyanosis—bluish or purplish discoloration of the tongue, inside the mouth, and skin—results from abnormally low oxygen levels in the blood. This must be distinguished from peripheral cyanosis and can occur even in severe anaemia. Cyanosis is assessed by examining the mouth and tongue. An oxygen saturation of less than 92% indicates dangerously low oxygen levels and constitutes an emergency requiring immediate intervention.

#### Airway Obstruction and Signs

Several conditions can obstruct or partially obstruct the airway, each producing characteristic signs. Gurgling sounds indicate pooled secretions or blood in the airway that requires suctioning. Stertor or snoring suggests soft palate obstruction. Stridor indicates upper airway obstruction due to airway narrowing and requires investigation for the underlying cause.

Croup is the most common infectious cause of upper airway obstruction in children and produces the characteristic stridor sound. Foreign body aspiration represents another important cause of airway obstruction, with coins and peanuts being among the most frequently encountered objects. Foreign body aspiration is a genuine emergency because the child faces the threat of complete airway obstruction and the aspiration can lead to obstructive respiratory failure, central respiratory failure, or tiredness with subsequent pneumonia. A thorough history should be obtained to assess the risk of foreign body aspiration. Epiglottitis, though less common than croup, can rapidly cause severe upper airway obstruction and must always be considered in the differential diagnosis.

#### Head Positioning for Airway Opening

The position of the child's head during airway management differs significantly between age groups due to anatomical differences. Infants have small-diameter airways and a soft, compressible windpipe that is easily obstructed. In infants, the head should be maintained in a neutral position with the nose pointing upward. Excessive backward head tilt must be avoided because it compresses and narrows the infant's windpipe, worsening obstruction. In contrast, older children can safely tolerate the head tilt-chin lift maneuver used in adults, where gentle pressure is applied to the forehead to produce a slight backward tilt of the head.

Before any head positioning or manipulation, always check for signs of head and neck trauma or history of falls that might have damaged the cervical spine. If trauma is suspected, alternative airway-opening techniques must be employed to avoid further injury. After performing any airway maneuver, the senior practitioner must verify its effectiveness using the Look-Listen-Feel assessment rather than assuming success from a single attempt.

#### Airway Opening Maneuvers

The head tilt-chin lift maneuver remains a standard airway-opening technique for patients without suspected cervical spine injury. This maneuver begins with assessment for trauma and proceeds with gentle head extension and chin elevation to open the airway.

The jaw thrust maneuver without head tilt is the safe technique when cervical spine injury is possible. The procedure involves placing both thumbs on the child's forehead while positioning 2 to 3 fingers under the angle of the jaw on both sides. The jaw is then lifted upward to displace the tongue away from the posterior pharynx. Importantly, the thumbs serve to keep the head in place and ensure the neck is not moved during the maneuver. This technique is safe for use in all ages and must be mastered by anyone managing pediatric airways in trauma settings.

#### Cervical Spine Management

Any child with suspected cervical spine injury requires careful attention to neck stabilization and alignment during all procedures. Assessment should include questioning about head or neck trauma or any falls that could have damaged the cervical spine, combined with visual inspection for bruises or other signs of trauma. Neck stabilization begins with manual support and progresses to more formal immobilization.

The log roll technique represents a critical procedure for children with suspected cervical spine injury. A senior person assumes responsibility and positions themselves at the top end of the child. This senior practitioner holds the child's head in order to keep the neck straight and in line with the body by placing fingers under the angle of the mandible with palms over the parietal regions. Two to three assistants carefully roll the child's body in line with the head and neck following the senior person's clear instructions. A further person examines the back for injuries. Notably, per rectal examination is NOT performed in children during this process.

Immobilization of the cervical spine involves multiple methods used individually or in combination. Manual support involves constant hands-on stabilization of the head and neck. Blocks, sandbags, rolled towels, or one-litre bags of intravenous fluid are placed on each side of the head to prevent movement. An appropriately sized rigid neck collar must be applied with the child lying on their back. A critical safety point must be emphasized: the child's head should NEVER be taped to the bed or trolley, as this creates a risk of vomiting and aspiration if the child develops nausea.

#### Airway Equipment

The oropharyngeal airway is a simple artificial airway device that moves the tongue away from the back of the throat and opens the upper airway. It is curved to fit the contours of the throat and must be long enough to reach the airways leading to the lungs. Oropharyngeal airways are typically only tolerated in children with a low level of consciousness. However, they may provoke vomiting, and if the child develops a gag reflex or begins to retch, the airway must be removed immediately as this indicates the child is regaining consciousness and will not tolerate the device.

The Guedel airway is a specific type of oropharyngeal airway that comes in seven sizes: 000, 00, 0, 1, 2, 3, and 4, each marked with a different colour for easy identification. Sizing is critical and is measured from the midpoint of the child's incisors to the angle of the mandible. An oversized Guedel airway can depress the epiglottis and obstruct the airway, while an undersized airway may allow the tongue to obstruct the airway. Insertion technique differs by age: in infants, the airway is inserted right side up using a tongue depressor to prevent tongue obstruction. In older children, the device can be inserted like an anaesthetic would insert it, with care to ensure the curved tip points upward without being obstructed by the tongue, then rotated 180 degrees to pass under the hard palate. In all children, if a gag reflex develops or the child begins to retch, the airway must be removed immediately.

The Yankaer suction tip is a firm plastic suction device with a large opening surrounded by a bulbous head. This design allows effective suctioning of secretions without damaging surrounding tissue. It provides superior control compared to softer flexible suction catheters and is longer and more effective at removing secretions from the airway. The Yankaer tip's design prevents plugging of the suction line and makes it the preferred device for oral suctioning in emergency situations.

#### Ambu Bag Use and Manual Ventilation

The Ambu bag is a hand-held device that delivers positive pressure ventilation to children requiring respiratory support. It consists of a squeezable bag with a one-way valve system and a face mask attachment. When the bag is compressed, the valve opens and forces air through the mask or artificial airway into the lungs. When the bag is released, the valve closes and exhalation occurs passively through an exhalation port. With exhalation, the bag refills with oxygen or room air, preparing for the next compression cycle.

Before using an Ambu bag, five essential checks must be performed. First, verify that the bag is connected to an oxygen source. Second, confirm that it has a functioning blow-off valve to prevent excessive pressure build-up. Third, ensure that the bag and mask fit the child appropriately. Fourth, check for any leaks in the system. Fifth, verify that the ventilation rate is appropriate—typically 2 seconds per breath for adequate inflation and exhalation time.

The C-grip represents the correct hand positioning technique for holding the mask in place during manual ventilation with an Ambu bag. Different mask types may be used depending on the child's age and size, but proper grip technique is essential regardless of mask type to ensure an adequate seal and prevent air leak.

Several common errors can prevent effective ventilation even when the basic setup is correct. Soft tissue compression under the chin can obstruct the airway—this error may occur even when the head position is appropriate, the reservoir is present, oxygen is attached, and the mask fits well. Addressing this requires careful attention to hand positioning. Other causes of failed ventilation include inappropriate head or jaw position, mouth closure, insufficient pressure application, mucus or blood obstructing the airway, presence of a foreign body, or a poor seal between mask and face. If the chest does not rise during ventilation, systematically address each of these potential causes.

The ideal ventilation rate when manually ventilating a child with an Ambu bag is 2 seconds per breath, allowing adequate time for both chest inflation and passive exhalation.

#### Abnormal Respiratory Sounds

Stridor is a high-pitched, squeaky noise heard during both the inspiratory and expiratory phases of breathing. It is caused by upper airway obstruction or narrowing. Common causes of stridor include croup (the most frequent infectious cause), foreign body aspiration (less common than croup), and epiglottitis. This sound must be distinguished from stertor, which is the snoring sound produced by obstruction at the level of the oropharynx and tongue rather than the upper airway.

Grunting is a short noise heard during exhalation that generally indicates low oxygenation in the child. It is a sign of respiratory distress requiring prompt assessment and intervention.

Wheeze indicates lower airway obstruction and requires evaluation for causes such as bronchospasm or aspiration.

#### Breathing Management Principles

The management of breathing problems in paediatric emergencies follows three main principles: manage the airway, give oxygen, and keep the child warm. These principles work together to optimize oxygenation and ventilation while maintaining normothermia.

When administering oxygen during resuscitation for respiratory or airway problems, always give the highest concentration of oxygen possible until you are satisfied with the child's breathing and oxygenation. This aggressive approach to oxygenation during emergencies contrasts with chronic oxygen management and is justified by the immediate threat to life.

#### Oxygen Sources

Two primary oxygen sources are available in emergency settings, each with distinct advantages and limitations. Oxygen cylinders are easy to use and require only a flow meter and tubing. They operate without electricity, making them reliable in any setting. However, they are relatively expensive to purchase and maintain, and ensuring a continuous supply can be logistically difficult in resource-limited settings.

Oxygen concentrators work by pumping room air through a zeolite canister to remove nitrogen and concentrate oxygen. They have a moderate initial cost, require little maintenance, and produce oxygen continuously at low ongoing cost. The critical disadvantage is their dependence on a continuous electrical supply. Oxygen concentrators produce approximately 5 L/min of oxygen, which is best suited for use with nasal prongs or nasal catheters that do not require high flow rates to achieve adequate oxygenation.

#### Oxygen Delivery Methods

Four methods are recommended for oxygen delivery in paediatric emergency settings, each suited to different clinical situations and oxygen concentration requirements.

Face masks with non-rebreather reservoir bags deliver the highest concentration of oxygen available, typically exceeding 80% when properly sealed to the face. The mask includes a one-way valve system that directs exhaled air away from the reservoir bag, preventing inhalation of room air and re-inhalation of exhaled air. This system ensures the highest possible oxygen concentration and is preferred when emergency situations demand maximum oxygenation.

Venturi face masks utilize a tube-shaped reservoir with a Venturi fitting at the end. These masks deliver variable oxygen concentrations ranging from 28 to 60% depending on the colour code of the Venturi fitting used. The specific percentage is determined by checking the colour marking on the bottom of the Venturi fitting. The percentage delivered depends critically on providing the correct flow rate of 100% oxygen to the oxygen tube fitting.

Nasal prongs consist of short tubes inserted into the child's nostrils. Correct application requires attention to right size, right way up, and right fixation to the child's face. It is essential to keep the nose clear of mucus because secretions can block the flow of oxygen. When 100% oxygen is supplied at a flow rate of 1 L/min in infants and 1 to 2 L/min in older children, nasal prongs deliver 30 to 35% oxygen concentration in the inspired air.

Nasal catheters are thin tubes made from nasogastric tubes or suction catheters, typically sized 6 or 8 French gauge (with 8 FG being preferred when using a suction catheter). The catheter is inserted into either nostril and secured in place, then connected to an oxygen source. The tip of the catheter should not be visible below the uvula. Like nasal prongs, correct application requires attention to right size, right way up, and right fixation. When 100% oxygen is supplied at a flow rate of 0.5 L/min in infants and 1 L/min in older children, nasal catheters deliver 35 to 40% oxygen concentration in the inspired air, providing slightly higher oxygenation than prongs at equivalent flow rates.

#### Choking Assessment and Initial Management

The first step in managing a choking child is to determine whether the child is actually choking. Once choking is suspected, the immediate priority is to assess the effectiveness of the child's cough. This assessment has critical implications for management.

The assessment of a choking child must address two dimensions: the effectiveness of the child's cough and the child's level of consciousness. These two factors together determine the appropriate management approach. A child with an effective cough and maintained consciousness requires a very different approach than a child with an ineffective cough.

An effective cough is recognized by several positive findings. The child should be able to speak, cry, and take breaths between coughs. If the child demonstrates these abilities, the cough is effective and the child's own spontaneous cough is more effective at relieving the obstruction than any externally imposed maneuver. Therefore, no intervention should be made. Instead, the child should be observed closely and assessed continuously for any change in cough effectiveness.

Signs that the cough is becoming ineffective include the cough becoming quiet or silent, inability to cry or speak, inability to take a breath, development of cyanosis, and beginning loss of consciousness. When any of these signs appear, the situation has escalated to an emergency. Call for help immediately and begin intervention maneuvers without delay.

The principle of supporting effective cough cannot be overemphasized: spontaneous cough is more effective than any externally imposed maneuver, and interrupting an effective cough with unnecessary interventions may worsen the outcome.

A child with a history of foreign body aspiration who shows increasing respiratory distress is in immediate danger of completely obstructing the airway. In this situation, attempts to remove the foreign body must be made instantly, but care must be taken not to push the object further into the airway with the intervention.

#### Choking Intervention Maneuvers

When a choking child develops an ineffective cough, action and intervention maneuvers are required immediately. The specific maneuvers differ according to the child's age, with infants requiring one approach and older children requiring another.

If a child becomes unconscious during choking maneuvers, discontinue the choking maneuvers immediately. Instead, begin ventilation with a bag and mask using high-flow oxygen, and perform cardiac compressions if there are no signs of circulation. The transition from choking management to resuscitation must be prompt and decisive.

#### Infant Choking Management

Infants with choking and an ineffective cough are managed using back blows combined with chest thrusts. Throughout all maneuvers, the infant's head should be kept in a head-down position, which aids in the expulsion of the foreign body through gravity.

Back blows are directed forcefully at the back of the infant, between the shoulder blades. These blows force the diaphragm upward and create a pressure wave in the airway that can dislodge the foreign body. The sequence is to perform 5 back blows, then reassess the infant's condition before proceeding to chest thrusts.

Chest thrusts for infants utilize a 2-finger compression technique. The location is precisely 1 finger breadth ABOVE the xiphisternum (the lower end of the breastbone). The compression depth should be 1/3 of the chest diameter and must be forceful enough to cause a compression wave in the airway. The infant should be maintained in a head-down position throughout. The sequence is to perform 5 chest thrusts, then reassess before recycling the cycle of back blows and chest thrusts. Continue this alternating sequence until the foreign body is expelled or the clinical status changes.

#### Older Child Choking Management

Older children with choking and an ineffective cough are managed using a combination of back blows and abdominal thrusts (the Heimlich maneuver). This differs from infant management because abdominal thrusts are not appropriate for infants.

Back blows in older children are directed forcefully at the back to force the diaphragm upward and create a compression wave in the airway. Perform 5 back blows and then reassess the child's condition.

The abdominal thrust or Heimlich maneuver involves positioning the fist above the navel and below the xiphisternum, then pulling the fist up and back with force to elevate the diaphragm and create a compression wave in the airway. The direction of pull is upward into the abdomen. Perform 5 abdominal thrusts, then reassess. This maneuver is not appropriate for infants.

The complete cycling sequence for an older child with ineffective cough involves performing 5 back blows and reassessing, then performing 5 chest thrusts or abdominal thrusts (depending on whether the child is transitioning between age categories) and reassessing, then repeating this cycle. Continue until the foreign body is expelled and the child can cough effectively, or until the child loses consciousness and resuscitation becomes necessary.

#### Choking Management Algorithm

A systematic approach to the choking child encompasses the following steps: First, determine whether choking is actually likely based on history and presentation. Second, assess the effectiveness of the child's cough—is the cough effective or ineffective?—and determine the child's level of consciousness—is the child conscious or unconscious? Third, if the cough is effective, observe the child continuously without intervening; reassess frequently. Fourth, if the cough is ineffective, call for help and begin intervention maneuvers immediately. Fifth, apply age-specific maneuvers: for infants, use back blows plus chest thrusts; for older children, use back blows plus abdominal thrusts. Sixth, if the child becomes unconscious during any maneuver, discontinue the choking maneuvers immediately and begin bag-mask ventilation with high-flow oxygen plus chest compressions if no signs of circulation are present. Seventh, repeat cycles of maneuvers (5 compressions or thrusts per technique) until the object is expelled, the child can cough effectively, or resuscitation becomes necessary.

This iterative approach ensures that spontaneous cough is maximally supported while intervention is provided when needed, and that transitions to resuscitation occur rapidly if the child deteriorates.

-

## 2022 CHILD RIGHTS  SA LEGISLATURE

### Lecture Notes


#### Definition and Characteristics of Childhood

A child is legally defined as a person below the age of 18 years. Childhood is not merely a chronological phase but a complex state characterized by growth, development, vulnerability, and dependency. These characteristics are shaped by the inherent abilities of the individual child, the family setting in which they develop, and the broader social context surrounding them. Understanding childhood in this multidimensional way is fundamental to appreciating why children require special protections and distinct legal frameworks from those applicable to adults.

#### Basic Needs of Children

Children have fundamental needs that must be met to ensure their survival, health, and development. These basic needs encompass food, water, shelter, and protection from harm. Beyond these physiological requirements, children require access to health care services and a supportive family environment that provides love and stimulation. Critically, children must be protected from harmful situations, including involvement in armed conflict. The recognition of these comprehensive needs forms the foundation for all child rights legislation.

#### United Nations Convention on the Rights of the Child

The United Nations Convention on the Rights of the Child (CRC) represents a watershed moment in international child protection. Developed in 1989 and implemented in 1990, the CRC recognizes the unique status, needs, and rights of children. The preamble of the Convention explicitly states that children need special care and protection, and it defines a child as any person under 18 years of age. South Africa ratified the CRC in 1995, committing itself to the principles and obligations outlined in the Convention.

#### General Principles of the CRC

The CRC is built upon four general principles that guide all its provisions. First is the right to survival and development, ensuring that children have access to the resources necessary to live and grow. Second is the right to equality and non-discrimination, protecting all children from prejudice based on any characteristic. Third is the duty to promote the best interests of children, requiring that children's welfare be a paramount consideration in all matters affecting them. Fourth is the right to be listened to and taken seriously, also framed as participation, recognizing that children have voices worth hearing in decisions that affect their lives.

#### Civil and Political Rights of Children

The CRC guarantees children a range of civil and political rights essential to their dignity and autonomy. These include the right to life, survival, and development; the right to equality and non-discrimination; and the right to dignity. Children have the right to birth registration, a name, and nationality, establishing their legal identity. The Convention protects the right to participation and the right to information, enabling children to engage with the decisions and information that affect them. Children are guaranteed freedom of expression and privacy and confidentiality. The CRC protects the right to physical and psychological integrity, the right to access courts, and the right to protection from abuse and exploitation.

#### Socio-economic Rights of Children

The CRC recognizes that children's wellbeing depends on access to social and economic resources. Children have the right to life, survival, and development, along with the right to an adequate standard of living. The Convention guarantees children the right to health and healthcare services, the right to social security including social assistance, and the right to education. Children have the right to water and sanitation, to housing and shelter, and to adequate food and nutrition. The CRC also protects the right to a clean, unpolluted environment and the right to protection from neglect, recognizing the interconnectedness of environmental and child welfare issues.

#### Child-Specific Rights

Beyond the rights shared with adults, the CRC identifies rights that are particular to children due to their developmental stage and vulnerability. These include the right to rest, leisure, and play, which recognize that childhood is not solely preparation for adulthood. Children have the right to family, parental, or alternative care, and the right to special protection within the criminal justice system when they come into contact with the law. Unaccompanied foreign children receive special protections. The CRC emphasizes that children's best interests must be of paramount importance in matters concerning them, and children have the right to be heard and taken seriously. Importantly, children have the right to guidance that respects their evolving capacities—meaning that the level of autonomy granted to a child should grow as they mature. Finally, the CRC protects children from being used in armed conflict.

#### African Charter on the Rights and Welfare of the Child

The African Charter on the Rights and Welfare of the Child was adopted in 1996 by the Organization of African Unity (OAU) to address the specific contexts and challenges of children in Africa. South Africa ratified the African Charter in 2000, becoming one of 38 states that have committed to its provisions. The purpose of the African Charter is to extend the principles of the CRC to include issues specific to children's rights in the African context, acknowledging that African children face particular vulnerabilities and require targeted protections grounded in continental values and realities.

#### South African Constitution and Bill of Rights

The South African Constitution provides explicit protections for the rights of all people, including children. Section 12(2)(c) of the Constitution guarantees everyone—including children—the right to bodily and psychological integrity, which includes the right not to be subjected to medical or scientific experiments without their informed consent. This section forms the legal foundation for medical consent principles in South African law. Section 27(1)(a) ensures that everybody has the right to have access to health care services, establishing healthcare as a fundamental right rather than a privilege. Section 28(2) places paramount importance on the best interests of the child, stating that a child's best interests are of paramount importance in every matter concerning the child.

The South African Bill of Rights in Section 28 enumerates specific rights for every child. These include the right to family care or equivalent care arrangements, access to basic nutrition, shelter, and health and social services. Every child has the right to protection from harm and exploitation, the right not to work (protecting them from child labor), the right not to be detained except as a last resort, the right to legal representation, and the right not to participate in armed conflict. These provisions reflect South Africa's commitment to treating children not merely as future citizens but as rights-bearing persons deserving of protection and provision in the present.

#### Age Thresholds in South African Law

South African legislation establishes specific age thresholds at which children acquire new legal capacities and responsibilities. The age of majority is 18 years, the point at which a person becomes a fully autonomous adult in law. However, the law recognizes that children mature at different rates and grants certain capacities at earlier ages depending on the context.

A child can obtain an identity document at 16 years, as provided in the Identification Act section 15. Under the South African Schools Act section 3(1), a child may leave school at 15 years upon completion of Grade 9. The law permits children to work legally from 15 years of age, as specified in the Basic Conditions of Employment Act sections 43 and 55(6)(b), though employment of children is subject to strict protections regarding the nature and hours of work. The right to choose domicile—that is, to establish one's place of legal residence—is granted at 18 years under the Domicile Act section 1.

Sexual consent thresholds reflect the law's concern with protecting children from sexual abuse. A child is deemed incapable of consenting to sexual activity until 12 years of age; from 12 years, the law recognizes a capacity to consent but subjects such consent to heightened scrutiny. At 16 years, the law presumes a child is capable of consenting to sexual activity, as provided in the Criminal Law (Sexual Offences and Related Matters) Amendment Act sections 15 and 16 read with section 1(1)(b) and section 56(2).

Medical consent capacity is recognized at 12 years with sufficient maturity. A child can donate blood at 14 years, as provided in the Human Tissue Act section 8(aa). The termination of pregnancy can be accessed at any age under the Choice on Termination of Pregnancy Act sections 5(2) and 5(3), reflecting the principle that reproductive autonomy is not contingent on adult status.

#### Medical Consent Rules in South African Law

South African medical law recognizes that consent capacity is age-dependent and maturity-dependent, varying by the nature of the medical intervention. For surgical treatment, children above 12 years who demonstrate sufficient maturity and capacity can consent to their own surgical care. Children below 12 years, or those above 12 years who lack sufficient maturity and capacity, require the assistance of a parent in addition to their own consent or assent to surgical treatment.

Access to contraceptives and condoms operates under different principles. Regardless of a child's age, maturity, or formal legal capacity, a child can access contraceptives, but this access requires medical advice and examination to ensure the choice is informed and medically appropriate.

For HIV testing, children above 12 years who demonstrate sufficient maturity and capacity can consent to testing. Children below 12 years are deemed to have sufficient maturity but not capacity, meaning they can participate in the decision-making process, but parental involvement is required for the test to proceed. Importantly, for all children regardless of age, pre- and post-test counseling is mandatory, ensuring that the child understands the implications of testing and receives support with the results.

Male circumcision presents a distinct legal framework. Boys above 16 years can undergo circumcision regardless of their individual maturity or capacity to consent; the age threshold alone grants this right. Importantly, every male child retains the right to refuse circumcision, ensuring that this medical procedure is not imposed without some form of assent.

For general medical treatment, the law distinguishes between minors above and below 18 years. For medical treatment of children above 18 years (effectively for 18-year-old minors still under parental authority in some contexts), the law requires both the consent of the parent and the consent or assent of the minor. For children below 18 years receiving general medical treatment, those with sufficient maturity but not formal capacity can be treated on the basis of their wishes, provided the health professional advises the minor to consult with their parent and respects the minor's decision where it appears sound.

#### Health Care Principles

Health professionals working with children must be guided by key principles that ensure child-centered, rights-respecting care. First, practitioners must recognize vulnerability in newborns, children, and adults, acknowledging that vulnerability does not diminish human dignity but rather indicates a need for specific protections and support. Health care for children should be holistic, encouraging play and stimulation as vital components of healing and development, not incidental to medical care. Appropriate support must be ensured for each child based on their developmental stage and individual needs.

Health professionals must create child-friendly environments that reduce fear and promote engagement with healthcare. This involves age-appropriate communication, comfort measures, and a physical environment designed for children. Participation should be actively fostered, ensuring that children receive information suited to their understanding, that their assent or consent is obtained, and that their voices influence decisions about their care. Finally, health professionals must facilitate access to suitable services beyond the medical encounter, including assisting with vital registration and ensuring that families know about and can access child grants and support programs.

#### Social Assistance and Child Grants

The Social Assistance Act in South Africa establishes the framework for government grants that provide crucial support to vulnerable children and families. The Act controls the distribution of grants and targets assistance to the poor; consequently, most grants available to families with children have a means test to ensure resources are directed where need is greatest. Multiple types of grants are available, including the Child Support Grant, Foster Care Grant, Care Dependency Grant, Temporary Grant-in-Aid, and Social Relief of Distress.

#### Child Support Grant

The Child Support Grant serves the purpose of poverty alleviation by providing cash support to caregivers of poor children. To be eligible for the Child Support Grant, the child must be less than 18 years of age and the caregiver must be older than 18 years. The income limits for the Child Support Grant are single parent earnings below R 48,000 per annum or married couple earnings below R 96,000 per annum. The benefits of the Child Support Grant include annual revision of the grant amount and the ability to claim all biological children in the family, with a maximum of six non-biological children per caregiver.

To apply for the Child Support Grant, applicants must provide proof of income, marriage or divorce certificates, and proof of guardianship. Applications are made through the local Department of Welfare office. The grant terminates when the child turns 18 years of age, upon the death of the child or primary caretaker, or if the primary caretaker ceases to care for the child.

#### Foster Care Grant

The Foster Care Grant is designed to ensure appropriate alternative care for children not cared for by their biological parents. Eligibility requires that both the applicant and child be residents of South Africa, that a court order indicates foster care status, and that the foster parent be a South African citizen, permanent resident, or refugee. Additionally, the child must remain in the care of the foster parent or parents. The Foster Care Grant provides R1040 per month to support the costs of caring for a foster child.

#### Care Dependency Grant

The Care Dependency Grant supports parents whose ability to work is limited because they are caring for a child with severe disability. The child must be under 18 years of age. The applicant must be a South African citizen, permanent resident, or refugee, and must be resident in South Africa. The income limits for the Care Dependency Grant are single parent earnings below R202,800 per annum or family earnings below R405,600 per annum. The Care Dependency Grant provides R1860 per month to support the care of children with severe disabilities.

#### Children's Act

The Children's Act represents a comprehensive legislative response to the needs of children in South Africa. The aims of the Children's Act are threefold: to support families in enabling them to ensure their children's well-being, to prevent abuse and neglect of children, and to ensure that children in need of care and protection receive appropriate care. The Act is particularly important for health professionals, who should view it as a one-stop reference for multiple child-related issues. The Children's Act provides guidance on child consent to medical treatment and surgical operations, HIV testing and disclosure, consent when a child lacks legal capacity, the age at which a child can access contraceptives, mandatory reporting requirements in cases of abuse and neglect, and definitions of what constitutes a child in need of care and protection. The Act also clarifies the legal definition and responsibilities of caregivers, providing health professionals with clear terms to guide their interactions with families.

#### Progressive Realisation and the Reasonableness Test

Child rights are increasingly understood through the lens of progressive realisation, a concept embedded in international human rights law. Progressive realisation imposes an obligation on the state to move as expeditiously and effectively as possible toward the full realization of children's rights. This is not an absolute obligation to achieve all rights immediately, but rather an obligation to make continuous progress. Plans for progressive realisation must include clear goals, specific timeframes, identification of responsible parties, and allocation of sufficient funds to support implementation. The state's efforts are assessed through the Reasonableness Test, which evaluates whether laws, policies, and programs are reasonably designed and implemented to advance children's rights.

The Reasonableness Test comprises several key components. First, conceptualisation requires that the design of a law, policy, or program must be such that it can reasonably be expected to support the realisation of the right. Second, the approach must be comprehensive, coherent, and coordinated across different government departments and levels of government. Third, appropriate financial and human resource allocation is essential; good intentions unsupported by resources cannot satisfy the test. Fourth, reasonable implementation means that the program is actually executed as designed, and that barriers to access are identified and addressed. Fifth, transparency and public knowledge are required; people cannot access rights they do not know exist. Finally, the approach must be balanced and flexible, capable of meeting short-term, urgent needs while working toward medium-term and long-term sustainable solutions. The Reasonableness Test provides a framework for holding states accountable for their progress toward children's rights, moving beyond mere rhetoric to tangible, measured advancement in children's lives.

-

## 2022 Neonatal infections 2021 UKZN 5th year tut

### Lecture Notes


#### Neonatal Immunity

Neonatal immunity is characterized as new and immature. This fundamental limitation underlies much of neonatal susceptibility to infection. Preterm neonates are at particularly high risk for infection because they are less capable of responding to infection due to immunological deficiencies, lack of IgM and IgA antibodies, and passive antibody transfer occurs later in gestation. The combination of an underdeveloped immune system with incomplete maternal antibody protection creates a critical vulnerability window in early life.

#### Definition and Pathophysiology of Neonatal Sepsis

Neonatal sepsis is defined as a clinical syndrome of bacteremia with systemic signs and symptoms of infection in the first 28 days of life. Understanding sepsis as a systemic inflammatory response to infection is essential for timely recognition and intervention.

#### Classification of Neonatal Infections

Neonatal infections are classified along two important axes: timing of acquisition and route of transmission.

By timing of acquisition, infections are divided into early-onset sepsis (birth to 7 days) and late-onset sepsis (8 days to 28 days, acquired in hospital, home, or community). Early-onset infections are primarily maternally derived, while late-onset infections reflect environmental exposure.

By route of acquisition, infections are classified as congenital (antenatal acquisition) or acquired (typically occurs later). Congenital infections have four main routes of transmission: transplacental, ascending through the birth canal, intrapartum exposure, and acquisition from the birth canal during delivery.

#### Risk Factors for Neonatal Infection

Multiple maternal and neonatal risk factors contribute to infection susceptibility. Maternal factors include infection (such as urinary tract infection and chorioamnionitis), prematurity, and colonization with Group B Streptococcus. Specific early risk factors are prematurity, chorioamnionitis, PROM (prolonged rupture of membranes) greater than 18 hours, and maternal colonization with Group B Streptococcus.

Late-onset risk factors include prematurity, invasive procedures, indwelling devices, ventilators, and VP shunts. Neonatal factors include immunological deficiencies, lack of IgM and IgA antibodies, skin barrier defects (particularly in preterm infants), and prolonged healthcare facility stay. Facility-related factors contributing to late-onset infection include overcrowding, poor hand-washing practices, and understaffing.

#### TORCH Pathogens and Congenital Infections

TORCH infections represent the major causes of congenital infection and include Toxoplasma, Other agents (parvovirus, varicella, hepatitis), Rubella, Cytomegalovirus, Herpes, HIV, and Syphilis. These pathogens can traverse the placental barrier and cause significant fetal and neonatal morbidity and mortality. Most congenital infections are preventable and treatable, especially if early diagnosed, leading to cure and control. A critical clinical principle is that if one congenital infection is present, clinicians must look for others.

#### Bacterial Pathogens in Acquired Neonatal Sepsis

Gram-positive organisms causing acquired neonatal sepsis include Group B Streptococcus (GBS), Streptococcus, Staphylococcus, and Listeria monocytogenes. Gram-negative organisms include Escherichia coli (E. coli) and Klebsiella. These organisms are typically acquired from maternal genital flora or the environment rather than transplacentally.

#### Clinical Presentation of Neonatal Infections

The clinical signs of neonatal sepsis are variable and nonspecific. Temperature instability is a hallmark finding. Respiratory manifestations include respiratory distress and apnoea. Feeding problems and vomiting indicate systemic illness. Neurological signs include lethargy and refusal to suckle. Dermatological findings include pallor, petechiae, and mottling of skin. Jaundice (both physiological and pathological) may be present. Hemodynamic signs include poor perfusion. Metabolic derangement is reflected in unstable blood glucose.

HIV-exposed neonates in the nursery are mostly asymptomatic; however, they may have other neonatal problems such as prematurity, meconium aspiration syndrome, or hypoxic-ischemic encephalopathy.

Syphilis presentation is particularly insidious. Infected neonates can be asymptomatic and missed if maternal status is not checked. The condition may become symptomatic later as late congenital syphilis (presentation up to 5 years of age), representing a tragic lost opportunity for early intervention. Early congenital syphilis presents with pneumonia (respiratory distress), rash, and hepatomegaly. Often one or more features are present, and clinicians must look for others including long bone X-ray findings. Late congenital syphilis includes earlier features with permanent morbidity and difficult treatment.

Key maternal factors in presenting congenital infections in the nursery are maternal history, exposure history, and disease state, which are key to recognizing congenital infections.

#### Investigations for Neonatal Infection

Nonspecific investigations include Full Blood Count (FBC), platelet count assessment, and white cell count (normal 5-20). The immature to total (IT) neutrophil ratio greater than 0.2 is predictive of infection. C Reactive protein is a useful inflammatory marker.

Specific culture investigations include blood, cerebrospinal fluid (CSF), urine, and superficial swabs. Serology for congenital infection is important for suspected TORCH infections.

Radiological investigations include X-rays of areas suspected to be affected.

Placental histology is important (noted as significant: NB) for diagnosis of congenital infections, particularly CMV (Cytomegalovirus), Rubella, Herpes, and Toxoplasmosis, which require specific laboratory tests and placental histology review.

#### Management of Neonatal Sepsis

Management of neonatal sepsis addresses both supportive care and antimicrobial therapy. Supportive care involves fluid management, electrolyte correction, glucose correction, respiratory support, and cardiovascular support. The specific approach depends on whether the infection is local versus systemic, congenital versus nosocomial.

First-line antimicrobial therapy for suspected neonatal sepsis is penicillin with or without aminoglycoside. Treatment duration depends on the site of infection: proven bacterial septicaemia in a neonate should be treated for 7 to 10 days, while confirmed bacterial meningitis in a neonate requires 21 days of antimicrobial therapy.

#### Prevention of Congenital Infections

Prevention strategies for congenital infections center on improved antenatal care and maternal screening. Syphilis testing must be performed and checked during pregnancy with clear documentation of results and treatment if needed per DOH guidelines. Prevention of Mother-To-Child Transmission (PMTCT) with ARV fast-tracking is essential for HIV-exposed neonates. TB screening during pregnancy identifies mothers with active disease. Education about respiratory and viral pathogen exposure reduces risk of acquisition during pregnancy.

The PMTCT 2020 antenatal strategy emphasizes Test, Test, Test at the 1st antenatal visit and every BANC (Basic Antenatal Care) visit, with an ideal target of 8 visits for optimal screening. Additional screening during PMTCT antenatal care includes viral load monitoring, TB screening and opportunistic infection (OI) screening, and TPR (Treponema pallidum—syphilis) testing.

The PMTCT 2020 treatment strategy uses Option B+ 2015: Start all HIV-positive pregnant women on ART as soon as possible. In labour, if tested positive, give TDF, 3TC, DLG (FDC) stat and continue lifelong next day with ART counselling and CHW (Community Health Worker) referral.

#### Prevention of Acquired and Nosocomial Infections

Prevention strategies for acquired and nosocomial neonatal infections include early skin-to-skin contact with mother (Kangaroo Mother Care), exclusive breastfeeding, strict hand washing, aseptic technique during procedures, and limiting duration of ventilation with active weaning.

#### South African Epidemiology and PMTCT

The impact of congenital infections is significant in South African maternal and child health. HIV is the 3rd leading cause of maternal mortality in South Africa. The infections prioritized in South African neonatal care are HIV/PMTCT, Syphilis (commonest cause of congenital infection in the SA context), TB, Hepatitis B (HBV), Malaria, Listeria monocytogenes, and COVID-19.

Congenital tuberculosis carries a high mortality burden, with 43.48% mortality (range 33.9-52.6%) reported in affected neonates.

The significance of maternal HIV testing frequency in PMTCT is critical: testing should occur at the first antenatal visit and ideally at every BANC visit. The peak mortality age of HIV-infected infants in South Africa is 2-4 months. Birth PCR (BPCR) implementation allows earlier diagnosis, enabling initiation of treatment before peak mortality. The target timeframe for ART initiation in HIV-infected neonates with reactive BPCR is within 7 days of birth, or at the 1st postnatal visit in primary health care settings.

If congenital syphilis is diagnosed, clinicians must actively seek HIV, TB, Hepatitis B, and malaria—a reminder that if one congenital infection is present, other infections must be excluded.

Special considerations apply to HIV-exposed premature neonates less than 35 weeks gestation, who require modified neonatal treatment regimens appropriate for prematurity and gestational age. Postnatal maternal care in PMTCT provides opportunities to care for the mother through postnatal check (CHW) and EPI (Expanded Programme on Immunization) visits.

Syphilis in pregnancy must be managed as a notifiable condition requiring appropriate follow-up and treatment in the postnatal period per DOH guidelines.

The DOH 4-Pillar approach to HIV prevention (Pillars 1+2) includes testing, couples counselling and partner testing, safe sex education (dual contraception), PEP (Post-Exposure Prophylaxis), PrEP (Pre-Exposure Prophylaxis), VMMC (Voluntary Medical Male Circumcision), and management of discordant couples.

#### Learning Objectives and Clinical Integration

The comprehensive learning goal for neonatal congenital infections is that most congenital infections are preventable and treatable, especially if early diagnosed with good outcomes (cure and control). If one infection is present, clinicians must look for others. The primary focus infections for neonatal care in South Africa are HIV/PMTCT and Syphilis (commonest causes). TB, Hepatitis B (HBV), and Malaria require a structured clinical approach per DOH guidelines. CMV (Cytomegalovirus), Rubella, Herpes, and Toxoplasmosis require knowledge of specific diagnostic tests and placental histology.

#### Reference Standards

The reference standards for neonatal infection management in South Africa are the 2020 Neonatal Guidelines and Drug Doses by Joolay and Horn (5th edition) and the National DOH PMTCT Guideline 2020.

## 2022 POISONING

### Lecture Notes


Poisoning in children represents a significant public health concern, ranking as the fourth biggest cause of unintentional injury after motor vehicle accidents, fires, and drowning in high and middle income countries. Understanding the epidemiology, pathophysiology, and management of poisoning is critical for pediatric practitioners. This lecture, instructed by Wayne Rajah across 44 slides, provides comprehensive coverage of poisoning epidemiology, pathophysiology, toxidromes, and evidence-based management strategies.

## Epidemiology and Mortality

The highest peak age for accidental poisoning in children is between 1 and 4 years, when children are most inquisitive and explore their environment without understanding consequences. Non-accidental poisoning peaks at 15 years of age, driven by adolescent peer pressure and risk-taking behavior despite greater awareness of consequences. Boys are more commonly affected than girls in poisoning incidents across all age groups.

Fatality rates remain low overall, with less than 2% of poisoning cases proving fatal in children. However, mortality follows a bimodal distribution, with the highest fatal poisoning rates occurring in children under 1 year and a secondary peak at 15 years of age.

## Physiologic Vulnerabilities in Children

Children face unique physiologic vulnerabilities that increase their susceptibility to toxins. Children under 4 months have increased skin and mucosal permeability to toxins compared to older children and adults, creating a window of heightened vulnerability during infancy. Decreased glycogen stores in children may contribute to metabolic complications during poisoning. Increased body surface area (BSA) relative to body mass in children leads to thermoregulatory issues that can complicate toxin-induced disease. Most critically, smaller body size means that the dose per kilogram is more toxic, amplifying the toxicity of ingested substances even at quantities that might be tolerable in adults.

## Risk Factors and Prevention

Risk factors for childhood poisoning fall into several categories. Young children are at high risk for accidental poisoning due to their behavior—they are naturally inquisitive, unable to read warning labels, and imitate adult behavior without understanding danger. Adolescents are at risk for non-accidental poisoning due to peer pressure and risk-taking behavior.

Primary prevention strategies aim to reduce exposure to poisoning agents. These include: (1) removing the poisoning agent from the environment entirely, (2) replacing a poisonous agent with a lower toxicity alternative (for example, paracetamol versus aspirin), (3) implementing legislation for child-resistant packaging, and (4) reducing toxicity by packaging substances in non-lethal concentrations or doses. Secondary prevention aims to prevent injury or illness once poisoning has occurred, utilizing poison control centres and decontamination methods to minimize harm.

## Assessment of Poisoning

When a poisoning is suspected, detailed history is essential. Key information includes: what toxic agent or medicine was involved, what amount was ingested, the route of exposure, when exposure occurred, any odours noted, whether the child was alert upon discovery, and any history of substance abuse. This history guides subsequent management and helps identify the specific toxin involved.

## Toxidromes: Recognizing Poisoning Patterns

Toxidromes are characteristic clinical syndromes that result from specific classes of toxins, allowing clinicians to recognize poisoning patterns and guide presumptive management before definitive toxin identification. Understanding toxidromes enables rapid clinical recognition and supports treatment decisions even when the exact toxin is unknown.

#### Cholinergic Toxidrome

Cholinergic toxidrome results from excessive cholinergic stimulation, typically from organophosphates, carbamates, or muscarinic agents. The syndrome presents with characteristic signs reflecting overstimulation of parasympathetic activity.

#### Anticholinergic Toxidrome

Anticholinergic toxidrome results from blockade of acetylcholine receptors, presenting with decreased parasympathetic activity and signs opposite to those of cholinergic poisoning.

#### Salicylism

Salicylism is the toxidrome resulting from salicylate poisoning from aspirin and related compounds, characterized by metabolic derangements and neurological effects that evolve through distinct phases.

#### Sedative Toxidrome

Sedative toxidrome is a clinical syndrome from CNS depressant poisoning, resulting in decreased consciousness and respiratory depression that can progress to severe respiratory failure.

#### Dystonic Toxidrome

Dystonic toxidrome presents with dystonia (abnormal muscle tone and posturing) from agents like antipsychotics or metoclopramide.

#### Sympathomimetic Toxidrome

Sympathomimetic toxidrome results from sympathetic nervous system overstimulation, causing hypertension, tachycardia, and agitation.

## Organophosphate Poisoning

Organophosphate poisoning results from exposure to organophosphate compounds used as pesticides, which irreversibly inhibit acetylcholinesterase leading to a cholinergic toxidrome. The severity of poisoning depends on the extent of acetylcholinesterase inhibition and the duration of exposure.

#### Clinical Presentation: SLUDGE Mnemonic

The characteristic signs of cholinergic toxidrome from organophosphate poisoning are remembered by the SLUDGE mnemonic:

- **S (Salivation)**: Excessive drooling and salivary production in cholinergic poisoning
- **L (Lacrimation)**: Excessive tearing
- **U (Urination)**: Involuntary urination (urinary incontinence)
- **D (Diaphoresis)**: Profuse sweating
- **G (GIT upset)**: Gastrointestinal symptoms including nausea, diarrhea, and abdominal pain
- **E (Emesis)**: Vomiting

These signs develop as acetylcholinesterase inhibition allows acetylcholine to accumulate at synapses, overstimulating muscarinic and nicotinic receptors throughout the body.

#### Management of Organophosphate Poisoning

Management of organophosphate poisoning follows the ABC approach, addressing life-threatening complications first. The three respiratory symptoms requiring immediate intervention—bradycardia, bronchorrhea (excessive bronchial secretions), and bronchospasm—are treated with atropine.

**Atropine Administration**: Atropine is given as a test dose, followed by repeat doses, and thereafter continued as a continuous infusion. The dose and rate of infusion are titrated to the clinical response, aiming to control respiratory and cardiovascular manifestations. Atropine should never be stopped abruptly because symptoms will recur immediately; instead, wean atropine gradually over 24 hours as the child stabilizes.

Seizures should be watched for during atropine treatment and treated accordingly with benzodiazepines or other anticonvulsants. The main limitation of atropine is that it has no effect on the paralysis caused by organophosphate poisoning—paralysis results from neuromuscular blockade and persists despite adequate atropinization.

**Pralidoxime (2-PAM)**: Pralidoxime is a cholinesterase reactivator used in conjunction with atropine in organophosphate poisoning. It must be given within 48 hours of exposure to be effective, as delayed administration does not reactivate the inhibited enzyme. Pralidoxime addresses the underlying acetylcholinesterase inhibition, complementing atropine's symptomatic management.

## Salicylate Poisoning

Salicylate poisoning results from aspirin (acetylsalicylic acid) or other salicylate-containing compound overdose. The pathophysiology evolves through three distinct phases, each with characteristic metabolic and clinical features.

#### Phase 1: Respiratory Alkalosis (0–24 hours)

Phase 1 of salicylate poisoning is characterized by hyperventilation due to direct stimulation of the respiratory center. This hyperventilation leads to respiratory alkalosis. Simultaneously, salicylates inhibit renal reabsorption of bicarbonate, causing compensatory alkaluria where potassium and bicarbonate are lost in the urine. Hypokalemia develops as potassium is wasted. Phase 1 may last as long as 12 hours and can appear deceptively mild.

#### Phase 2: Paradoxic Aciduria (hours to 24 hours)

Phase 2 of salicylate poisoning is characterized by paradoxic aciduria when sufficient potassium has been lost. As hypokalemia worsens, the kidney retains hydrogen ions to conserve potassium, resulting in urine that is acidic despite systemic alkalemia. This paradox reflects the kidney's priority on electrolyte conservation over acid-base balance.

#### Phase 3: Metabolic Acidosis (4–6 hours in children)

Phase 3 of salicylate poisoning, which occurs 4 to 6 hours after ingestion in children, is characterized by dehydration, hypokalaemia, and high anion gap metabolic acidosis. Salicylates uncouple oxidative phosphorylation in mitochondria, generating organic acids and excessive heat. The combination of dehydration, electrolyte loss, and metabolic acidosis creates a critical clinical state.

#### Neurological and Systemic Manifestations

As salicylate toxicity progresses, neurological symptoms develop in a characteristic sequence: agitation, delirium, hallucinations, convulsions, lethargy, and stupor. Hyperthermia (elevated body temperature) occurs in severe salicylate toxicity due to the metabolic effects of uncoupling. Hypoglycemia (low blood glucose) can occur as a metabolic complication. Multiple organ involvement reflects systemic effects affecting the respiratory system (hyperventilation and pulmonary edema), central nervous system (neurological symptoms), renal system (electrolyte losses), and metabolic systems (acidosis and hypoglycemia).

## Iron Poisoning

Iron poisoning results from overdose of iron supplements or iron-containing products, which are commonly found in prenatal vitamins and iron fortified products. The toxicity is related to the elemental iron content, not the total iron compound dose.

#### Mechanism and Organ System Effects

Iron is toxic in overdose because it causes direct corrosive injury to the gastrointestinal tract and generates free radicals that damage cellular structures throughout the body. Iron poisoning affects the gastrointestinal tract (the primary injury site), cardiovascular system, central nervous system, kidneys, and liver. Systemic toxicity follows absorption of iron into the bloodstream.

#### Gastrointestinal Manifestations

Gastrointestinal effects of iron poisoning include vomiting (often with blood indicating mucosal damage), abdominal pain, diarrhea, and hemorrhage. Hemorrhagic gastroenteritis can develop in severe cases, with potential for hypovolemic shock.

## Paracetamol Poisoning

Paracetamol (acetaminophen) poisoning causes hepatotoxicity leading to acute liver failure if not treated promptly. Despite being a widely available over-the-counter medication, paracetamol poisoning remains one of the most important causes of drug-induced liver failure in children.

#### Mechanism of Hepatotoxicity

Paracetamol toxicity occurs when glutathione stores are depleted and the toxic metabolite N-acetyl-p-benzoquinoneimine (NAPQI) accumulates, causing hepatocyte necrosis. In therapeutic doses, NAPQI is rapidly conjugated with glutathione and rendered non-toxic. In overdose, glutathione becomes depleted and NAPQI accumulates to toxic levels, binding covalently to hepatocyte proteins and causing cell death.

#### N-Acetylcysteine (NAC) Antidote

N-acetylcysteine (NAC) is the primary antidote for paracetamol poisoning. NAC replenishes glutathione stores and prevents hepatotoxicity by providing sulfhydryl groups for conjugation of NAPQI. NAC is most effective when given within the first 8 to 10 hours after paracetamol ingestion, but can still provide benefit when administered up to 24 hours or beyond if hepatotoxicity has not yet developed.

#### Clinical Phases of Paracetamol Poisoning

Paracetamol poisoning evolves through three phases:

- **Phase 1 (0–24 hours)**: Minimal symptoms. The child may be asymptomatic or have mild gastrointestinal symptoms (nausea, vomiting). This deceptively benign phase creates danger if not recognized early.
- **Phase 2 (24–72 hours)**: Apparent improvement with hepatotoxicity developing. Gastrointestinal symptoms resolve and the child appears to recover, but hepatotoxicity is developing silently. Liver enzymes begin to rise.
- **Phase 3 (72–96 hours)**: Peak hepatotoxicity with jaundice, coagulopathy, and encephalopathy. If not treated, this phase may progress to acute liver failure and death.

#### Risk Assessment: Rumack-Matthew Nomogram

Serum paracetamol concentration is plotted on the Rumack-Matthew nomogram at specific times (4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24 hours post-ingestion) to determine hepatotoxicity risk and the need for NAC treatment. The nomogram accounts for the time elapsed since ingestion, as the same serum concentration carries different risk at different times after ingestion. Levels below the risk line do not require treatment; levels on or above the line indicate hepatotoxicity risk and mandate NAC therapy.

#### Supportive Care

Supportive care in paracetamol poisoning includes monitoring liver function tests to assess hepatocyte injury, coagulation studies to detect coagulopathy, and blood glucose to identify hypoglycemia. Management of coagulopathy with fresh frozen plasma (FFP) is necessary if the prothrombin time is significantly prolonged. Close monitoring for encephalopathy, a sign of acute liver failure, guides decisions about transfer to a center capable of liver transplantation if required.

## Caustic and Corrosive Agent Poisoning

Caustic and corrosive agents are chemical substances that destroy or damage organic tissue through direct chemical action. They include strong acids (sulfuric acid, hydrochloric acid), strong alkalis (sodium hydroxide, potassium hydroxide), and other reactive industrial chemicals. Caustic ingestion in children is a serious emergency that can cause severe burns to the mouth, oesophagus, and stomach, potentially leading to perforation, mediastinitis, and death.

#### Sites and Extent of Injury

Caustic and corrosive ingestion primarily affects the mouth, oesophagus, and stomach, with potential for full-thickness burns and perforation. Alkalis typically cause deeper penetrating burns because they saponify tissue fats and dissolve proteins, causing liquefactive necrosis that extends through tissue layers. Acids cause coagulative necrosis that may be more superficial but still severely damages tissue.

#### Acute Management

The immediate management principle for caustic ingestion prioritizes preventing additional harm. Management includes: (1) stop ingestion immediately, (2) do NOT induce vomiting (vomiting causes further contact of caustic with tissues) or use activated charcoal, (3) dilute with small sips of water or milk (dilution reduces concentration and heat generation), (4) assess airway if burns involve the mouth, (5) urgent endoscopy to assess depth of injury. The goal of dilution is to reduce the chemical concentration, not to remove the substance completely.

#### Endoscopy and Risk Assessment

Endoscopy is essential to determine the extent and depth of tissue damage (superficial versus deep burns), assess perforation risk, and guide decisions on steroids, antibiotics, and surgical intervention. Endoscopy should be performed urgently, typically within the first 24 hours, to establish the degree of injury and plan management. Superficial (first-degree) burns may resolve without long-term sequelae, while deep (second and third-degree) burns risk stricture formation and perforation.

#### Complications

Complications of caustic ingestion include oesophageal stricture formation (developing days to weeks after the acute injury as scarring develops), gastric perforation (acute complication risking peritonitis), mediastinitis (if the esophageal perforation extends into the mediastinum), peritonitis (from gastric perforation), sepsis, and death if perforation occurs. These complications necessitate close monitoring and aggressive management.

## Hydrocarbon and Petrochemical Poisoning

Petrochemical poisoning results from ingestion or inhalation of petroleum-derived products such as petrol (gasoline), paraffin, kerosene, or other hydrocarbons. These substances are commonly found in homes for fuel, cleaning, and lamp oil purposes.

#### Route and Systemic Effects

Ingestion is the most common route of petrochemical poisoning in children, often through accidental swallowing of petrol or paraffin. Systemic effects of petrochemical (hydrocarbon) poisoning include gastrointestinal irritation (nausea, vomiting, diarrhea), central nervous system depression (lethargy, ataxia, loss of consciousness), and potential aspiration pneumonitis if aspirated into the respiratory tract.

#### Aspiration Pneumonitis: A Critical Complication

Aspiration pneumonitis is a serious complication occurring when petroleum products are aspirated into the lungs, causing chemical pneumonitis with cough, dyspnea (shortness of breath), and potential respiratory failure. The hydrocarbons cause direct chemical injury to the respiratory epithelium, leading to inflammation, increased permeability, and pulmonary edema.

Vomiting is particularly dangerous in petrochemical poisoning because it increases the risk of aspiration of hydrocarbons into the lungs, causing chemical pneumonitis. This is why inducing vomiting is contraindicated in hydrocarbon poisoning—the risk of aspiration pneumonitis outweighs any benefit from removing the substance from the stomach.

#### Management of Hydrocarbon Ingestion

Management of petrochemical ingestion includes: (1) do NOT induce vomiting (increases aspiration risk), (2) do NOT use activated charcoal (hydrocarbons are poorly absorbed and activated charcoal is ineffective), (3) supportive care with airway protection, (4) monitor for aspiration pneumonitis, (5) provide oxygen and mechanical ventilation if respiratory symptoms develop. The key is prevention of aspiration while providing supportive care for any systemic toxicity.

#### Monitoring and Observation

Monitoring after petrochemical ingestion includes observation for 6 to 12 hours for symptoms of aspiration (cough, dyspnea, wheezing), chest X-ray to detect pneumonitis (infiltrates may not appear immediately but develop over hours), and continuous pulse oximetry to detect hypoxemia. A normal initial chest X-ray does not exclude aspiration pneumonitis, as radiographic changes may develop over 6 to 12 hours after exposure.

#### Prognosis

Most cases of hydrocarbon aspiration pneumonitis resolve with supportive care and time, as the body gradually clears the aspirated material and the inflammatory response resolves. However, severe cases can progress to acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS), multi-organ failure, and death, particularly if aspiration is large volume or if secondary bacterial infection develops.

#### Prevention of Petrochemical Poisoning

Prevention includes proper storage of petrochemical products in child-resistant containers, storage away from food containers (to prevent accidental ingestion), and storage out of reach of children.

## General Approach to Poisoning Management

The general approach to any suspected poisoning in children follows a systematic framework: (1) ensure airway, breathing, circulation (ABC), (2) obtain detailed history of exposure, (3) identify the toxin (or likely toxin class), (4) assess toxidrome, (5) perform decontamination if appropriate, (6) administer antidotes if available, (7) provide supportive care, (8) monitor closely for complications.

Decontamination methods vary by route and toxin type and include: (1) dilution with water or milk for caustic agents (never induce vomiting), (2) activated charcoal for many toxins (must be given within hours of ingestion for effectiveness), (3) gastric lavage for recent ingestions (controversial and rarely used now), (4) skin decontamination by washing with water (for topical exposures), and (5) decontamination of eyes with water irrigation (for eye exposures).

## Poison Control Centers and Expert Resources

Poison control centres play a critical role in managing poisoning by providing expert consultation on the toxicity, management, and prognosis of poisonings. They offer guidance on specific antidotes, decontamination methods, and monitoring protocols tailored to the specific poison and dose involved. Consulting poison control should be routine in all suspected poisoning cases, providing access to expert knowledge that guides evidence-based management.

## Summary

Poisoning in children requires a systematic approach combining rapid recognition of toxidromes, accurate identification of the toxin when possible, and evidence-based management including decontamination, antidotes, and supportive care. Understanding the epidemiology of poisoning and the physiologic vulnerabilities of children informs both prevention strategies and acute management. The specific agents covered in this lecture—organophosphates, salicylates, iron, paracetamol, caustic agents, and hydrocarbons—represent common and serious poisonings in the pediatric population. Prompt recognition and appropriate management can prevent progression to severe toxicity and death.

-

## 2024 CYANOSIS

### Lecture Notes


#### Definition and Pathophysiology

Cyanosis is a bluish discolouration of the skin and mucous membranes that results from two primary mechanisms. The first occurs when the concentration of deoxygenated haemoglobin is excessive in blood. The second mechanism involves increased concentration of abnormal haemoglobin derivatives, such as methaemoglobinemia or sulphaemoglobinemia, in the superficial blood vessels. Understanding these fundamental pathways is essential for recognising and managing cyanotic presentations in clinical practice.

The underlying pathophysiology of central cyanosis involves five key mechanisms. The first is hypoventilation of the alveoli, where inadequate air exchange leads to insufficient oxygenation. Second, diffusion impairment occurs when barriers prevent oxygen from crossing the alveolar-capillary membrane. Third, ventilation-perfusion mismatch develops when areas of the lung are ventilated but not perfused, or vice versa. Fourth, right-to-left shunting at intracardiac or intrapulmonary vessels allows deoxygenated blood to bypass gas exchange entirely. Fifth, haemoglobinopathy that limits oxygen transport, such as methaemoglobin accumulation, impairs the blood's ability to carry oxygen regardless of how much is available.

#### Central Cyanosis

Central cyanosis presents as bluish discolouration of the highly vascularised areas, specifically the lips, tongue, and mucous membranes. Unlike peripheral cyanosis, which is caused by poor circulation, central cyanosis reflects a fundamental problem with oxygenation. The arterial oxygen saturation (SO₂) is reduced in central cyanosis, while cardiac output and temperature typically remain normal. This pattern helps distinguish central from peripheral causes and guides investigation toward cardiopulmonary pathology.

The differential diagnosis of central cyanosis is systematically approached using the PALMS mnemonic. P represents polycythaemia, an increase in red blood cell mass that can present with cyanosis due to high concentrations of deoxygenated haemoglobin. A stands for altitude, where reduced atmospheric oxygen pressure leads to lower arterial saturation. L encompasses lung disease, the most common category. M includes methaemaglobin and metabolic disorders such as hypoglycaemia. S represents shunt, referring to intracardiac or intrapulmonary right-to-left shunting of blood.

Pulmonary causes of central cyanosis subdivide into three categories. Alveolar hypoventilation results from central nervous system depression caused by asphyxia, sedation, or intracranial pathology. Neuromuscular diseases such as Guillain-Barré syndrome or Duchenne muscular dystrophy impair respiratory muscle function. Airway obstruction, exemplified by choanal stenosis, prevents adequate air entry to the lungs. Ventilation-perfusion mismatch occurs from both extrinsic compression of the lungs—including pneumothorax, pleural effusion, and thoracic dystrophies—and intrinsic compression from conditions like pneumonia, atelectasis, hyaline membrane disease, and asthma. Diffusion impairment, the third pulmonary mechanism, develops in pulmonary oedema and bronchopulmonary dysplasia, where the alveolar-capillary interface is thickened or fluid-filled.

Cardiac causes of central cyanosis involve structural heart defects. Decreased pulmonary blood flow occurs in tetralogy of Fallot and tricuspid atresia, both conditions in which blood bypasses the lungs due to right-to-left shunting. Increased pulmonary blood flow with systemic desaturation occurs in transposition of the great vessels, total anomalous pulmonary venous return, and truncus arteriosus, where mixing of oxygenated and deoxygenated blood occurs. Severe heart failure can present with cyanosis, particularly in coarctation of the aorta and hypoplastic left heart syndrome, where global perfusion is severely compromised.

Haemoglobinopathy as a cause of cyanosis includes methaemoglobinaemia, which may be congenital or secondary to toxin exposure, and sulphaemoglobinaemia, which is secondary to toxin exposure. These conditions reduce the oxygen-carrying capacity of blood or prevent oxygen binding to haemoglobin, resulting in tissue hypoxia and cyanotic appearance despite potentially normal oxygen saturation readings on standard pulse oximetry.

#### Peripheral Cyanosis

Peripheral cyanosis, in contrast to central cyanosis, involves bluish discolouration of the distal extremities only, sparing the lips, tongue, and mucous membranes. This localised presentation arises from increased extraction of oxygen from sluggish movement of blood through the capillaries. The systemic arterial oxygen saturation remains normal, indicating that the lung and heart are functioning adequately to oxygenate blood; rather, peripheral tissues are extracting more oxygen due to poor perfusion. Common mechanisms include peripheral vasoconstriction and polycythaemia.

The causes of peripheral cyanosis are remembered by the COLD mnemonic. C stands for cold exposure, where peripheral vasoconstriction reduces blood flow to extremities. O represents obstruction to blood flow, whether from thrombosis, embolism, or external compression. L encompasses left ventricular failure and shock states, where cardiac output is severely reduced.
