D stands for decreased cardiac output from any cause, including arrhythmias or severe systemic disease.

#### Distinguishing Central from Peripheral Cyanosis

Clinical discrimination between central and peripheral cyanosis is crucial for narrowing the differential diagnosis. The area of involvement differs markedly: central cyanosis is generalised, while peripheral cyanosis is localised to the distal extremities. Tongue involvement distinguishes central (involved) from peripheral (not involved) cyanosis. Temperature is warm in central cyanosis but cold in peripheral cyanosis. Clubbing, a chronic sign of hypoxaemia, is usually present in central cyanosis but absent in peripheral cyanosis.

The response to oxygen supplementation differs significantly. In central cyanosis due to pulmonary causes, oxygen supplementation produces improvement in the cyanotic appearance as oxygenation is restored. In peripheral cyanosis, oxygen supplementation produces no improvement because the problem is circulatory, not oxygenation. Warming the affected parts improves peripheral cyanosis by reversing peripheral vasoconstriction, whereas warming does not improve central cyanosis. The underlying mechanism differs: central cyanosis reflects reduction in oxygen saturation, while peripheral cyanosis reflects reduction in blood flow. Capillary refill time, measured by pressing the skin and observing how quickly colour returns, is less than 2 seconds in central cyanosis but greater than 2 seconds in peripheral cyanosis.

#### Factors Affecting Detection of Cyanosis

The clinical recognition of cyanosis is influenced by several patient and environmental factors. Haemoglobin concentration plays a critical role; cyanosis becomes more apparent at high levels of saturation because more deoxygenated haemoglobin is present in absolute terms. Conversely, fetal haemoglobin makes cyanosis more apparent at low levels of saturation. Skin pigmentation significantly affects the visibility of cyanosis; in patients with darker skin pigmentation, cyanosis is less apparent due to increased melanin masking the subtle colour change. These factors mean that absence of visible cyanosis does not exclude significant hypoxaemia, and that cyanosis may appear with relatively modest degrees of desaturation in some patients.

#### Investigation of Cyanosis

A systematic approach to investigating cyanosis begins with a saturation monitor (pulse oximetry) to quantify oxygen saturation, though recognising its limitations in methaemoglobinaemia. Haemoglobin or haematocrit measurement is essential because cyanosis depends on absolute deoxygenated haemoglobin concentration, not saturation percentage. Arterial blood gas analysis provides definitive assessment of oxygenation, ventilation, and acid-base status, distinguishing hypoxaemic from non-hypoxaemic causes. The hyperoxia test is a pivotal diagnostic tool: the child breathes 100% oxygen, and if saturation rises significantly (typically to above 90-95%), a pulmonary cause is likely; if saturation remains low despite supplemental oxygen, a cardiac shunt or methaemoglobinaemia is suggested. Septic screen including blood cultures and inflammatory markers helps exclude infection as an underlying cause. Electrocardiogram and echocardiography assess cardiac structure and function, identifying congenital heart defects or cardiac dysfunction. Haemoglobin electrophoresis is performed when haemoglobinopathy is suspected, confirming diagnoses such as methaemoglobinaemia or sulphaemoglobinaemia.

#### Management of Cyanosis

Treatment of cyanosis is pathophysiologically directed toward the underlying cause. The first principle is to treat the primary condition causing hypoxaemia or poor perfusion. Simultaneously, efforts must be made to optimise oxygenation through oxygen supplementation, airway management, or ventilatory support as indicated. Symptomatic management addresses comfort and prevents complications of chronic hypoxaemia, such as polycythaemia or clubbing progression. In acute presentations, rapid assessment and stabilisation takes precedence, while in chronic conditions, long-term management focuses on preventing complications and optimising quality of life.

-

## 2024 IMAM

### Lecture Notes


#### Definition and Classification of Malnutrition

Severe Acute Malnutrition (SAM) is defined by any of the following: Weight-for-height/length <-3 SD, Mid-Upper Arm Circumference (MUAC) <11.5 cm (for children >6 months), or bilateral pitting oedema. SAM is distinguished from Moderate Acute Malnutrition (MAM), which is defined as Weight-for-height/length between -2 and >-3 SD, or MUAC between 11.6 and 12.5 cm.

#### Danger Signs Requiring Immediate Intervention

SAM children presenting with any of the following danger signs require urgent intervention: hypoglycaemia, hypothermia, hypovolaemia, septic shock, severe dehydration (not in shock), severe anaemia, and eye signs of vitamin A deficiency.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Managing Hypoglycaemia

Low blood glucose in SAM is defined as <3 mmol/l. For asymptomatic hypoglycaemia, immediate feeding with F75 is the first-line approach. Alternatively, give sugar solution orally at 10 ml/kg, or a 50 ml bolus of 10% dextrose. After treatment, check blood glucose after 30 minutes; if normal, continue feeding and monitor every 3 hours. If blood glucose remains low, continue to check every 30 minutes until it rises above 3 mmol/l.

For symptomatic hypoglycaemia, administer IV Dextrose 10% at 2 ml/kg over 2-3 minutes, then check blood glucose after 30 minutes. Once the acute episode is managed, continue feeding and maintain 3-hourly monitoring.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Managing Hypothermia

Normal axillary temperature in children is 36.5–37.4°C. Any temperature <36.5°C indicates an urgent need to warm the child. Prevention of hypothermia is paramount and includes keeping the child covered, especially the head, at all times; avoiding drafts; keeping the child dry and changing wet nappies regularly; avoiding exposure during examination and bathing; maintaining the ambient environment at 25–30°C; ensuring regular, correct feeding; and checking axillary temperature every 3–4 hours.

Active rewarming measures are employed when hypothermia is present. These include skin-to-skin contact with the mother or caregiver, wrapping the child with warm blankets, placing a heater nearby, and using external warming devices such as heaters, hot-water bottles, or bear huggers. During rewarming, monitor body temperature every 30 minutes. Body temperature generally rises at a rate of 0.5–1°C per hour. Stop rewarming once body temperature has stabilised above 36.5°C to prevent overheating.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Managing Dehydration

Dehydration is assessed in three grades. No visible dehydration presents with normal general appearance. Some dehydration is indicated by sunken eyes, slow skin pinch turgor (>2 seconds), the child drinks eagerly, and irritability or restlessness. Severe dehydration shows sunken eyes, very slow skin pinch (>2 seconds), and poor drinking.

For diarrhoea without dehydration, show the mother how to give ORS using a cup and spoon with frequent small sips. Give 10 ml/kg of ORS after each loose stool until diarrhoea resolves. Continue feeding with F75 and reassess for dehydration frequently.

For diarrhoea with some or severe dehydration, give ORS at 5 ml/kg every 15 minutes (equivalent to 20 ml/kg/hour) for 4 hours, using frequent small sips. Continue to feed the child throughout. After 4 hours, reassess the level of dehydration by evaluating general condition, capillary filling time, level of consciousness, skin turgor, sunken eyes, respiratory rate, abdomen, urine output, and the number and quality of stools.

For IV fluid replacement in dehydration, use Darrow's half-strength solution with dextrose 5%. The infusion rates are weight-dependent: for children weighing 2–10 kg, give 10 ml/kg/hour; for 11–20 kg, give 8 ml/kg/hour; for 21–50 kg, give 6 ml/kg/hour. These IV fluids are given in addition to normal oral feed volumes and times.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Managing Shock

Compensated shock in SAM presents with delayed capillary filling time >3 seconds, increased pulse rate (>140–160 bpm), and cool peripheries. Late or preterminal shock is characterised by decreased level of consciousness (lethargic), decreased blood pressure, and decreased pulse volume.

Resuscitate shock with a fluid bolus using 0.9% Normal Saline or Ringers lactate. The volume is 10 ml/kg, given as a bolus over 20 minutes. Monitor closely for response and signs of fluid overload. If shock is resolved after the first bolus, proceed to manage dehydration. If there is no response, repeat the fluid bolus up to 3 times. After three 10 ml/kg boluses (total 30 ml/kg) with inadequate response, transfer the patient to a high-care bed for CVP monitoring and inotropic support.

Monitor for fluid overload during IV therapy, evidenced by further tachycardia, gallop rhythm, breathing that increases even faster, puffy eyelids, and enlargement of the liver.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Managing Anaemia

Very Severe Anaemia is defined as Hb <4 g/dL. Severe Anaemia is defined as Hb 4–6 g/dL with respiratory distress.

Blood transfusion is indicated using packed cells at 5–10 ml/kg, infused slowly over 4 hours. Blood transfusions should only be given in the first 24 hours if a child has very severe anaemia or severe anaemia with respiratory distress.

To prevent fluid overload during transfusion, give furosemide 1 mg/kg IV at the start and at the end of the transfusion. Watch for signs of fluid overload throughout.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Managing Electrolyte Abnormalities and Oedema

All SAM children have excess total body sodium, even though plasma sodium may be low. There is also deficiency of potassium and magnesium. These electrolyte imbalances can take up to 2 weeks to correct. Oedema in SAM is partly due to these electrolyte imbalances.

The critical principle in managing oedema in SAM is: do not treat oedema with a diuretic. Giving high sodium can kill. Therefore, electrolyte correction must occur gradually through appropriate nutrition and feeding, not through diuretic therapy.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Antibiotic Therapy

All SAM children receive antibiotic therapy initiated on the first day of admission. Gentamycin IV is given at 6 mg/kg once daily for 7 days.

For children with no danger signs who are alert and feeding well, Amoxicillin is given orally at 30 mg/kg per dose, 8-hourly for 5 days.

For all other children (those with danger signs or not meeting low-risk criteria), Ampicillin is given IV or IM at 50 mg/kg, 6-hourly for 7 days. Avoid IV infusions if possible; use a heparin lock to prevent fluid overload. The infusion hang time is 1 hour.

For severely ill children or those failing to improve after 48 hours on initial antibiotic therapy, use a third-generation cephalosporin such as Ceftriaxone IV or IM at 50 mg/kg per dose once daily. If meningitis is suspected, increase the dose to 80–100 mg/kg per dose.

#### Stabilisation and Rehabilitation Phases: Managing Worm Infestation

Mebendazole is given during the rehabilitation phase. For children aged 1–2 years, the dose is 100 mg orally, 12-hourly for 3 days. For children older than 2 years, a single dose of 500 mg is given immediately.

#### Stabilisation and Rehabilitation Phases: Micronutrient Supplementation

Folic acid: Give a stat dose of 5 mg on day 1. Do not give daily supplementation, as folic acid is already present in F75, F100, and RUTF. If the child is not receiving F75 or F100, prescribe folic acid at 2.5 mg daily.

Iron supplementation: Give only after the stabilisation phase is completed, typically during the rehabilitation phase. Iron is started once the child is gaining weight and oedema has resolved, usually after 7 days. The dosing during rehabilitation is 2 mg/kg of elemental iron per dose, 8-hourly with meals when the child is on F100. Stop iron supplementation when the child is taking the full prescribed amount of RUTF.

Vitamin A: Give a single age-specific high-dose vitamin A capsule only if the child has severe measles and/or clinical signs of vitamin A deficiency. For children without these complications who are receiving F75, F100, or RUTF, high-dose vitamin A supplementation is not required, as these therapeutic feeds contain adequate amounts.

Zinc: Children with SAM receiving Ready-To-Use therapeutic feeds (F75, F100, or RUTF) should not be given additional zinc supplements, even if they have diarrhoea, because these therapeutic feeds contain the recommended amounts of zinc for managing diarrhoea.

#### Stabilisation Phase: Feeding Principles and Protocols

F75 (Starter Formula) is the initial feed during stabilisation. For children without oedema, the volume is 130 ml/kg/day, distributed over 8 feeds. For children with severe oedema, reduce the volume to 100 ml/kg/day over 8 feeds. F75 provides 100 kcal/kg/day with protein at 0.9 g/kg/day and is low-lactose.

Lactose intolerance should be diagnosed only if copious watery diarrhoea occurs promptly after milk-based feeds are begun, the diarrhoea clearly improves when milk intake is reduced or stopped, and it recurs when milk is given again. Other diagnostic features include acidic faeces (pH <5.0) and the presence of increased levels of reducing substances in the faeces. For lactose intolerance, substitute an extensively hydrolysed feed, and reintroduce milk feeds gradually during the rehabilitation phase.

During F75 feeding, monitor and record: the amounts of feed offered and any leftover, vomiting (and reoffering of vomited amounts), the frequency of watery stools, and daily body weight. During the stabilisation phase, diarrhoea should gradually diminish. In oedematous children, expect gradual weight loss as fluid is mobilised.

For exclusively breastfed infants <6 months admitted as SAM, there is likely a problem with breastfeeding (low milk supply, incorrect suck, or poor latching) or the breastmilk is insufficient to meet increased nutritional requirements. For these infants, add F75 in addition to breastmilk, regardless of HIV status. Calculate the F75 volume according to stabilisation guidelines and allow the baby to breastfeed in-between F75 feeds.

Absolute contraindications to feeding exist in two situations: while shock is being corrected, and in cases of surgical abdominal emergency. In all other situations, feeding should be initiated as early as possible.

Feeding principles protect vulnerable organs. For the heart, avoid excess fluid and give the correct calculated volume of feed. For the kidneys, avoid excess fluid and excess sodium; give the correct volume and use a low-sodium feed. For the liver, avoid excess protein and provide adequate glucose; give a low-protein feed that is sugary and offered frequently. For the gut, avoid large meals; give small amounts and feed frequently.

#### Rehabilitation Phase: Transitioning to Higher-Calorie Feeds

For the first 1–2 days of the rehabilitation phase, replace the stabilising feed (F75) with the rehabilitation feed (F100 or DF100) at the same volume. The transition phase should be gradual over 2 days to avoid the risk of heart failure.

Ready-To-Use Therapeutic Food (RUTF) is an energy-dense, vitamin and mineral-enriched therapeutic food that is equivalent to F100. RUTF is a groundnut paste composed of vegetable fat, peanut butter, skimmed milk powder, lactoserum, maltodextrin, sugar, and a mineral and vitamin complex. Start RUTF once the child is tolerating oral feeds well during the rehabilitation phase. Increase the volume by 20 ml per day until some feed remains unfinished or the volume reaches 220 ml/kg.

During rehabilitation, the target for weight gain is moderate, defined as >1–2 g/kg/day, which indicates good positive weight gain.

#### Discharge Criteria

For children aged 0–6 months, discharge criteria are: established breastfeeding, bilateral pitting oedema resolved, child clinically well and alert, medical complications resolved, and persistent and good weight gain of >5 g/kg/day over 5 days.

For children aged 6–59 months, discharge criteria are: appetite returned and tolerating RUTF, child clinically well and alert, bilateral pitting oedema resolved, medical complications resolved, and persistent and good weight gain of >10 g/kg/day over 5 days.

Before discharge, investigate for tuberculosis by conducting a tuberculin skin test (TST) and chest X-ray, asking about TB contacts and symptoms. If the TST is negative, repeat it just before discharge and read it within 48 hours; record findings. Ensure HIV counselling and testing of the mother or caregiver was undertaken and record findings. Ensure information on family background and socio-economic status was obtained and reviewed by a social worker.

#### Follow-up After Discharge

Follow up at 2 weeks or 1 month after discharge at the Place Of Primary care Discharge (POPD). Continue dietetics review until the child is growing well. Maintain a malnutrition register and nutrition care plans with contact details and addresses of patients. Follow up with defaulting patients to prevent readmissions.

#### Special Considerations

For TB management in SAM, actively investigate for tuberculosis as soon as possible, recognising that TB is difficult to diagnose and confirm. Ask about TB contacts and symptoms. Perform a Tuberculin Skin Test (TST) and chest X-ray. If the TST is negative, repeat it just before discharge. If TB is clinically likely, presumptive TB treatment is reasonable, but once begun should be completed.

For HIV management in SAM, counsel the mother or caregiver and undertake HIV testing. Once the child enters the rehabilitation phase, commence antiretroviral therapy (ART) according to provincial guidelines. HIV-infected children with SAM should be closely monitored in the first 6–8 weeks following initiation of ART.

Sensory stimulation and development promotion during rehabilitation phase includes fostering cognitive skills, language skills, motor skills, exploratory skills, and social skills. All SAM patients must be referred to occupational therapy and speech therapy in the rehabilitation phase. All SAM patients must be referred to a social worker.

Special cases requiring continued inpatient care after completion of the rehabilitation phase include children remaining due to another medical condition, those unable to eat RUTF and ward diet, and those with significant social challenges.

-

## 2024 NON ACCIDENTAL INJURIES

### Lecture Notes


#### Historical Context and Overview

Child abuse has deep historical roots, evolving from infanticide in antiquity through abandonment in the medieval period to organized exploitation and abuse from the 13th to 19th centuries. Today, the modern paediatrician must approach suspected child abuse with systematic clinical acumen, understanding that it represents a significant cause of child morbidity and mortality across all socioeconomic strata.

#### Types of Child Abuse

Child abuse encompasses a spectrum of harmful behaviors perpetrated against children. The primary categories include physical abuse, sexual abuse, emotional and psychological maltreatment, medical neglect, nutritional neglect, intentional drugging, safety neglect, and organized abuse. Each form carries distinct clinical presentations and requires tailored clinical and medicolegal responses.

#### Physical Abuse: Epidemiology and Risk Factors

Physical abuse is a leading cause of childhood injury and death. The epidemiology reveals critical patterns: the average age of abused children is approximately 7 years, though the average age at fatality is notably younger at 3 years. Males are disproportionately affected compared to females. The distribution of fatal cases shows that approximately one-third occur in children under 6 months, one-third between 3 months and 3 years, and one-third in children over 3 years—demonstrating that younger, more vulnerable children face the highest risk of severe injury and death.

Socioeconomic factors play a significant role, though physical abuse is not exclusive to disadvantaged families; it occurs across all social classes. Key predisposing factors include a parental history of abuse, alcoholism and drug abuse, severe psychological disorders (present in approximately 10% of abuse cases), family breakdown, social isolation, poverty, and unemployment.

Certain child characteristics increase abuse risk. Children with behavioral disorders—including Attention Deficit Disorder, Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder, Conduct Disorder, and Oppositional Defiant Disorder—are at heightened risk. Children with learning difficulties, neurodevelopmental disorders such as Autism Spectrum Disorder, speech and language delays, visual or hearing impairments, and chronic illnesses (neurological, hepatic, renal, or cardiac) are similarly vulnerable.

#### Physical Abuse: Clinical Recognition and Presentation

Physical abuse is fundamentally determined by what the clinician observes. The pattern, age distribution, and anatomical location of injuries provide critical diagnostic clues. Several features should raise suspicion: delay in seeking medical attention, discordance between the reported history and the actual injury pattern, parents or caregivers seeking help from multiple different healthcare providers (inappropriate health-seeking behavior), and presentation to different health facilities at different times, including late at night.

Clinical features of blunt force trauma include haematoma, swelling, bruising, and subconjunctival hemorrhage. The clinician must examine the entire child, including the oral cavity and genitals, recording bruises by size, shape, position, color, and estimated age.

#### Differential Diagnosis of Physical Abuse

Several conditions may mimic physical abuse and must be excluded through careful evaluation: birth trauma, congenital syphilis, osteogenesis imperfecta, rickets, scurvy (presenting with poor wound healing and bleeding gums), bleeding disorders (haemophilia, meningococcemia, and idiopathic thrombocytopenic purpura), and skin diseases such as impetigo, chickenpox, and staphylococcal scalded skin syndrome.

#### Modes of Injury in Physical Abuse

Non-accidental injury is defined as deliberate action by a perpetrator that intentionally threatens and inflicts physical harm to a child. This contrasts with accidental injury, which results from unforeseen events causing external trauma without intent to harm. Critically, there should be no discordance between the reported history and the actual injury in accidental cases.

Specific modes of non-accidental injury include blunt force trauma from objects such as fists, bats, elbows, and wooden spoons; whipping injuries from implements including hosepipes, sjamboks, sticks, twigs, and wet face cloths; and thermal burns, including glove-and-stocking distribution burns from immersion in hot water and branding injuries from hot metallic objects.

#### Management of Physical Abuse: The Multidisciplinary Approach

Effective management of physical abuse requires a coordinated multidisciplinary team comprising a psychologist, social worker, medical doctor, nurse, and teachers. This integrated approach ensures comprehensive assessment of the child's physical health, psychological welfare, social circumstances, and educational needs.

#### Long-Term Consequences of Physical Abuse

Survivors of physical abuse face substantial psychological morbidity. Anxiety, depression, mood disorders, and personality disorders are common sequelae. Critically, survivors of abuse are themselves at elevated risk of perpetuating the cycle of abuse in their own families.

#### Sexual Abuse: Definitions and Frameworks

Two foundational definitions guide clinical and medicolegal practice. Kempe's definition characterizes sexual abuse as involvement of a child in sexual activity without consent, without understanding, and contrary to societal norms. Faller's definition emphasizes the element of power imbalance, defining sexual abuse as sexual activity involving a child in which a power differential exists.

#### Sexual Abuse: Epidemiology and Perpetrators

Sexual abuse affects children across all social classes, races, and religions. Epidemiological data show a female-to-male ratio of approximately 1:3 in girls and 1:5 in boys. Age-specific peaks differ by sex: girls show increased incidence before age 6 years and again in early adolescence, while boys are most commonly abused in the late first decade.

Over 80% of abused children are victimized by someone they know, with approximately two-thirds of perpetrators being family members. The abuser is frequently the primary caregiver. Perpetrators commonly experience financial stress, substance abuse, underachievement, low self-esteem, and jealous or manipulative personality traits. A history of abuse in the perpetrator's own childhood is common. The vast majority of perpetrators (>95%) are male; perpetration patterns range from isolated incidents to systematic grooming and repeated abuse.

#### Sexual Abuse: Behavioral Indicators and Presentation

Behavioral indicators provide crucial clinical clues to suspected sexual abuse. Presenting signs include poor concentration, learning difficulties, poor social skills, bullying victimization, separation anxiety, depression and generalized anxiety disorder, hyperactivity, behavioral disturbance, stealing, and inappropriate sexual behavior toward other children. These behavioral changes often reflect the child's attempt to cope with trauma and dysregulation.

Physical symptoms and signs of sexual abuse include recurrent abdominal pain, difficulty walking or sitting, painful micturition and recurrent urinary tract infections, secondary enuresis or fecal soiling, genital or penile discharge, abnormal vaginal or anal dilatation, genital or anal laceration and bruising, vaginal bleeding, and clinical or laboratory evidence of sexually transmitted infection.

#### Key Red-Flag Signs and Suspicious Circumstances

Several clinical scenarios should prompt immediate consideration of sexual abuse: unexplained injuries, discrepancy between the reported history and physical findings, delay in presentation for medical evaluation, alleged self-inflicted injuries, allegations of third-party infliction without corroboration, repeated injuries across visits, sexualized behavior inappropriate to developmental stage, and detection of sexually transmitted infection in a prepubertal child.

#### Differential Diagnosis of Sexual Abuse

Conditions that may simulate sexual abuse must be systematically excluded. These include bleeding disorders, straddle injuries (which require consistency between the reported mechanism and injury pattern—questioning the plausibility of mechanisms such as bicycle seat, palisade fence, concrete wall, car handbrake, shopping trolley, or broom handle contact), effects of anesthesia or conscious sedation, vulvovaginitis from non-abuse causes (worm infestation, poor hygiene, yeast infection, prolapsed anus secondary to acute gastroenteritis, dermatological conditions including lichen sclerosus et atrophicus and eczema, harsh soaps, allergic reactions, and chlorine exposure from swimming pools).

#### The J88 Form: Medico-Legal Documentation

The J88 form is the standardized report on medico-legal examination by a health care professional and forms the cornerstone of forensic documentation in sexual abuse cases in the South African context. The form comprises six pages: the initial report page with demographic and administrative details; detailed description of the medico-legal examination; history relevant to the alleged sexual offense; clinical findings; gynecological examination results and clinical conclusions; and diagrams for anatomical documentation of injuries.

Meticulous completion of the J88 is essential, as it serves both clinical and legal purposes, documenting findings with precision and creating an evidence record that may be presented in court proceedings.

#### The Adams Classification

The Adams Classification provides a standardized framework for categorizing genital findings in cases of suspected child sexual abuse. This classification describes the patterns of injuries observed in child sexual abuse and assists clinicians in distinguishing abuse-related findings from normal variants and other conditions.

#### Examination Technique: History-Taking

Obtaining a careful history is the foundation of assessment. Clinicians must document the circumstances of the alleged injury: how it occurred, the informants providing the account, dates, times, place, sequence of events, and persons present. The child must be interviewed directly without leading questions, and explicit inquiry must be made regarding the alleged perpetrator. The child's psychological state during disclosure should be documented—whether the child was crying, calm, distressed, or withdrawn—and a mental status examination should be performed, with particular attention to signs of post-traumatic stress disorder.

Additional historical elements include birth history and pregnancy details, growth trajectory, developmental milestones, immunization status, systemic review of symptoms, comprehensive family and social history, school attendance and performance history (including grades passed, failed, or condoned), teachers' reports, and administration of validated behavioral rating scales such as the Conner's scale.

#### Examination Technique: General Inspection and Physical Examination

The physical examination must be comprehensive and systematic. Bruises, the most common sign of abuse, should be recorded with precise documentation of size, shape, position, color, and estimated age. The entire body surface must be examined, including the oral cavity and genitals. In acute cases presenting within 72 hours—particularly when the child is uncooperative or perineal injury is present—examination under anesthesia should be considered to allow thorough inspection and evidence collection.

#### Examination Technique: Sexual Abuse Examination

Sexual abuse examination requires a structured, skilled approach conducted in a private environment. Key elements include: involvement of a skilled medical officer experienced in child sexual abuse assessment; presence of a third person as a chaperone and witness; thorough explanation of the examination procedure to the child; collection of all samples and photographs before washing the child; performance of complete general examination; and appropriate positioning based on age (small children may be examined on a parent's lap, while older children are typically positioned supine in the lithotomy position for genital examination).

#### Vaginal Examination Findings

The normal prepubertal vagina typically demonstrates a crescentic hymen, which is the most common normal finding. Complete absence of the hymen is extremely rare as a normal variant. When hymenal tears occur, they may heal leaving clefts or bumpy scar tissue at the healing margins. Findings should be carefully documented using clock-face notation to precisely record location of injuries.

A key principle guides interpretation: lack of physical evidence does not exclude abuse. Historical features—disparity between the reported history and injury pattern, delay in seeking medical evaluation—and behavioral findings—changes in behavior and recurrent injuries—must be integrated with physical findings to form a complete clinical assessment.

#### Anal Examination Findings

Anal examination may reveal several findings with varying significance. Anal dilatation can result from child sexual abuse but must be distinguished from other causes including stool in the rectum, chronic constipation, Crohn's disease, recurrent rectal prolapse, and effects of anesthesia or sedative medications. Anal fissures may be associated with constipation or abuse; fissures from abuse are often multiple or show signs of acute trauma. Anal skin tags represent healed anal fissures and require investigation of the cause. Mucocutaneous changes and complete anal dilatation warrant careful assessment. Examination should be performed with the child in the lateral decubitus position, and the examiner should note the child's pubertal stage.

#### Imaging and Photographic Documentation

Every physically abused child under 5 years of age requires skeletal survey consisting of anteroposterior views of the skull, chest, spine, and arms and legs. In suspected head injury, for medico-legal purposes, or when screening for previous injuries, computed tomography of the head should be performed. Retinal examination by an ophthalmologist with retinoscopy is essential to identify flame-shaped retinal hemorrhages.

In severe sexual abuse cases with physical signs, photographic documentation is critical for forensic and clinical purposes. Digital photography is acceptable provided images are printed immediately for the medical record.

#### Treatment and Medical Management

Treatment protocols for child sexual abuse include post-exposure prophylaxis to prevent sexually transmitted infection, empiric antibiotics for proven or suspected infection, pregnancy testing when appropriate, and management of injuries evident on initial presentation. Treatment decisions must be individualized based on the nature of the abuse and associated injuries.

#### PTSD Prevention and Management

Prevention of post-traumatic stress disorder should commence as early as possible and involves two key components. First, debriefing and disclosure support: the social worker facilitates the child's disclosure and initial processing, while a psychologist provides specialized counseling. The evidence is clear—the earlier this intervention, the better the outcome.

Treatment of established PTSD requires recognition of characteristic signs: sleep disturbance, change in appetite, separation anxiety, deterioration in school performance, general behavioral changes including hypervigilance, mood lability, and inappropriate sexualized behavior. Comprehensive psychological and psychiatric management is required.

#### Child Abuse Accommodation Syndrome

Understanding the typical trajectory of disclosure following sexual abuse is essential for clinicians. The Child Abuse Accommodation Syndrome describes six progressive stages: Helplessness (recognition that the abuse is occurring), Coercion to secrecy (the perpetrator's enforcement of silence), Entrapment (the victim's increasing sense of powerlessness and continued victimization), Accommodative behavior (the victim's adaptation and coping mechanisms), Disclosure (often delayed and presented in an unconvincing manner), and Recantation (retraction of the disclosure, often due to guilt, fear, or pressure). Understanding this sequence assists clinicians in interpreting a child's disclosure pattern.

#### The Multidisciplinary Team Approach

Effective management of child sexual abuse requires a coordinated multidisciplinary team including social workers (to facilitate disclosure and provide social support), psychologists (for specialized assessment and treatment), medical doctors (for medical assessment and documentation), nurses (for support and care), police officers from the Child Protection Unit (for investigation and protection), victim assistance officers (to support the child and family through the legal process), counselors (for ongoing psychological support), school authorities including teachers and principals (to monitor the child's adjustment and provide educational support), and court representatives (to facilitate legal proceedings and protection orders).

#### Health Worker Roles and Professional Responsibilities

Health care professionals have critical responsibilities in cases of suspected child abuse. These include: recognition and identification of abuse; accurate documentation of the extent of clinical findings, both physical and psychological; provision of appropriate medical and psychological treatment; and timely reporting to relevant authorities including child protection services, law enforcement, and social services. Failure to report suspected abuse represents a breach of professional duty and violates mandatory reporting legislation.

#### Professionals as Parents at Risk

Healthcare professionals including nurses, doctors, social workers, lawyers, police officers, and soldiers are themselves at risk of perpetrating abuse or failing to recognize it in their own children. These professionals commonly work extended hours, spend prolonged periods away from their families, and may have reduced vigilance regarding warning signs in their own children due to professional desensitization or personal denial.

#### Sibling Assessment and Family Considerations

When abuse is identified in one child, approximately 20% of siblings will have experienced abuse at some stage. Assessment must therefore include all siblings, as well as other children in homes or places of safety. Referral for comprehensive assessment and intervention should occur promptly. The concept of "opening a can of worms" or "Pandora's box" reflects the reality that disclosure of one child's abuse often reveals abuse of multiple family members and necessitates complex family intervention.

#### Long-Term Psychological Consequences of Sexual Abuse

Survivors of childhood sexual abuse are at elevated risk for multiple psychiatric and personality disorders, including mood disorders, personality disorders, depression, and anxiety disorders. The prognosis for survivors who also have underlying mental illness or a history of childhood sexual assault is guarded, requiring long-term specialized mental health care.

#### Systemic Challenges in Management

The management of child sexual abuse faces significant obstacles. These include fragmented systems of care, lack of dedicated units specifically managing sexual assault cases, and overloaded public sector hospitals. Training deficiencies result in insufficient numbers of skilled health care workers capable of providing specialized assessment and treatment. Families face inadequate support services, leading to high rates of follow-up default. Insufficient places of safety for children complicate protective placement. Extended delays between initial patient contact and court proceedings increase distress and reduce evidence quality. Safety concerns affect health workers, caregivers, and children. Staff managing sexual assault cases experience severe burnout and insufficient institutional support. Finally, inconsistent adherence to clinical guidelines compromises care quality.

#### South African Legislation Context

This lecture addresses non-accidental injuries within the South African legal and clinical framework, referencing the J88 form and other medicolegal documentation required by South African law. The National Child Protection Act and related legislation establish mandatory reporting requirements for all health care workers and provide the legal foundation for child protection interventions.

#### Conclusions and Clinical Practice

Child abuse is a common and significant problem affecting children across all populations. All health care workers must develop competence in recognizing and appropriately managing cases of suspected abuse. Management must adopt a holistic approach, addressing the child's physical health, psychological welfare, family dynamics, and legal protection. The ultimate aim of effective management is to prevent secondary trauma to survivors and their families, facilitating recovery and promoting long-term wellbeing.

-

## 2024 RES CONDITIONS

### Lecture Notes


#### Normal Respiratory Rate by Age

Respiratory rate varies significantly by age in paediatric patients. Newborns have the highest normal respiratory rates at 40 - 50 breaths per minute. In infants, this decreases to 25 - 40 breaths per minute. Between 1 and 5 years of age, the normal respiratory rate is 20 - 30 breaths per minute. Children aged 5 - 10 years should have a respiratory rate of 15 - 25 breaths per minute. By age 10 years and older, the respiratory rate approaches adult values of 15 - 20 breaths per minute. These cutoffs are essential for recognizing tachypnoea and assessing respiratory status in different age groups.

#### Cough Classification and Associated Conditions

The character and quality of a cough provides valuable diagnostic information in paediatric respiratory conditions. A bark cough is characteristic of subglottic obstruction and often sounds like a seal barking. Brassy cough indicates tracheal compression and typically has a metallic, hollow quality. Paroxysmal cough with a characteristic whoop is pathognomonic for pertussis and reflects the intense inspiratory effort following prolonged expiratory bursts.

#### Chest Wall Deformities

Abnormalities of chest wall structure may indicate underlying systemic disease or be functionally significant. Pectus excavatum presents as a caved-in or sunken appearance of the chest and is characteristically associated with Marfan syndrome. Pectus carinatum, conversely, shows protrusion of the sternum and ribs and is associated with Noonan syndrome. A barrel chest develops secondary to chronic hyperinflation, particularly in conditions causing air trapping.

#### Recognition of Respiratory Distress

Respiratory distress manifests through several clinical signs that indicate increased work of breathing. Recession—inward movement of the chest wall during inspiration—is classified by anatomical location. Tracheal tug represents recession at the level of the trachea. Supraclavicular recession occurs above the clavicles. Intercostal recession occurs between the ribs, while subcostal recession occurs below the rib cage. Nasal flaring represents use of accessory muscles of inspiration, as does abdominal breathing and head bobbing, the latter secondary to sternocleidomastoid contractions. General signs of increased work of breathing include difficulty speaking or feeding, expiratory grunting, restlessness and agitation, drowsiness, and cyanosis.

#### Acute Tonsillitis

Acute tonsillitis is one of the most common infections in paediatric practice. Typical symptoms include sore throat, fever, and pain on swallowing. The condition progresses through characteristic inflammatory stages. The parenchymatous stage presents with uniformly inflamed, hyperaemic tonsils. The follicular stage develops yellow exudate within the tonsillar crypts. In the membranous stage, this exudate coalesces to form a visible membrane covering the tonsillar surface.

First-line antibiotic treatment consists of penicillin or amoxycillin for 7 - 10 days. Without appropriate treatment or despite treatment, complications may arise. Chronic tonsillitis can develop with repeated infection. Peri-tonsillar abscess, also known as Quinsy, represents purulent collection adjacent to the tonsil. Para-pharyngeal abscess develops between the pharyngeal constrictor and superior pharyngeal constrictor muscles.

Recurrent tonsillitis is defined by specific frequency criteria: more than 7 episodes per year in a single year, or more than 5 episodes per year over 2 consecutive years, or more than 3 episodes per year over 3 consecutive years. Recognition of this pattern influences management decisions regarding potential tonsillectomy.

#### Epidemiology and Risk Factors for Acute Respiratory Infection

Acute respiratory infection (ARI) remains a leading cause of morbidity and mortality in paediatric populations. In South Africa, ARI accounts for 20% of deaths in children under 5 years of age. Multiple factors influence the incidence of ARI. Breastfeeding status provides significant protective benefit through passive immunization and improved nutrition. Nutritional status directly correlates with immune competence and infection risk. Socio-economic status, particularly poverty and overcrowding, increases transmission. HIV status profoundly affects susceptibility to severe infection. Immunization status determines protection against vaccine-preventable causes. Parental smoking increases respiratory epithelial irritation and infection risk. Parasitic infection may impair immune function. Structural abnormalities of the airways predispose to infection. Environmental factors including rainy and cold weather correlate with increased ARI incidence.

#### Microbiology of Respiratory Tract Infections

Different sites of respiratory infection have characteristic microbial causes. Rhinitis, the commonest upper respiratory tract infection, is exclusively viral in aetiology. Otitis media is caused by bacteria, particularly Streptococcus pneumoniae and Haemophilus influenzae. Tonsillitis and pharyngitis are caused by Streptococcus species. Sinusitis may be viral or bacterial, with S. pneumoniae and H. influenzae being common bacterial pathogens. Epiglottitis is caused by Haemophilus influenzae and represents a medical emergency. Laryngotracheobronchitis (croup) is typically viral, caused by Parainfluenza types 1, 2, and 3, or measles. Tracheobronchitis is exclusively viral. Bronchiolitis is caused by respiratory syncytial virus (RSV), Adenovirus, or Parainfluenza. Bacterial pneumonia is caused by Streptococcus pneumoniae, Staphylococcus aureus, Haemophilus influenzae, or Mycobacterium tuberculosis. Viral pneumonia may be caused by RSV, Adenovirus, Influenza, or COVID-19.

#### Pneumonia: Antibiotic Regimens

Community-acquired pneumonia (CAP) treatment depends critically on age and immune status. In children under 3 months of age or in immunosuppressed children, the regimen is ampicillin/gentamycin. For children older than 3 months with uncomplicated CAP, ampicillin/amoxil is used. When aspiration risk or Staphylococcus aureus coverage is needed, augmentin (amoxicillin-clavulanate) is the choice.

Hospital-acquired pneumonia requires more broad-spectrum coverage. The second-line antibiotic regimen is piperlillin-tazobactam/amikacin, reserved for cases with normal renal function and no CNS involvement concerns. For resistant organisms, carbapenem-class antibiotics represent third-line therapy.

#### Stridor and Upper Airway Obstruction

Stridor is a high-pitched breathing sound indicating partial airway obstruction. Characteristically, stridor begins during inspiration. As obstruction worsens, it may become biphasic, occurring during both inspiration and expiration. Stridor indicates primarily extrathoracic airway obstruction. Associated hoarseness suggests vocal cord involvement. Stridor that changes with head and neck position indicates supra-glottic aetiology.

Upper airway obstruction has multiple aetiologies categorized by acuity and mechanism. Acute infectious causes include laryngotracheobronchitis (croup), epiglottitis, bacterial tracheitis, retropharyngeal abscess, and paratracheal gland enlargement. Acute mechanical causes include foreign body and tumour. Acute allergic causes include angioneurotic oedema. Acute traumatic causes include intubation injury and thermal injury.

Chronic congenital causes include laryngomalacia, tracheomalacia, laryngeal cysts or clefts, and vascular rings. Chronic infectious causes include laryngeal papillomatosis and hypertrophy of tonsils and adenoids. Chronic traumatic causes include subglottic stenosis.

#### Laryngomalacia

Laryngomalacia is the most common congenital abnormality affecting the larynx. The condition is characterized by flaccidity of the supra-glottic region, sometimes described as a 'floppy voice box'. Affected infants develop inspiratory stridor from birth. Notably, the stridor may disappear when the infant is in a prone position and becomes absent or softer when the infant is asleep. The condition has a favourable natural history; mild stridor improves with age and completely disappears by 1 - 2 years of age without intervention in most cases.

Diagnosis is confirmed by visualization using a flexible pharyngo-laryngoscope. Management of mild cases is supportive and expectant. However, moderate to severe laryngomalacia complicated by upper respiratory tract infection requires admission, provision of humidified oxygen, and adrenaline nebulization to reduce subglottic oedema.

#### Croup (Laryngotracheobronchitis)

Croup is the most common cause of stridor in infancy and occurs most frequently between 6 months and 2 years of age. The typical causative viruses are Parainfluenza types 1, 2, and 3, and respiratory syncytial virus (RSV). In developing countries, measles and herpes simplex virus remain common causes of severe croup. The virus causes inflammation of the subglottic larynx and proximal trachea, resulting in the characteristic seal-like barky cough and stridor.

#### Wheeze and Lower Airway Obstruction

Wheeze is a musical breathing sound indicating small or large airway disease or obstruction. It is classically associated with hyperinflation of the lungs and development of a barrel-shaped chest. The wheeze is typically more prominent during expiration, particularly early in the course of obstruction. Wheeze represents partial obstruction within the intrathoracic airways.

The causes of wheezing in children are diverse. Acute wheezing is commonly caused by reactive airway disease (asthma), bronchial oedema from bronchiolitis, foreign body or aspiration, eosinophilic pneumonia, cardiac failure, cystic fibrosis, and bronchiectasis. Chronic or recurrent wheeze may indicate hypersensitivity reactions such as aspergillosis or cystic fibrosis, dynamic airway collapse from bronchomalacia, airway compression from vascular rings or enlarged lymph nodes, aspiration from gastro-oesophageal reflux or tracheo-oesophageal fistula, failure to clear secretions as in cystic fibrosis, intrinsic airway lesions such as tumours or tracheal stenosis, congestive heart failure, or parasitic infection causing Loeffler syndrome.

#### Asthma Diagnosis

Asthma should be strongly considered in ALL children presenting with recurrent airway obstruction that is relieved by bronchodilators. This diagnostic principle is critical because early recognition and treatment can prevent progression and reduce morbidity. Signs suggesting asthma diagnosis include a family history of asthma in parents and clinical signs of atopy such as eczema, allergic rhinitis, or food allergy.

#### Acute Viral Bronchiolitis

Acute viral bronchiolitis is the most common respiratory tract infection in infants up to 2 years of age. The peak incidence occurs at 3 - 4 months of age. Certain populations are at increased risk for severe disease, including preterm infants, those with congenital heart disease, and those with chronic lung disease (particularly those with bronchopulmonary dysplasia). Respiratory syncytial virus (RSV) is the most common causative agent, though Adenovirus and Parainfluenza also cause bronchiolitis.

The pathophysiology involves viral infection of the bronchiolar epithelium. This causes mucosal oedema and desquamation of the bronchiolar epithelium, leading to small airway obstruction. The obstruction causes increased airway resistance and air trapping, particularly during expiration. This leads to hyperinflation of the distal alveoli and the characteristic radiological and clinical findings of bronchiolitis. Most cases are self-limited, resolving over 7 - 10 days, though severe cases may require hospitalization for oxygen support and monitoring.

-

## 2024 TUBERCULOSIS

### Lecture Notes


#### Epidemiology and WHO Strategy

Tuberculosis remains a significant global health burden, particularly in children. The WHO End TB Strategy (2016-2035) sets ambitious targets: a 90% reduction in TB deaths and an 80% reduction in TB incidence by 2035. In paediatric populations, TB incidence is approximately half that observed in adults, yet the disease carries particular severity in young children. The progression from infection to active disease is age-dependent: 50% of infants under 12 months progress to active TB disease after primary infection, and 10-20% of infants develop complicated TB including miliary TB and TB meningitis. These statistics underscore the critical importance of early detection, preventive therapy, and appropriate management in the paediatric age group.

#### Definitions and Classification

Understanding TB requires clarity on key terminology. **Adherence** is defined as the extent to which a person's behaviour corresponds with agreed recommendations from a healthcare worker for taking medication, following a diet and/or making lifestyle changes. **Adolescents** are defined by WHO as persons aged 15 to 19 years.

An **index patient** is a person with infectious pulmonary TB who is newly diagnosed. **Latent TB infection** represents a state of immune response to stimulation by Mycobacterium tuberculosis antigens, indicated by a positive tuberculin skin test (TST) or interferon-gamma release assay (IGRA) test, with no evidence of active TB disease. In contrast, **TB disease** is caused by Mycobacterium tuberculosis and can either be bacteriologically confirmed or clinically diagnosed.

Contact classification is crucial for management decisions. **Recent TB exposure** refers to contact with a person diagnosed with pulmonary TB in the last 12 months, which may be from the same household or close contacts outside the household (care providers, colleagues, teachers, family members or friends). **Significant TB exposure** occurs when there is known exposure to a person with pulmonary TB who shared the same enclosed space for one or more nights or for frequent or extended daytime periods during the three months before the index patient started their TB treatment. A **TB contact** is defined as all people (family members and other individuals regardless of age and HIV status) who have had a significant TB exposure.

**TB exposed infants** are infants born to mothers diagnosed with TB before or after the baby was born, or infants with other documented close TB exposure (e.g., another caregiver, family member, daycare provider).

Children weighing >25 kg (typically eight to ten years of age) can be treated with adult doses of TB medications, marking an important transition point in dosing considerations.

#### Pathogenesis and Immunopathology

Mycobacterium tuberculosis is a slow-growing, rod-shaped bacterium with bacilli ranging in length from 1-10 micrometers. Children under 10 years of age rarely develop lung cavities and are therefore less likely to transmit TB to others, distinguishing the paediatric epidemiology from adult disease.

Key factors determining TB disease progression in children include the age of the child (particularly those <5 years), the time since exposure or infection (approximately 1 year), and the immune status of the child. These factors interact to determine whether primary TB infection resolves, remains latent, or progresses to active disease.

**Mycobacterial Cell Wall Components and Immune Evasion:**

The cell wall of MTB contains several highly pathogenic components. Heat shock proteins are highly immunogenic and trigger strong immune responses. Cord factor, a glycolipid, directly induces granuloma formation. LAM (lipoarabinomannan) represents 15% of the bacterial mass and profoundly affects the host immune response. LAM inhibits macrophage activation by interferon gamma and induces macrophages to secrete IL-10 (which suppresses TB-induced T cell proliferation) and TNF-alpha (causing fever, weight loss, and tissue damage). This multifaceted immune evasion strategy explains why TB infection can persist despite immune activation.

**The Ghon Complex and Primary TB Infection:**

Following initial infection, the Ghon focus develops as the initial inflammatory reaction site in the lung parenchyma where TB organisms deposit. This occurs within 2-3 weeks of infection and may subsequently resolve and calcify. The **Ghon complex** consists of the Ghon focus in the lung parenchyma together with enlarged regional hilar lymph nodes, representing a controlled and retained non-proliferating state of Mycobacterium.

Primary TB infection in children has three possible outcomes: (1) clearance by host immunity (primary infection resolves), (2) suppression into an inactive form (latent TB) with a calcified scar visible on X-ray, or (3) spread and progression to active TB disease.

**Granuloma Formation and TB Progression:**

**CD4 Th cells** secrete interferon gamma, causing activation of macrophages into true killer cells, formation of reactive nitrogen intermediates, and epithelioid granuloma formation in clearance of mycobacterium. **CD8+ T suppressor cells** kill macrophages that are infected, leading to caseating granuloma formation as a delayed hypersensitivity reaction.

Two types of TB granulomas represent different pathological states. A **proliferative TB granuloma** demonstrates effective killing of Mycobacterium due to proliferating lymphocytes, plasma cells, an outer rim of fibrosis, and a low bacillary load. In contrast, an **exudative TB granuloma** (also called caseating granuloma or fibrocaseous granuloma) exhibits a higher bacillary load, caseating necrosis, and progression of infection if not treated.

**Fibrocaseous TB** represents a pathological state in which hydrolytic enzymes dissolve MTB in larger lesions surrounded by a fibrous capsule. These fibrocaseous nodules contain viable mycobacterium and pose a lifelong risk for reactivation.

While granulomas are acidic, anaerobic, extracellular environments that inhibit MTB growth, they present a paradox: if they fail to contain the disease, they can cause significant tissue damage, leading to spread and further destruction. This balance between host immune containment and immunopathology is central to TB pathogenesis.

#### Clinical Presentation and Symptomatology

**Constitutional Symptoms:**

TB infection typically presents with constitutional symptoms including fever and night sweats, weight loss and failure to thrive, and fatigue and anorexia. Two or more of these TB symptom criteria are highly suggestive of TB diagnosis. The persistent cough criterion specifically identifies persistent or non-remitting cough or wheeze lasting more than 2 weeks that is unresponsive to appropriate antibiotics for community-acquired pneumonia. The weight loss criterion includes documented low weight or failure to thrive persisting for the past 3 months, especially if not responding to intervention.

**Hypersensitivity Phenomena:**

Four to twelve weeks after primary TB infection, clinical hypersensitivity phenomena may occur, including TST conversion, erythema nodosum (raised red, painful macules on the anterior lower legs), phlyctenular conjunctivitis (a raised red nodule on the limbus with conjunctival injection), and Poncet's polyarthritis.

**Pulmonary TB with Airway Involvement:**

When TB involves the airways, the clinical presentation includes atelectasis of the involved segment or lung, persistent cough, enlarged regional lymph nodes, and large airway (monophonic) wheezing that responds poorly to bronchodilators. This airway involvement distinguishes a specific clinical pattern requiring careful radiological assessment.

#### Tuberculin Skin Test (TST) and Immunological Testing

**TST Interpretation and Timing:**

The TST is read 48-72 hours after placement. A positive TST indicates TB infection (not necessarily TB disease) and measures hypersensitivity to tuberculin purified protein derivative (PPD).

**TST Cutoff Values by Risk Group:**

TST interpretation is highly context-dependent:
- **≥5 mm** in children <5 years old, HIV-infected children, or those with evidence of TB exposure
- **≥10 mm** in children ≥5 years of age without risk factors
- **≥15 mm** in non-BCG vaccinated children without TB risk factors

In children <5 years or HIV-infected children, a positive TST indicates recent infection and is a significant risk factor for progression to active TB disease.

**Causes of False Negative TST:**

Several conditions can result in false negative TST results: HIV infection, malnutrition, severe viral infections (measles, chickenpox), cancer, immunosuppressive drugs (steroids), severe disseminated TB, and recent TB exposure (where a 2-3 month conversion window period may result in negative tests despite recent infection).

**TST Interpretation in BCG-Vaccinated Populations:**

In endemic countries, a positive TST after BCG vaccination should be interpreted as indicative of TB infection, especially in very young and immunocompromised children. BCG vaccination can cause TST positivity, making this distinction critical.

**X-Ray Findings of Controlled TB:**

X-ray findings indicating controlled primary TB infection include a calcified scar in the lung parenchyma and/or hilar lymph nodes, representing the Ghon focus and complex.

#### BCG Vaccination Protocol

**BCG Vaccine Characteristics:**

BCG vaccine is a live attenuated vaccine derived from Mycobacterium bovis. It is given to all neonates at birth via intradermal injection, except to symptomatic HIV-exposed neonates.

**BCG Protection:**

In TB-endemic countries, BCG provides children with a degree of protection against disseminated and severe forms of TB, specifically TB meningitis and miliary TB. This protection is particularly important in early childhood when the risk of severe disease is highest.

**BCG in HIV-Exposed Infants:**

Asymptomatic HIV-exposed babies should receive routine BCG vaccination at birth, but they must be monitored closely for BCG-related adverse events. However, BCG should not be given to neonates exposed to TB while screening for active disease; maternal infectiousness and drug susceptibility status should be carried out first.

#### Danger Signs Requiring Hospital Referral

Children with TB presenting with certain danger signs require urgent hospital referral. These critical presentations include:
- Severe respiratory distress (TB pneumonia)
- Severe wheezing not responding to bronchodilators (severe airway compression)
- Signs or symptoms of meningism and seizures (TBM)
- Hepatosplenomegaly (disseminated TB)
- Breathlessness and peripheral edema (pericardial effusion)
- Severe pulmonary disease combined with malnutrition
- Distended abdomen with or without ascites (TB abdomen)
- Angulation of the spine or gibbous deformity (TB spine)

#### TB Diagnosis in HIV-Coinfected Children

TB diagnosis becomes significantly more complex in HIV-infected children. TB disease is often indistinguishable from other HIV-related lung diseases, chronic cough, weight loss, and persistent fever are common to both conditions, and TST is frequently negative. This diagnostic challenge necessitates a lower clinical threshold for TB treatment initiation.

**Differential Diagnoses of Pulmonary TB in HIV Children:**

Conditions that must be differentiated from TB in HIV-infected children include bacterial pneumonia, viral pneumonia, fungal lung disease, Pneumocystis jiroveci pneumonia (PJP), pulmonary lymphoma, Kaposi's sarcoma, and lymphoid interstitial pneumonia (LIP). **LIP** is distinguished by clubbing, parotid enlargement, and generalized lymphadenopathy.

#### Clinical Diagnosis of TB

**Diagnostic Approaches:**

Children can be clinically diagnosed with TB without bacteriological confirmation in several scenarios: chest X-ray abnormalities consistent with active TB, clinical picture alone (empiric treatment), histological and biochemical tests suggestive of TB, or a combination of the above due to the paucibacillary nature of TB in children.

**Radiological Findings:**

The most common radiological finding of TB in children is an enlarged hilar region; a lateral chest X-ray is particularly useful to detect hilar adenopathy and the characteristic "doughnut sign." Radiological findings indicating airway compression by enlarged lymph glands include the ball valve effect (segmental or lobar hyperinflation) or complete airway obstruction (lobar collapse).

**Miliary TB:**

Acute dissemination in miliary TB causes widespread fine millet-sized (1-2 mm) lesions with a characteristic "millet seed" appearance.

**Pleural Involvement:**

Pleural effusions may occur in children older than 6 years and appear on chest X-ray as unilateral or bilateral uniform white opacity with a concave upper border. Tracheal or mediastinal shift may occur away from the side of effusion.

**Normal Variants:**

Widened mediastinum secondary to thymus enlargement (the "sail sign") should not be misinterpreted as mediastinal widening due to TB lymph nodes.

#### Bacteriological Diagnosis and Sputum Samples

**Sputum Collection Challenges:**

Sputum diagnosis in children is challenging. In children <8 years, sputum is usually smear negative (paucibacillary), lung cavities are rare, and adequate sputum sample collection is difficult due to the child's inability to expectorate.

**Sputum Sampling Protocol:**

At least 2 sputum samples on 2 consecutive days should be collected, as the probability of a positive sputum increases with multiple samples.

**Extrapulmonary TB Specimens:**

For extrapulmonary TB, tissue specimens and site-specific fluids or secretions should be sent for Gene Xpert (GXP) testing and TB culture.

#### Diagnostic Tests: Urine LAM

**Urine LAM Detection:**

Urine LAM (lipoarabinomannan) testing detects LAM levels in urine. LAM is a heat-stable glycolipid of the outer cell wall of Mycobacterium species that is released by metabolically active bacilli and can be used to detect TB.

**Sensitivity and Specificity:**

Urine LAM sensitivity increases among children with severe immunosuppression, but specificity is lower than that of Gene Xpert. This test is particularly useful in severely immunocompromised children where traditional sputum samples may be difficult to obtain.

#### Contact-Tracing Strategies

**Contact Tracing Definition:**

Contact-tracing is the strategy by which individuals in contact with a case of confirmed or suspected pulmonary TB are screened for the presence of TB infection or disease, with the aim of offering either anti-tuberculosis preventive therapy or anti-tuberculosis chemotherapy. This systematic approach is fundamental to TB control.

**Reverse Contact Tracing:**

Reverse contact-tracing is adopted when a child is the index case with active TB, and the potential source case (usually an adult or older child in the index case's home environment) is sought by symptom screening and/or chest radiography of household contacts. This approach is critical for identifying the source and preventing further household transmission.

#### Extrapulmonary Tuberculosis (EPTB)

**Risk Groups and Timing:**

Children <2 years of age with an immature cellular immune response are at high risk for extrapulmonary TB. EPTB occurs in the first few months after TB infection, or it may develop years later (e.g., osteoarticular TB, renal TB) following reactivation of organisms that were subclinically disseminated during primary infection.

**TB Lymphadenitis:**

TB lymphadenitis presents as enlarged cervical lymph nodes that are typically painless (unless secondarily infected), firm, and matted. They may become fluctuant prior to spontaneous drainage and sinus formation, a condition historically termed "scrofula." TB lymphadenitis may involve sites other than the neck. Differential diagnoses for enlarged lymph nodes that can mimic TB lymphadenitis include persistent generalized lymphadenopathy (PGL), lymphoma, Kaposi sarcoma, sarcoidosis, and carcinomatous metastases.

**Serous Cavity Effusions:**

Inflammatory tuberculous effusions can occur in pleural, pericardial, and peritoneal cavities.

**TB Pleural Effusion:**

Pleural effusions typically occur in children older than 3 years, particularly in older children and adolescents. Clinical presentations include intermittent fever, unilateral chest pain, breathlessness, and rarely haemoptysis (which may occur in cavitatory disease). Physical examination reveals decreased chest movement and stony dullness on percussion. Chest X-ray findings demonstrate tracheal or mediastinal shift away from the side of effusion, and unilateral or bilateral uniform white opacity with a concave upper border.

Pleural aspirate in TB reveals an exudate with straw color, high protein content, high white cell count, and raised adenosine deaminase (ADA). Pleural biopsy can be performed for Xpert testing for further diagnostic confirmation.

**Osteoarticular TB:**

TB bone and joint disease occurs occasionally in infants but more commonly in older children. Clinical symptoms include painful limbs and joints, and limp. Notably, 50% of osteoarticular TB is spinal TB. Spinal TB (TB spine) presents with back pain lasting several weeks, and acute spinal cord compression (manifesting as lower limb weakness, bladder and bowel neurological signs) constitutes a medical emergency.

**TB Pericarditis:**

TB pericarditis presents with clinical signs of congestive heart failure (CCF) or pericardial constriction, raised jugular venous pressure (JVP), palpable pulsus paradoxus, and a pericardial friction rub.

**TB Abdominal Disease:**

Complications of TB abdominal disease include malnutrition with protein-losing enteropathy, abdominal distension (from ascites or bowel distension), peritonitis, and biliary or lymphatic obstruction due to massive compressive abdominal lymph nodes.

**Miliary TB:**

Miliary TB is a complication of primary TB in young children resulting from widespread blood-borne dissemination of TB bacilli. Physical signs include lymphadenopathy, hepatosplenomegaly, fever, tachypnea, cyanosis, respiratory distress, and other signs including papular lesions on skin or choroidal tubercles in the retina. The chest X-ray appearance shows diffuse, uniformly distributed small miliary shadows with a "millet seed" appearance.

Blood investigations reveal pancytopenia on full blood count, abnormal liver function tests, and histological biopsy shows tubercles. MTB can be isolated from cerebrospinal fluid (CSF), bone marrow, and sputum in miliary TB.

**TB Meningitis:**

TB meningitis (TBM) presents with a typical history of TB contact, lethargy, headache (especially with early morning vomiting), irritability, drowsiness, convulsions, and weight loss. Physical signs include neck pain and resistance to neck flexion due to meningeal irritation (Kernig's sign), cranial nerve palsies, and altered level of consciousness.

Cerebrospinal fluid findings in TBM show raised protein, low glucose, low chloride, and predominantly lymphocytic pleocytosis. Gram stain is negative and acid-fast bacilli (AFB) are seldom found despite the TB diagnosis. Xpert (Gene Xpert) can be performed on CSF for MTB detection and rifampicin susceptibility testing.

Important diagnostic considerations: TST can be negative in TBM, and chest X-ray may be normal in children with TB meningitis. TB meningitis should always be considered in children diagnosed with meningitis who are not responding to standard bacterial meningitis treatment. Complications of TBM include hydrocephalus (obstruction to CSF flow), SIADH (syndrome of inappropriate antidiuretic hormone), hemi- or quadriplegia, convulsions, deafness, blindness, and neurodevelopmental delay.

#### Congenital and Vertical Transmission of TB

**Modes of Transmission:**

TB can be transmitted from mother to baby through three mechanisms: transplacental spread through the umbilical vein to the fetal liver (in utero), aspiration or ingestion of infected amniotic fluid (in utero/intrapartum), and inhalation of droplet nuclei from close contacts (mother or other family members) postpartum.

**Risk Factors for Vertical Transmission:**

Risk factors for vertical TB transmission include HIV infection in the mother, active TB in the mother, lack of prenatal care, non-adherence to anti-TB treatment, the infectiousness of the mother, and mother having multi-drug resistant TB.

**Progression Risk:**

Without TB preventive therapy or BCG vaccination, 50% of babies born to mothers with active TB disease will develop TB disease in the first year of life.

**Breastfeeding:**

Importantly, breast milk does not transmit TB. Breastfeeding is recommended to infants irrespective of the TB status of the mother, as the risk of transmission through breast milk is negligible.

**Clinical Presentation of Congenital TB:**

Congenital TB presents with low birth weight (LBW), poor feeding, poor weight gain, hepatosplenomegaly (HSM), abdominal distension, rashes, lethargy, and respiratory symptoms. TB infection is determined by TST. Active congenital TB disease can become apparent at 2-4 weeks of age.

**Investigation and Diagnosis:**

Specimens that should be collected include gastric washings for Xpert, microscopy and culture, and placenta for histopathology. TB treatment should be commenced while awaiting bacteriological confirmation as the disease progresses rapidly.

**Treatment and Management:**

The TB treatment regimen for congenital TB is the same as for all children <8 years (Regimen 3A). Response to treatment is indicated by increased appetite, weight gain, and radiological resolution.

**BCG Vaccination in TB-Exposed Neonates:**

BCG should not be given to neonates exposed to TB while screening for active disease. Maternal infectiousness and drug susceptibility status should be carried out and documented first.

#### TB Prevention and Preventive Therapy (TPT)

**TB Preventive Therapy Regimens:**

Four main TPT regimens are used:
- **3HP**: Three months of weekly isoniazid plus rifapentine
- **3RH**: Three months of daily rifampicin plus isoniazid
- **6H**: Six months of daily isoniazid monotherapy
- **12H**: 12 months of daily isoniazid monotherapy

All of these regimens are indicated for TB contacts, TB-exposed infants, and children with latent TB infection to prevent progression to active TB disease.

**TPT Initiation Timing:**

TPT should be started after active TB disease has been excluded through clinical assessment, symptom screening, and radiography in TB contacts.

#### TB Management Framework

**Pre-Treatment Investigations:**

Before starting TB treatment, the following investigations must be done:
- Exclude HIV infection
- Assess all co-infected children for ART (antiretroviral therapy) eligibility
- Nutritional assessment
- Psychosocial evaluation
- Chest X-ray
- TB Register completion

**Supportive Care:**

Psychosocial support should be provided to both the child and the parents or guardians. Referral for nutritional support should be considered in all children diagnosed with TB. Adequate nutrition is essential as TB is associated with malnutrition, which impairs immune function and treatment response. Adequate nutrition promotes weight gain and recovery.

**Administrative Steps:**

When a child is diagnosed with TB, complete the TB Register and make a note in the Road to Health Card. Additionally, ask about other children or adults in the household and screen them for TB as part of contact investigation.

**Management Categories:**

Four categories of paediatric TB management are recognized: (A) Uncomplicated pulmonary TB, (B) Complicated pulmonary TB, (C) Disseminated (miliary) TB, and (D) Tuberculous meningitis (TBM).

**Guideline References:**

Updated guidance for TB management in children comes from the Primary Healthcare STGs and EML 2020 edition (Section 17.4: Pulmonary tuberculosis) and the Paediatric Hospital Level STGs and EML 2017 edition (Chapter 10: Tuberculosis), as well as WHO 2014 guidance for national TB programmes, the National Guidelines on Treatment of TB infection 2023, and Southern African society for pediatric infectious diseases guidelines.

#### TB Treatment Regimens and Drug Selection

**Major TB Drugs:**

The major TB drugs used in paediatric regimens are:
- **Isoniazid (H)**
- **Rifampicin (R)**
- **Rifapentine**
- **Pyrazinamide (Z)**
- **Ethambutol (E)**

**Regimen 3A:**

Regimen 3A is used for children <8 years with uncomplicated and congenital TB disease and specifies exact dosages and durations per national guidelines. Children weighing >25 kg (typically 8-10 years) can be treated with adult doses of TB medications, marking a transition to standard adult dosing.

#### TB Treatment Phases and Duration

**Intensive Phase:**

The intensive phase comprises the first 2 months of anti-TB drugs with maximum bactericidal activity to eliminate the majority of viable TB organisms.

**Continuation Phase:**

The continuation phase represents the remaining treatment duration to prevent relapse and eliminate remaining bacilli. The typical duration of the continuation phase is 2-4 months, depending on TB type and response.

**Treatment Duration by Disease Type:**

- **Uncomplicated Pulmonary TB**: Typically 6 months total (2 months intensive + 4 months continuation)
- **Complicated Pulmonary TB**: 8 months total (2 months intensive + 6 months continuation)
- **Miliary TB**: 8 months treatment, with potential for a 2-month initial phase with 4 drugs depending on severity
- **TB Meningitis**: 9-12 months due to poor drug penetration into cerebrospinal fluid and the severity of disease

**Corticosteroids in TB Meningitis:**

Corticosteroids (typically dexamethasone or prednisone) are used as adjunctive therapy in TB meningitis to reduce inflammation and prevent complications.

#### Monitoring During TB Treatment

**Baseline and Ongoing Investigations:**

Baseline investigations before treatment include HIV testing, assessment for ART eligibility in co-infected children, nutritional assessment, psychosocial evaluation, chest X-ray, and TB Register completion.

**Adverse Effects Monitoring:**

During TB treatment, monitor the following parameters:
- Liver function tests (for hepatotoxicity)
- Peripheral neuropathy (from isoniazid toxicity)
- Visual acuity if on ethambutol (for optic neuritis)
- Ototoxicity if on streptomycin
- Hypersensitivity reactions

**Adherence Assessment:**

Treatment adherence is assessed through regular clinic visits, pill counts, clinical response monitoring (appetite, weight gain, symptom resolution), and radiological improvement on follow-up chest X-ray.

#### Treatment Outcomes

**Cured:**

Cured status is defined as completing treatment with evidence of clinical and radiological improvement, with no relapse for at least 12 months after treatment completion.

**Treatment Failure:**

Treatment failure is defined as lack of clinical improvement or radiological deterioration despite adequate treatment and adherence for at least 4 months.

**Default:**

Default is defined as loss to follow-up or interruption of treatment for more than 2 consecutive months after the start of treatment.

#### Treatment Failure Management

Management of treatment failures requires a systematic approach:
1. Assess adherence to the regimen
2. Investigate for drug malabsorption
3. Re-evaluate the TB diagnosis
4. Perform drug susceptibility testing
5. Consider the possibility of resistance and modify the regimen accordingly

#### Vitamin Supplementation

**Pyridoxine (Vitamin B6):**

Pyridoxine (vitamin B6) should be given to all children on TB treatment and/or antiretroviral treatment due to shared toxicities of these regimens. Vitamin B6 is specifically recommended to prevent peripheral neuropathy from isoniazid toxicity.

#### Infection Control and Transmission

**Impact of Effective TB Treatment:**

Appropriate TB treatment renders a child non-infectious within 2 weeks of starting effective therapy, significantly reducing transmission risk.

**Infection Control Measures:**
