The following infection control measures should be implemented during TB treatment:
- Respiratory isolation for symptomatic patients (first 2 weeks of treatment)
- Good ventilation in patient areas
- Use of masks for close contacts
- Strict adherence to treatment to achieve rapid reduction in infectivity

#### HIV-TB Coinfection Management

**TB Development in ART-Treated Children:**

Development of TB in a child on antiretroviral therapy (ART) could indicate:
- Immune reconstitution (if within the first 6 months of ART initiation)
- Failure of the ART regimen
- New TB infection depending on exposure history

**TB Management While on ART:**

When TB is diagnosed in a child already on ART, TB treatment should be started without delay and ART should be continued. The ART regimen should be reviewed and may need to be changed based on TB drug interactions.

**Supplementation in TB-ART Coinfection:**

Pyridoxine (vitamin B6) should be given to all children on both TB and ARV treatment due to shared toxicities of the regimens.

**Drug Interactions Between TB and ART:**

Rifampicin induces hepatic enzymes, reducing ART levels significantly. Lopinavir/ritonavir levels are particularly affected, requiring dose adjustments. Isoniazid may accumulate with certain protease inhibitors (PIs).

**Ritonavir Dosing Adjustment:**

For each millilitre of lopinavir/ritonavir 80/20 mg/mL solution, add 0.75 mL of ritonavir 80 mg/mL solution. This additional ritonavir should be added at a dose of 0.75 times the volume of the lopinavir/ritonavir dose to compensate for enzyme induction by rifampicin.

#### Immune Reconstitution Inflammatory Syndrome (IRIS)

**IRIS Definition:**

IRIS is observed in patients on TB treatment who start antiretroviral therapy (ART), characterized by worsening of disease after initial clinical improvement. This phenomenon reflects the immune system's recovery and mounting response to TB antigens.

**Timing and Duration:**

IRIS may occur during the first three months of ART initiation. It is generally self-limiting and typically lasts 10-40 days.

**Clinical Manifestations:**

Clinical manifestations of IRIS in TB patients include worsening disease after initial improvement, fever, increased size of lymph nodes, tuberculomas, and exacerbation of symptoms, signs, or X-ray manifestations.

**Management of IRIS:**

TB treatment and ART should be continued when IRIS is suspected. In some cases, the addition of corticosteroids might be useful to manage the inflammatory response. Patients already on ART may experience temporary exacerbations of symptoms, signs, or X-ray manifestations after beginning TB therapy, which should be differentiated from true treatment failure.

#### Contact Screening Components

**TB Contact Screening:**

TB contact screening includes several key components:
- Clinical assessment for TB symptoms
- Tuberculin skin test (TST) or interferon-gamma release assay (IGRA)
- Chest X-ray if symptomatic
- TB preventive therapy for eligible contacts

This comprehensive approach ensures all exposed individuals are appropriately managed.

---

## Summary of Key Points

#### Critical Epidemiological Facts
- WHO End TB Strategy targets 90% reduction in TB deaths and 80% reduction in incidence by 2035
- TB incidence in children is approximately half that in adults
- 50% of infants progress to active TB after primary infection
- 10-20% of infants develop complicated TB (miliary or meningitis)

#### Essential TST Cutoff Values
- ≥5 mm in children <5 years, HIV-infected, or with TB exposure
- ≥10 mm in children ≥5 years without risk factors
- ≥15 mm in non-BCG vaccinated without TB risk

#### TPT Regimens at a Glance
- **3HP**: 3 months weekly (isoniazid + rifapentine)
- **3RH**: 3 months daily (rifampicin + isoniazid)
- **6H**: 6 months daily (isoniazid alone)
- **12H**: 12 months daily (isoniazid alone)

#### TB Treatment Duration by Type
- Uncomplicated PTB: 6 months (2 intensive + 4 continuation)
- Complicated PTB: 8 months (2 intensive + 6 continuation)
- Miliary TB: 8 months (with possible 4-drug induction)
- TB Meningitis: 9-12 months (poor CSF penetration)

#### EPTB Sites Requiring Special Attention
- Lymphadenitis (cervical and other sites)
- Pleural effusion (>3 years age)
- TB spine (spinal cord compression emergency)
- TB meningitis (always consider non-responders)
- Miliary TB (widespread dissemination)
- Pericarditis (CCF/constriction)
- Abdominal TB (ascites, obstruction)

#### Key Management Principles
- HIV testing before treatment initiation
- Nutritional support essential
- Psychosocial support for family
- Vitamin B6 for all TB-treated children
- Infection control first 2 weeks critical
- TST can be falsely negative (HIV, malnutrition, disseminated TB)
- Breast milk safe; breastfeeding recommended
- BCG at birth except symptomatic HIV+ neonates

-

## 2025 CIRCULATION

### Lecture Notes


#### Understanding Shock in Paediatric Patients

Shock represents a critical emergency state defined by impaired tissue perfusion and inadequate oxygen delivery. In paediatric patients, shock is identified through three cardinal clinical signs that guide rapid assessment and intervention. These signs are the cornerstone of circulation assessment and must be recognised immediately. Cold hands indicate peripheral vasoconstriction and reduced perfusion. Capillary refill time (CRT) of ≥3 seconds demonstrates sluggish circulation, measured by blanching the skin and counting the time for colour return. A weak and fast pulse reflects the body's compensatory response to low blood pressure. When all three signs are present together, the child is in shock and requires emergency intervention. When only one or two of these signs are present, the child is classified as having impaired circulation and requires priority attention but may allow slightly more time for careful evaluation.

The causes of shock in paediatric settings are diverse but follow predictable physiological patterns. Diarrhoea remains the most common cause globally, leading to acute fluid loss from the circulation. Blood loss from trauma or active bleeding can rapidly deplete circulating volume. Capillary leak—where fluid escapes from the vasculature into the interstitial space—occurs in sepsis and severe malnutrition. Septic shock results from infection-triggered inflammation that compromises cardiac output and vascular tone. Understanding the underlying cause helps guide management, though the initial resuscitation approach remains consistent across all shock types.

#### Initial Management of Shock

Upon recognition of shock, the first priority is to stop any ongoing bleeding by applying direct pressure, elevating affected limbs, or arranging urgent haemostasis. Oxygen delivery must be maximised immediately, typically through high-flow oxygen by mask or bag-and-mask ventilation if the child is in extremis. Warmth is critical because hypothermia impairs cardiac function and metabolic processes; remove wet clothing and apply blankets or heat lamps. Obtain intravenous or intraosseous (IO) access urgently—this is not a moment to attempt multiple peripheral veins when IO access is available. At the time of access establishment, take blood for emergency laboratory investigations; the mnemonic DEFG reminds clinicians to check specific parameters during bolus administration. Consider antibiotics immediately if septic shock is suspected, with ceftriaxone being the empiric agent of choice.

#### Fluid Resuscitation: Sequential Bolus Protocols

The primary therapeutic intervention for shock is fluid bolus resuscitation, which restores circulating volume and re-establishes perfusion. The bolus volume and infusion rate depend critically on whether the child has severe acute malnutrition, which fundamentally alters cardiac and renal physiology.

For children without severe acute malnutrition, fluid resuscitation follows an aggressive protocol. The first bolus consists of 10 ml/kg administered rapidly over 15 minutes using balanced crystalloid solutions—either normal saline or Ringers lactate. After this bolus is completed, reassess the child. If shock signs persist, administer a second rapid bolus of 10 ml/kg over 15 minutes. Reassess again. If shock remains unresolved, give a third rapid bolus of 10 ml/kg over 15 minutes. At this point, beware—three boluses of 10 ml/kg each represent approximately 30 percent of total body weight, which approximates to about half the child's circulating volume. Continuing beyond this point without escalation to advanced support carries substantial risk of fluid overload and organ dysfunction.

For children with severe acute malnutrition, the pathophysiology is fundamentally different. These children have profound depletion of lean body mass, including cardiac and skeletal muscle. They have reduced cardiac reserve and impaired renal function that limits their ability to handle rapid fluid shifts. The first bolus is 10 ml/kg of normal saline or Ringers lactate, but infused cautiously over 30 minutes rather than 15. If shock persists after reassessment, give a second bolus of 10 ml/kg over 30 minutes. If shock still remains, the third bolus is 10 ml/kg over 60 minutes—double the time of the second bolus. This stepped increase in infusion time reflects the progressive risk of fluid overload in already-compromised physiology. The choice of fluids—normal saline or Ringers lactate—remains the same, as these balanced crystalloids are appropriate for resuscitation in both groups.

#### Reassessment During Fluid Therapy

Reassessment is not optional; it is mandatory and must occur frequently. After each bolus is completed, check for resolution of shock signs. Look for warm hands, capillary refill return to ≤2 seconds, and a stronger, more regular pulse. Simultaneously, assess for emerging signs of heart failure, which signals fluid overload. These signs include an increasing pulse rate (tachycardia that worsens despite fluid), rapid respirations (tachypnoea), hepatomegaly (enlarged liver edge palpable more than 2 centimeters below the costal margin), gallop rhythm (an audible triple rhythm on auscultation), and crepitations at the lung bases (fine crackling sounds suggesting pulmonary oedema). Also note the child's consciousness level—improved alertness and responsiveness are positive signs, while persistent lethargy suggests ongoing shock. Do not leave the shocked child unattended while fluids are infusing.

Reassessment may reveal one of three outcomes. The child's condition improves, with resolution of shock signs and no signs of heart failure. In this case, immediately stop the bolus administration and transition to maintenance fluids. The child shows no improvement, remaining shocked despite completed boluses. In this situation, proceed to the next bolus in the sequence. The child develops signs of heart failure during bolus administration—this is the critical complication. Stop the bolus immediately; do not give additional crystalloid.

#### Management After Shock Resolution or Heart Failure

When shock improves after fluid bolus administration, maintenance fluid therapy replaces ongoing losses and provides basal requirements. If the child has no clinical evidence of dehydration, use half-daily dextrose or dextrose-saline (½ DD) at 4 ml/kg/hr. This conservative rate avoids excessive fluid in a child who was recently in shock. If the child has clinical evidence of dehydration in addition to shock, calculate the rehydration requirement as percentage deficit multiplied by 10 multiplied by body weight in kilograms, then add this to the daily maintenance requirement. Change the child to oral feeds or breastfeeding as soon as shock has resolved and the child is able to tolerate enteral intake.

If heart failure develops during fluid resuscitation, the immediate action is to stop all bolus fluids. Reduce the infusion rate dramatically to ½ DD at 4 ml/hr—roughly 10 times slower than bolus administration. Seek help from senior clinicians or specialists immediately. Simultaneously, give glucose and antibiotics. Consider more advanced respiratory and cardiovascular support: intubation to secure the airway and reduce work of breathing, mechanical ventilation to rest the respiratory muscles and allow better oxygenation, and inotropes to support cardiac output when fluid alone cannot restore perfusion.

#### The Third Bolus Threshold and Escalation

The third bolus represents a critical threshold beyond which standard shock management must evolve. After administering three boluses of 10 ml/kg each, the child has received 30 ml/kg total, representing approximately half the circulating volume. If the child is still shocked at this point, it indicates either refractory shock (shock not responding to standard resuscitation) or ongoing losses. Continuing with additional boluses without escalating care is inappropriate. Instead, seek help from senior clinicians or specialists. Give glucose and antibiotics as part of comprehensive shock management. Consider intubation for airway protection and ventilation for respiratory support. Initiate inotrope infusions to augment cardiac output chemically when fluid administration alone is insufficient.

#### Glucose Management and Metabolic Complications

Shocked children are at high risk of severe hypoglycaemia, which worsens outcomes and must be identified and treated during the bolus infusion. Check capillary or laboratory glucose at the time of IV or IO access while the first bolus is being given. If glucose is below 3 mmol/L, administer 5 ml/kg of 10 percent dextrose intravenously. This should be given as a bolus, not as a slow infusion, to restore blood glucose rapidly. Re-check glucose after treatment and repeat the dextrose dose if hypoglycaemia persists.

#### Blood Transfusion in Shock

Blood transfusion is indicated in shocked children when anaemia is severe enough to compromise oxygen-carrying capacity further. Transfuse fresh blood when haemoglobin is below 5 g/dL. Fresh blood (not older stored blood) is preferred in shock because it has better functional properties and avoids the complications of stored blood. This typically occurs as part of sequential resuscitation in children with blood loss and profound anaemia.

#### Intraosseous Line: Definition, Anatomy, and Technique

An intraosseous (IO) line is a sturdy needle placed through cortical bone into the medullary cavity, allowing emergency infusion of life-saving fluids and medications. It is indicated in very sick children, particularly those in shock with difficult or impossible vascular access in emergency situations. When peripheral and central venous access are compromised, IO access may be lifesaving by enabling immediate delivery of resuscitation fluids and medications that cannot wait for line placement.

The preferred anatomical site is the tibia 1 cm below and medial to the tibial tuberosity (the bony prominence below the knee). This location is easy to identify, has thin cortical bone, and is distant from important neurovascular structures and the epiphysis. An 18-gauge pink short lumbar puncture (LP) needle is the standard equipment. The technique requires meticulous sterile preparation. Clean the insertion site with iodine solution. Stabilise the proximal tibia firmly with one hand to prevent movement. Insert the needle at a 90-degree angle (perpendicular to the bone surface) and use a twisting and drilling motion to penetrate the cortex. You will feel a distinct 'give' as the needle passes through cortical bone into the medullary cavity—this is the key sensation confirming penetration. Obtain flashback of marrow (usually blood-stained fluid) into the needle hub, then aspirate gently to confirm placement. Secure the needle firmly and immobilise the extremity to prevent dislodgement.

#### Contraindications to Intraosseous Insertion

Several conditions contraindicate IO insertion at a planned site. Do not insert an IO line if there is an acute or recent fracture of the target bone, as the needle will not achieve secure placement and may damage bone fragments. Previous significant orthopaedic procedures at the site, such as an intramedullary nail or plate fixation, contraindicate IO insertion. Overlying skin infection at the insertion site is an absolute contraindication because the needle will track the infection into bone. Previous IO insertion at the same site, even if successful previously, is contraindicated to avoid multiple bone penetrations. Conditions causing abnormal bone quality, such as osteoporosis or osteogenesis imperfecta, are relative contraindications because the needle may traverse the bone entirely without secure placement.

#### Acute Complications of Intraosseous Insertion

Acute complications can occur at the time of insertion or immediately afterward. Failure to enter the bone marrow occurs when the needle does not penetrate far enough or deviates through cortex. Infused fluid then extravasates (leaks) into surrounding soft tissues, creating subcutaneous swelling and potentially necrosis. Through-and-through penetration means the needle passes completely through both cortices of the bone, emerging on the other side; this prevents reliable infusion and risks injury to posterior structures. Bleeding into the medullary cavity or from perforation of marrow vessels can cause compartment syndrome if sufficient blood accumulates in the fascial compartment. Skin sloughing can occur if the needle is inserted at too large an angle to the skin surface, causing ischaemia of the overlying dermis.

#### Delayed Complications of Intraosseous Insertion

Delayed complications develop hours to days after IO insertion. Osteomyelitis (bone marrow infection) is a serious but relatively rare complication, occurring in less than 2-3 percent of cases and usually only if the line is left in place for extended periods or if sepsis was already present. Physeal (growth plate) injury can occur if the needle passes through or damages the growth plate, potentially causing growth disturbance or limb length discrepancy, though modern IO techniques below the tibial tuberosity usually avoid the physis. Local infection at the insertion site can develop if sterile technique was breached. Compartment syndrome can develop from bleeding or swelling in the closed fascial space. Fat embolism, where marrow fat and bone particles enter the venous circulation, is rare but can occur and cause respiratory or cerebral complications.

#### Confirmation of Successful IO Placement

Successful intraosseous placement is confirmed by several findings. You will feel the distinct 'give' as you push through cortex—this tactile sensation is reliable. Obtain flashback of marrow into the needle hub (often appearing as blood-tinged fluid). Confirm your ability to aspirate fluid or blood from the medullary cavity—resistance to aspiration suggests failure of placement. All three findings together indicate successful placement; if any is absent, placement has failed and the needle should be repositioned or removed.

#### Special Anatomical Considerations

The physeal (growth) plate lies between the epiphysis and metaphysis and is vulnerable to damage. The cortical bone must be penetrated completely to access the medullary cavity, but penetration through both cortices is a complication. The medullary cavity contains abundant haematopoietic marrow and fat, which are the target tissues for the needle's final position. Growth disturbances from physeal injury are potential long-term consequences of unsuccessful or traumatic IO insertion.

#### Malnutrition as a Critical Modifier of Shock Management

Severe acute malnutrition profoundly alters the physiology of shock and resuscitation. Malnourished children have depleted cardiac muscle mass and reduced myocardial contractility. Their kidneys have diminished function and cannot excrete excess fluid appropriately. As a result, rapid fluid administration risks pulmonary oedema, hepatomegaly, and acute cardiac decompensation. The principle is to give the same total volume (10 ml/kg per bolus) but over extended time periods. This allows gradual restoration of perfusion without overwhelming cardiac and renal function. The time differences are critical: non-malnourished children receive boluses over 15 minutes, but malnourished children receive the first and second boluses over 30 minutes and the third over 60 minutes. The difference between these groups highlights how malnutrition status is not merely an underlying condition but an active modifier of resuscitation tactics.

#### Monitoring Parameters During Fluid Therapy

Ongoing monitoring during bolus administration tracks progress and early warning signs. Pulse rate should decrease and become stronger as perfusion improves, though it may initially rise if the child is compensating for hypovolaemia. Respiratory rate should decrease as tissue hypoxia resolves. Watch continuously for signs of congestive cardiac failure: the appearance of hepatomegaly, development of crackles on lung auscultation, or emergence of a gallop rhythm indicate fluid overload. Consciousness level should improve as the brain receives better perfusion. All children receiving fluid boluses must be observed continuously—do not leave them unattended.

#### Practical Approach to Reassessment

At each reassessment point, use a systematic approach. Check for resolution of the three shock signs: are the hands now warm, is capillary refill ≤2 seconds, is the pulse stronger and more regular? Check for signs of heart failure that would demand immediate cessation of bolus therapy: increasing pulse rate unrelated to fever or anxiety, rapid respirations worsening despite oxygenation, newly palpable hepatomegaly, or new crackles on auscultation. Assess consciousness, which should improve with restoration of cerebral perfusion. This systematic reassessment after each bolus determines the next step: continue to maintenance fluids if shock resolves, give another bolus if shock persists, or stop fluids immediately if heart failure emerges.

-

## 2025 HIV STAGING, TREATMENT AND PMTC

### Lecture Notes


#### WHO Classification and CD4 Staging in Paediatric HIV

The World Health Organization employs a 4-stage classification system to categorize paediatric HIV disease. This system classifies clinical status along a spectrum from asymptomatic to severely symptomatic presentation, based on documented clinical or immunological criteria in the presence of lab-confirmed HIV infection. Understanding this classification is essential for risk stratification and treatment planning in children with HIV.

A critical distinction between paediatric and adult HIV management lies in CD4 cell interpretation. CD4 counts in healthy uninfected infants are considerably higher than those observed in uninfected adults, and these values slowly decline to adult levels by approximately age 6 years. This age-related variation means that absolute CD4 count must be interpreted within age-specific context—a CD4 count of 1000 cells/mm³ in a 6-month-old infant represents severe immunosuppression, whereas the same count in a 7-year-old indicates relative preservation of immune function.

In children younger than 5 years, percentage CD4 (%CD4+) proves more valuable and reliable than absolute CD4 count, since absolute values fluctuate considerably within individual children in this young age group. This is particularly important because viral load thresholds and immunological criteria for treatment decisions often rely on these parameters. The CD4 threshold for AIDS diagnosis across all ages is less than 200 cells/mm³, and this criterion defines severe immunosuppression warranting urgent intervention.

#### Natural History and Stages of HIV Infection

Acute HIV infection develops within 2 to 4 weeks after a person becomes infected. During this window, many patients experience flu-like symptoms including fever, headache, and rash. The virus directly attacks and destroys CD4 cells of the immune system. Critically, HIV transmission risk is greatest during acute HIV infection, although transmission can occur during any stage of infection.

Following the acute phase, HIV progresses to chronic infection, also termed asymptomatic HIV infection or clinical latency. During chronic HIV infection, the virus continues to replicate in the body but at very low levels. People may have no HIV-related symptoms yet can still transmit HIV to others. Without treatment with HIV medicines, chronic HIV infection usually advances to AIDS in 10 to 12 years.

AIDS represents the final stage of HIV disease. AIDS is diagnosed when a person with HIV has a CD4 count of less than 200 cells/mm³ and/or one or more opportunistic infections. At this stage, HIV has destroyed the immune system such that the body cannot adequately fight off opportunistic infections and malignancy. Without treatment, people with AIDS typically survive about 3 years.

#### Early Clinical Manifestations in Paediatric HIV

The clinical presentation of HIV in children varies dramatically by age and degree of immunosuppression. In the first year of life, common early signs include oral candidiasis, failure to thrive (FTT), and developmental delay. Lymphadenopathy frequently occurs alongside hepatosplenomegaly, reflecting systemic viral replication and immune activation.

Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia (PJP) holds particular significance in paediatric HIV, accounting for approximately 50% of all AIDS-defining conditions in the first year of life, with a median age of presentation at 5 months. Clinically, PJP presents with low-grade fever, tachypnea, non-productive cough, and shortness of breath. A characteristic and diagnostically challenging feature is that auscultation on physical examination may be entirely normal despite severe disease and significant hypoxemia. Chest X-ray imaging may also appear normal; however, CT imaging can reveal extensive ground-glass opacities with patchy distribution affecting the central lung while sparing the periphery.

Wasting syndrome in HIV is defined as persistent weight loss greater than 10% of baseline, OR a downward crossing of 2 or more major percentile lines on the paediatric weight-for-age chart, OR weight-for-height less than the 5th percentile on two consecutive measurements taken at least 30 days apart, accompanied by chronic diarrhea or documented fever. Wasting can result from HIV infection itself, concurrent opportunistic infections, and increased metabolic demands, leading to loss of both total body mass and lean muscle.

HIV encephalopathy represents severe neurological involvement and is diagnosed by the constellation of: failure to attain developmental milestones or loss of cognitive ability, AND evidence of impaired brain growth with acquired microcephaly, brain atrophy on CT or MRI, AND acquired symmetric motor deficits (specifically, two or more of the following: paresis, pathologic reflexes, ataxia, or gait disturbances).

Cytomegalovirus (CMV) disease in advanced HIV can present in multiple forms including fever, pneumonia, colitis, encephalitis, or retinitis. CMV transmission occurs from mother to child perinatally, through breastfeeding, and during pregnancy, as well as through person-to-person contact with secretions.

#### Epidemiology and Burden of Paediatric HIV Disease

The epidemiological impact of paediatric HIV remains substantial, particularly in sub-Saharan Africa. In 2014, the global HIV burden was estimated at 36.9 million people living with HIV (range 34.3 to 41.4 million), with 220,000 children under 15 years (range 190,000 to 260,000) living with HIV. That year saw 1.2 million AIDS deaths globally (range 980,000 to 1.6 million), including 150,000 deaths among children under 15 years (range 140,000 to 170,000).

Geographically, approximately 75% of new HIV infections in children in 2014 occurred in Sub-Saharan Africa. Similarly, approximately 70% of all children under 15 years living with HIV globally are found in Sub-Saharan Africa, reflecting both the high prevalence in the region and the profound impact on childhood mortality and morbidity.

Within South Africa specifically, approximately 12% of the national population is HIV infected. However, prevalence is highly heterogeneous by region. KwaZulu-Natal (KZN) has the highest HIV prevalence rate among persons age 15 to 49 in South Africa at approximately 35 to 40%.

The impact of antiretroviral therapy on opportunistic infection rates has been transformative. In a study of 364 HIV-infected children born between 1986 and 1998 and monitored until 2004, the incidence rate of opportunistic infections decreased dramatically from 14.4 cases per 100 person-years before 1997 to 1.1 cases per 100 person-years after 1997, demonstrating the profound effect of combination antiretroviral therapy introduction.

#### Current Treatment Policy and ART Initiation Criteria

Current policy mandates that all children infected with HIV should receive antiretroviral therapy (ARVs), with no CD4-based thresholds for treatment eligibility. Two developments have had the greatest impact on improving paediatric HIV outcomes: the availability and use of highly effective combination antiretroviral therapy (ART), and the early initiation of ART in HIV-infected infants.

Fast-track ART initiation (defined as starting treatment within 7 days) is indicated for children meeting any of the following criteria: age less than 1 year, WHO clinical stage 4, active multidrug-resistant or extensively drug-resistant tuberculosis (MDR or XDR-TB), or CD4 count less than 200 cells/μL or less than 15% CD4.

#### Baseline Assessment and Monitoring at ART Initiation

Before starting antiretroviral therapy, specific baseline assessments are required to identify contraindications and establish baseline parameters for monitoring. These include CD4 count (if none has been performed within the previous 6 months), hemoglobin or full blood count (with the understanding that ART can be started if hemoglobin is less than 8 g/dL, though this should be discussed with a specialist regarding concurrent management), cholesterol and triglyceride levels (particularly if the planned regimen includes protease inhibitors), ALT testing (if the child is jaundiced or concurrently on tuberculosis treatment), and renal function assessment for children weighing more than 35 kg who will be receiving tenofovir.

#### Current First-Line Antiretroviral Regimens by Age

The first-line antiretroviral regimens have evolved to maximize efficacy while minimizing toxicity in different age groups. For neonates, the new first-line regimen is AZT (zidovudine) plus 3TC (lamivudine) plus NVP (nevirapine). For infants and children in the weight range of 3 to 20 kg, the new first-line regimen is ABC (abacavir) plus 3TC (lamivudine) plus DTG (dolutegravir). For adolescents and adults, the first-line regimen is TDF (tenofovir) plus 3TC (lamivudine) plus DTG (dolutegravir).

Paediatric dolutegravir is available as 10 mg dispersable scored tablets for standard first-line use in children older than 4 weeks to less than 10 years of age in the weight band of 3 to 20 kg. The benefits of this paediatric formulation include increased clinical efficacy compared to older regimens, a better side effect profile, ease of administration, and improved palatability, facilitating adherence in young children.

#### Antiretroviral Drug Classes and Pharmacology

Seven major classes of antiretroviral medications are currently available. Nucleoside reverse transcriptase inhibitors (NRTI) include abacavir (ABC), didanosine (ddI), emtricitabine (FTC), lamivudine (3TC), stavudine (d4T), tenofovir (TDF), and zidovudine (AZT or ZDV). Non-nucleoside reverse transcriptase inhibitors (NNRTI) include delavirdine (DLV), efavirenz (EFV), etravirine (EVR), nevirapine (NVP), and rilpivirine. Protease inhibitors comprise atazanavir (ATV), darunavir (DRV), fosamprenavir (FPV), indinavir (IDV), lopinavir (LPV), nelfinavir (NFV), ritonavir (RTV), saquinavir (SQV), and tipranavir (TPV). Integrase inhibitors include dolutegravir (DTG), elvitegravir (EVG), and raltegravir (RAL). Additionally, enfuvirtide (ENF or T-20) serves as a fusion inhibitor, and maraviroc (MVC) acts as a CCR5 antagonist.

#### Dolutegravir: Profile and Monitoring

Dolutegravir represents a cornerstone of modern paediatric HIV treatment. Side effects are typically mild and self-limiting, including insomnia, headache, various CNS effects, and gastrointestinal symptoms. Weight gain has emerged as a notable side effect in some patients. A unique pharmacological property is that DTG decreases tubular secretion of creatinine without affecting glomerular filtration rate. As a consequence, serum creatinine levels increase early in treatment by less than 15%, remain stable thereafter, and this rise alone is not an indication to discontinue DTG unless creatinine continues to rise, which could indicate concurrent tenofovir nephrotoxicity or other renal pathology.

An important consideration for women of childbearing age is that dolutegravir may increase the risk of neural tube defects. Resistance to dolutegravir is relatively slow to develop, with at least 2 mutations required to acquire resistance, demonstrating synergy with other NRTIs in combination regimens.

#### Abacavir: Benefits and Toxicity

Abacavir's key resistance property is that it requires at least 2 mutations to acquire resistance, showing synergy with other NRTIs. However, abacavir hypersensitivity reaction is a critical safety concern. This reaction can be fatal and typically presents with fever, rash, abdominal pain, pharyngitis, and dyspnea. If abacavir hypersensitivity reaction occurs, rechallenge is absolutely contraindicated, as re-exposure may precipitate severe or fatal reaction.

#### Lamivudine, Efavirenz, and Protease Inhibitors: Individual Profiles

Lamivudine (3TC) is a well-tolerated NRTI available as an easy-to-take syrup and scored tablets, with an excellent safety profile overall. Efavirenz carries the burden of CNS side effects including drowsiness, insomnia, abnormal dreams, hallucinations, nightmares, and poor concentration. These effects are often significant enough that bedtime dosing is specifically recommended to minimize daytime impact.

Lopinavir/ritonavir (marketed as Kaletra or Aluvia) has a distinctive side effect profile including a strange and strong taste, increased triglycerides and cholesterol, hepatitis, pancreatitis, lipodystrophy, and numerous drug interactions that complicate co-administration with other medications.

#### Zidovudine and Hematologic Toxicity

Zidovudine carries important toxicological concerns. Adverse effects include risk of anemia, neutropenia, headache, and myalgia. Practical considerations include the fact that capsules are insoluble, and the syrup formulation must be kept in a brown (light-opaque) bottle to prevent degradation.

#### Early and Long-Term Toxicities of HAART

Common early toxicities associated with HAART therapy include gastrointestinal intolerance, anemia, sleep disturbances, and hyperbilirubinemia. These effects often improve with time or dose adjustment.

Hematologic complications deserve particular attention. Anemia is seen most frequently with zidovudine (AZT/ZDV). Neutropenia, while less common, usually causes no clinical complications unless the absolute polymorphonuclear cell (PMN) count falls below 250 cells/μL. Neutropenia in HIV patients can result from non-ARV drugs including trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole (TMP-SMX), ganciclovir, rifabutin, and hydroxyurea, in addition to zidovudine which remains the ARV most associated with this complication. Thrombocytopenia, by contrast, is typically associated with untreated HIV rather than with antiretroviral drugs.

Long-term metabolic complications can develop during extended HAART. These include glucose disorders, insulin resistance, impaired glucose tolerance, and hyperglycemia progressing to diabetes mellitus. Dyslipidemia develops with decreased HDL cholesterol and increased triglycerides and total cholesterol. Morphologic changes reflecting lipodystrophy syndrome include fat accumulation in certain areas, abdominal obesity, buffalo hump appearance, and gynecomastia (breast tissue enlargement in males).

#### Tuberculosis-HIV Coinfection Management

When tuberculosis is diagnosed before ART initiation, the recommended approach is to complete 2 to 8 weeks of tuberculosis therapy before commencing ART. However, this timeline is dramatically shortened for patients with severe immunosuppression: if CD4 count is less than 50 cells/mm³, ART should be initiated as soon as possible. ART should be started as soon as the patient is tolerating tuberculosis therapy.

The choice of antiretroviral regimen in children with active tuberculosis differs from the standard regimen. Efavirenz-based regimens are generally preferred in patients with active tuberculosis. If lopinavir/ritonavir is being used, dose adjustment is mandatory: ritonavir should be doubled in patients with active tuberculosis due to enzyme induction by rifampicin reducing drug levels.

When tuberculosis is diagnosed after ART initiation has already begun, the approach differs: ART should be continued throughout TB treatment rather than interrupted or restarted.

Isoniazid preventive therapy (IPT) is recommended for HIV-infected children. Specifically, post-exposure IPT at a dose of 10 mg/kg/day for 6 months is recommended for all HIV-infected children irrespective of age or ART status. In contrast, pre-exposure IPT (given to children without known TB exposure) is not recommended in any HIV-infected or HIV-uninfected child.

#### Immunization in HIV-Infected and HIV-Exposed Children

HIV-infected and HIV-exposed children should be immunized according to the routine national immunization schedule. This is because routine immunizations are vital—HIV-infected children are more susceptible to infections due to their weakened immune systems, making vaccination even more critical than in non-infected children.

However, the schedule differs by vaccine type and disease severity. The BCG vaccine is recommended for asymptomatic HIV-infected children only and must not be given to symptomatic HIV infection due to risk of disseminated BCG disease. Optimal timing for BCG in asymptomatic children is at birth. Measles vaccine, by contrast, is recommended for both asymptomatic and symptomatic HIV-infected children, given at 6 and 9 months of age. Yellow fever vaccine, similar to BCG, is recommended for asymptomatic HIV-infected children only (not for symptomatic HIV infection), with optimal timing at 9 months of age.

#### Treatment and Prophylaxis of Opportunistic Infections

Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia treatment follows established first-line protocols. TMP-SMX (trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole) is the first-line treatment agent. Experts recommend that glucocorticoids be included in the treatment regimen to reduce inflammation and improve oxygenation.

CMV disease transmission occurs through multiple routes: from mother to child perinatally, through breastfeeding, and during pregnancy, as well as through person-to-person contact with secretions.

#### Immune Reconstitution Inflammatory Syndrome (IRIS)

Immune reconstitution inflammatory syndrome (IRIS), also colloquially termed "HAART attacks," represents a paradoxical clinical deterioration that typically occurs in the first 6 weeks after ART initiation. The mechanism involves the recovering immune system responding more vigorously to antigens from existing infections, resulting in an exaggerated inflammatory response. This complication can manifest with worsening of pre-existing opportunistic infections or inflammatory conditions, and recognition is essential to distinguish IRIS from treatment failure or drug toxicity.

#### Public Health Context and Treatment Goals

The global treatment target known as the 90-90-90 goal has been proposed to accelerate HIV disease control: 90% of the population tested for HIV, 90% of those testing positive placed on antiretroviral treatment, and 90% of those on treatment virally suppressed. This ambitious target requires coordinated efforts across testing, linkage to care, and treatment adherence programs.

The impact of combination antiretroviral therapy introduction has been transformative in paediatric HIV care. Early recognition of the two most impactful interventions—the availability of highly effective combination ART and the early initiation of treatment in infants diagnosed with HIV—has fundamentally shifted the trajectory of the disease from one of inevitable decline to one of durable viral suppression and preserved immune function compatible with normal growth and development.

-

## 2025 MANAGEMENT OF SEIZURES

### Lecture Notes


#### Definitions and Terminology

A **seizure** is an abnormal, excessive, or synchronous discharge of neurons that results in sudden, transient alterations in neurological function, manifested by clinical or EEG changes. The terms "convulsion" and "fit" are often used colloquially to describe seizures, with convulsion specifically referring to involuntary muscular contractions and relaxations or violent uncontrollable contraction of muscles.

**Epilepsy** differs from a single seizure; it is a tendency to have recurrent seizures—a chronic disorder characterized by a predisposition to generate epileptic seizures and by the neurobiological, cognitive, psychological, and social consequences of this condition. An **epilepsy syndrome** is a complex of signs and symptoms that define a unique epilepsy condition, typically with specific etiology, age of onset, seizure types, EEG pattern, prognosis, and response to treatment.

**Status epilepticus** represents a serious state of prolonged seizure activity or a series of seizures without full recovery of consciousness between attacks. It is formally defined as seizure activity lasting more than 30 minutes or two or more seizures without regaining consciousness. This condition has two main presentations: **convulsive status epilepticus** consists of prolonged or recurrent generalized tonic-clonic seizures with persistent alteration of consciousness and visible muscle contractions, while **non-convulsive status epilepticus** is a state of mental alteration without visible muscle contractions, characterized by absence or minimal motor signs despite ongoing epileptic activity on EEG.

#### Evolution of Seizure Classification

The modern era of seizure classification began in **1964** when Gastaut and colleagues proposed the first modern classification system. This approach gained international adoption in **1970** when Gastaut's work was popularized worldwide. The widespread implementation of video electroencephalography prompted a major update to seizure classification in **1981**, which introduced four classification terms: simple partial, complex partial, generalized, and unclassifiable seizures. This 1981 classification became the standard in clinical practice.

Epilepsy classifications underwent partial updates in 1985 and 1989, followed by another revision in 2010, which served as an intermediate stage toward a final accepted epilepsy classification. The 2010 ILAE decision was to maintain the 1981 classification of seizures with minor changes, following extensive discussions at the beginning of the 21st century.

#### ILAE 2017 Classification

The **1981 classification was superseded by the 2017 classification**, with careful mapping of prior 1981 terminology to the new 2017 terms. The **ILAE 2017 Classification of Epilepsies** represents the most current international standard for classifying epilepsy syndromes, reflecting advances in genetics, neuroimaging, and understanding of seizure semiology.

The **ILAE 2017 basic version** of seizure type classification stratifies seizures into three main categories: **generalized-onset**, **focal-onset**, and **unknown-onset** seizures. This simplified framework is practical for clinical use. The **expanded version** includes detailed subcategories, with generalized-onset seizures subdivided into tonic-clonic, absence, myoclonic, and other types; focal-onset seizures further stratified into focal aware, focal impaired awareness, and focal to bilateral tonic-clonic categories; and unknown-onset seizures representing cases where the origin cannot be determined.

The diagnostic approach to seizure and epilepsy classification incorporates three main diagnostic tools: **semiology** (clinical features and patient/witness descriptions), **EEG** (electroencephalography), and occasionally supplementary information from **neuroimaging**. For classification of epilepsy type and epilepsy syndrome, additional investigations including **genetics**, **laboratory findings**, and **neuro-imaging findings** are utilized while maintaining a major focus on etiology throughout the diagnostic process.

#### Seizure Types and Semiology

**Automatisms** are involuntary, coordinated motor behaviors occurring during altered consciousness in seizures. Common examples include lip smacking, chewing, picking at clothes, and mumbling. These manifest as purposeless but organized movements that reflect the altered state of consciousness during a seizure episode.

**Epileptic spasms** (also called infantile spasms) are sudden, brief flexor or extensor contractions of muscles that typically occur in clusters and are often associated with developmental regression. They are particularly characteristic of West syndrome and warrant urgent evaluation and treatment.

**Tonic seizures** are characterized by sustained muscle rigidity or contraction lasting seconds to minutes, with increased tone throughout the body. These may be either focal or generalized in presentation.

**Absence seizures** are brief episodes of altered consciousness lasting 5 to 10 seconds that typically occur multiple times daily. They are characterized by sudden onset and sudden offset without postictal confusion. During absence seizures, patients may exhibit subtle automatisms or myoclonic jerks. These seizures are distinctive for their brief duration and the absence of confusion following termination.

#### Mechanisms of Anti-Seizure Drug Action

Anti-seizure medications work through diverse mechanisms targeting different components of neuronal function. Understanding these mechanisms helps guide selection for individual patients and specific seizure types.

**Sodium channel blockade** occurs in two main forms: **phenytoin** and **carbamazepine** block repetitive sodium channel activation, while **lacosamide** enhances slow inactivation of sodium channels. Additional medications that block sodium channels include **lamotrigine**, **topiramate**, and others.

**Calcium channel modulation** represents another key target. **Ethosuximide** and **valproic acid** block T-type calcium channels, which is particularly relevant for absence seizures. **Zonisamide** blocks N- and L-type calcium channels. **Lamotrigine** adds another dimension by blocking both N- and L-type calcium channels in addition to its sodium channel effects and modulation of H-current.

**Topiramate** employs multiple mechanisms: blockage of voltage-gated sodium channels, blocking of AMPA (alpha-amino-3-hydroxy-5-methyl-4-isoxazole propionic acid) receptors, and inhibition of carbonic anhydrase. This multi-targeted approach contributes to its broad spectrum of efficacy.

**GABA-A receptor enhancement** is the primary mechanism of **phenobarbital** and **benzodiazepines**, which prolong the open state of these chloride channels to facilitate cellular hyperpolarization.

Other unique mechanisms include **NMDA receptor blockade** by **felbamate** and opening of neuronal potassium channels by **ezogabine**.

#### Phenobarbital

Phenobarbital is a barbiturate used for the treatment of **generalized and focal seizures**. Its mechanism of action involves binding to the GABA-A receptor and prolonging its open state, allowing for increased chloride influx and consequent cellular hyperpolarization.

Despite its long history of efficacy, phenobarbital is difficult to use in modern clinical practice due to its **significant sedating properties**, which compromise quality of life and cognitive function. A major clinical consequence of phenobarbital's interaction with the cytochrome P450 system is that it is a **potent inducer of the CYP system** and thus can substantially **decrease serum levels of other drugs metabolized by this pathway**, necessitating careful monitoring of drug interactions.

#### Carbamazepine

**Carbamazepine (CBZ)** is a first-line agent used for **focal and generalized seizures**. Beyond seizure management, it is also used for patients with bipolar disorder and trigeminal neuralgia.

The mechanism of action involves **inhibition of voltage-gated sodium channels**, which reduces neuronal firing and prevents seizure propagation. Carbamazepine is **metabolized by the cytochrome P450 system and is a strong inducer of hepatic enzymes**, leading to decreased levels of other drugs metabolized by this pathway. This enzyme-inducing property necessitates careful management of drug interactions and potential dose adjustments of concomitant medications.

Common side effects of carbamazepine include **gastrointestinal upset, hyponatremia, rash, itching, drowsiness, dizziness, blurred vision, and headache**. These are generally manageable and often improve with dose titration or time.

More serious and rare but dangerous side effects include **Stevens-Johnson syndrome, toxic epidermal necrolysis, leukopenia, and aplastic anemia (pancytopenia)**. These serious hematologic and dermatologic reactions require immediate medical attention and discontinuation of the medication.

#### Valproate

**Valproate (VPA)** is a **broad-spectrum anti-seizure medication** used for both focal and generalized seizures. Its effectiveness arises from multiple mechanisms of action: **voltage-gated sodium channel inhibition**, **augmentation of GABA concentrations**, and **mild inhibition of T-type calcium currents** (though not to the extent of ethosuximide).

Valproate is **metabolized by the liver and is an inhibitor of CYP enzymes**, so it must be used with caution in hepatic insufficiency. An important contraindication is its use in **patients with urea cycle disorders**, due to the high risk of **hyperammonemia**. When combining topiramate with valproate, clinicians must be aware that this combination can lead to **increased levels of ammonia**.

General side effects of valproate include **nausea and vomiting, tremor, easy bruising, weight gain, insulin resistance, metabolic syndrome, and subclinical hypothyroidism**. These metabolic and gastrointestinal effects should be monitored and discussed with patients.

Serious adverse effects of valproate include **acute hepatocellular injury with jaundice and acute pancreatitis**. Notably, valproate is **contraindicated during pregnancy** due to **teratogenic neurotoxicity** that has been observed with in-utero exposure, making it unsuitable for women of childbearing potential who may become pregnant.

#### Phenytoin

**Phenytoin** is widely used for **focal and generalized seizures** and is also administered for status epilepticus and as a second-line agent for patients with mixed seizure types such as tonic-clonic and myoclonic seizures.

The **primary mechanism of action** involves **blocking voltage-gated sodium channels**. Other possible mechanisms include decreased synaptic transmission, smaller changes in ionic gradients involving the sodium-potassium ATPase pump, and inhibition of calcium-calmodulin phosphorylation.

Phenytoin is a **broad-spectrum inducer of the CYP system**, leading to **decreased levels of other drugs metabolized by this pathway**, similar to phenobarbital and carbamazepine. This enzyme-inducing effect is a significant concern for polypharmacy.

**Major adverse effects** of phenytoin include **gingival hyperplasia** (enlargement of gum tissue), **increased body hair**, **folic acid depletion**, **rash**, and **worsening bone density**. Patients on long-term therapy should be counselled regarding the possible development of **confusion, ataxia, double vision, and neuropathy**, which can emerge insidiously with prolonged use.

#### Levetiracetam

**Levetiracetam** has a broad spectrum of efficacy and represents a modern anti-seizure agent with favorable properties. Its **precise mechanism is not fully understood**, but studies have linked its effectiveness to **binding of synaptic vesicle protein 2A (SV2A)**.

The spectrum of levetiracetam efficacy is **very broad**, and it is used as **adjunctive therapy for focal-onset seizures in children and adults**, **myoclonic seizures in patients with juvenile myoclonic epilepsy (ages 12 and up)**, and **primary generalized tonic-clonic seizures**. This broad applicability makes it a versatile option across many seizure types.

A major advantage of levetiracetam regarding drug interactions is that it is **uniquely metabolized independent of the hepatic CYP system and does not induce CYP enzymes** like phenytoin, phenobarbital, and carbamazepine. This dramatically simplifies polypharmacy and eliminates concerns about enzyme induction.

Levetiracetam **does not necessitate a prolonged titration period** like lamotrigine, which carries a risk of Stevens-Johnson syndrome. This allows for more rapid achievement of therapeutic doses. The medication is **extremely well-tolerated**, with common adverse effects limited to **behavioral disturbances, drowsiness, dizziness, and upper respiratory infections**—a notably favorable safety profile compared to many other anti-seizure medications.

#### Ethosuximide

**Ethosuximide** represents an exception in anti-seizure medication use, with **specific and limited use for absence seizures**. Its narrow spectrum makes it ideally suited for patients with absence-type seizures, where its selective mechanisms provide excellent efficacy with fewer off-target effects than broad-spectrum agents.

#### Topiramate

**Topiramate** is a broad-spectrum anti-seizure medication with multiple mechanisms of action: **blockage of voltage-gated sodium channels**, **enhancement of GABA transmission**, **antagonization of NMDA receptors**, and **modest carbonic anhydrase inhibition**. This multi-modal approach accounts for its broad effectiveness.

Topiramate can be used as **monotherapy in both pediatric and adult medicine for focal-onset seizures and primary generalized tonic-clonic seizures (ages 10 and up)**. It can also be used as **adjunctive therapy for focal seizures or in patients with Lennox-Gastaut syndrome (ages 2 and up)**, making it flexible for various clinical scenarios and age groups.

Common side effects of topiramate include **weight loss, impaired cognition, difficulties with expressive language, fatigue, depression, headache, and paresthesias**. The cognitive effects warrant discussion with patients, particularly students and those in cognitively demanding professions.

Serious metabolic complications can occur, including **metabolic acidosis with tachypnea** and **calcium phosphate nephrolithiasis (kidney stones)**. These complications require monitoring through laboratory assessment and patient education about hydration and warning signs.

#### Lamotrigine

**Lamotrigine** is a broad-spectrum anti-seizure medication used across multiple seizure types. It is also used off-label as a mood stabilizer in psychiatric practice.

A critical consideration with lamotrigine is that it requires **prolonged and careful titration** due to a significant risk of **Stevens-Johnson syndrome (SJS)**, particularly at the **initiation of therapy or with rapid dose increases**. This serious dermatologic reaction necessitates slow titration protocols and patient education regarding warning signs such as unusual rashes.

Lamotrigine's mechanisms of action include **blocking sodium channels**, **blocking N- and L-type calcium channels**, and **modulating H-current**. This multi-targeted approach contributes to its broad spectrum of effectiveness across seizure types.

#### Off-Label Uses of Anti-Seizure Medications

Anti-seizure medications are increasingly used for indications beyond epilepsy, leveraging their neurobiological mechanisms for other conditions. **Pregabalin, clonazepam, and clobazam** are used off-label as anxiolytics. **Zonisamide and valproic acid** are used off-label for migraine relief. **Valproic acid, lamotrigine, and carbamazepine** serve off-label as mood stabilizers in psychiatric management. **Pregabalin, gabapentin, and carbamazepine** are utilized off-label for neuropathic pain relief. **Zonisamide and topiramate** are used off-label for weight loss. Additionally, **zonisamide** has been used off-label as an antiparkinsonian agent.

#### Febrile Seizures

**Simple febrile seizures** are defined as a short generalized seizure of duration less than 15 minutes, not recurring within 24 hours, occurring during a febrile episode not caused by an acute disease of the nervous system, in a child aged 6 months to 5 years with no neurologic deficits (no pre-, peri-, or postnatal brain damage, normal psychomotor development, and no previous afebrile seizures). Fever may not be detected before the seizure, but it must be present at least in the immediate post-acute period and be the symptom of a pediatric disease.

**Complex febrile seizures** are defined as focal or generalized prolonged seizure of duration greater than 15 minutes, recurring more than once in 24 hours, associated with post-ictal neurologic abnormalities (more frequently post-ictal palsy or Todd's palsy), or with previous neurologic deficits. If duration is greater than 30 minutes or there is no regaining of consciousness, this constitutes febrile status epilepticus.

#### Admission and Investigation of Febrile Seizures

Admission to hospital is indicated for a simple febrile seizure in the **first episode or for known diagnosis of simple febrile seizures when without reliable familiar context**. For complex febrile seizures, **admission is indicated for complex febrile seizures or febrile seizure without reliable familial context**.

For simple febrile seizures, **routine laboratory tests, EEG, and neuro-imaging are not recommended**, as these are low-yield in uncomplicated cases. However, **lumbar puncture is indicated with meningeal signs**, should be done before antibiotic treatment, is particularly useful in children less than 18 months old, and in children greater than 18 months should not be done routinely but rather based on the clinical assessment of the individual patient.

For complex febrile seizures, investigation is more thorough: **search for aetiology using bloods, EEG is recommended** (particularly for viral encephalitis), **neuro-imaging is recommended**, and **lumbar puncture is necessary for all patients with suspected CNS infections**.

#### Treatment of Febrile Seizures

For simple febrile seizures lasting 2 to 3 minutes, **no treatment is required**, as these brief seizures typically self-resolve without complication. For **prolonged febrile seizures**, management can occur in a **hospital or home setting**, with focus on prevention and management of recurrence risk. For **complex febrile seizures**, treatment is **variable, depending on aetiology, semiology, and prognosis**, and must be **tailored to the specific underlying cause** rather than following a standardized protocol.

#### Assessment and Diagnostic Approach to Seizures and Epilepsy

A **comprehensive history** in a patient with seizures or epilepsy includes detailed seizure history encompassing onset, frequency, trigger factors, duration, semiology, post-ictal features, and impact on daily function. This detailed characterization is essential for accurate seizure classification.

**Physical examination** should include general medical examination with vital signs, full neurological examination, assessment of neurodevelopmental status, and evaluation for stigmata of underlying syndromes. These findings may reveal clues to syndromic diagnoses or structural etiologies.

**Bedside investigations** include fingerstick glucose and electrolytes assessment via simple bedside testing, along with other point-of-care investigations as indicated. These rapid assessments can identify reversible causes such as hypoglycemia.

**Laboratory investigations** should include complete blood count, serum electrolytes, renal function, liver function tests, glucose, and other tests as clinically indicated based on seizure type and suspected etiology. These baseline investigations help identify systemic causes or establish baseline values for medication monitoring.

**Neuroimaging**, including CT or MRI, should be considered based on seizure type, age, clinical presentation, and suspected etiology to identify structural abnormalities that may be causative or relevant to prognosis.

**Electroencephalography (EEG)** is essential for seizure classification, syndrome identification, and prognostic evaluation. EEG may include routine, prolonged, or ambulatory recordings depending on clinical context and diagnostic yield required.

#### Treatment Principles

**Medication is the mainstay treatment strategy for seizures**. When selecting anti-seizure medications, clinicians must balance multiple factors: **efficacy** for the specific seizure type, **adverse events** profile, **cost** of the medication, potential **drug interactions** with other patient medications, **patient preference** regarding administration schedules and side effect profiles, and **availability** of the medication in local settings.

**Monotherapy is the ideal pathway for the treatment of seizures**, as it minimizes adverse effects and drug interactions. Patients should be started on a single appropriate medication at a low dose and titrated to efficacy, adding additional agents only when monotherapy fails to achieve seizure control.

#### Broad-Spectrum vs. Narrow-Spectrum Anti-Seizure Medications

**Broad-spectrum anti-seizure medications** are those with **efficacy across multiple seizure types and epilepsy syndromes**. The list of broad-spectrum medications includes: **levetiracetam, lamotrigine, zonisamide, topiramate, valproic acid, clonazepam, perampanel, clobazam, and rufinamide**. These medications provide flexibility in treating patients with multiple seizure types or where seizure type classification remains uncertain.

**Narrow-spectrum anti-seizure medications** have **more limited efficacy, typically effective for specific seizure types or syndromes**. The list of narrow-spectrum medications includes: **lacosamide, pregabalin, gabapentin, carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine, ezogabine, phenytoin, and vigabatrin**. These agents are reserved for specific indications where their particular mechanisms provide optimal efficacy with fewer off-target effects.

#### Factors in Anti-Epileptic Agent Selection

When choosing anti-epileptic agents, clinicians should consider the **age of the patient** (as some medications are contraindicated or less effective in specific age groups), the **seizure semiology** (the specific type and characteristics of seizures), and the **clinical setting and contraindications** (such as pregnancy status, hepatic insufficiency, or psychiatric comorbidities).

#### Special Considerations

The modern approach to seizure management emphasizes individualized treatment selection based on seizure type, epilepsy syndrome, patient age, comorbidities, and medication side effect profiles. Understanding the mechanisms of action, metabolism, and adverse effects of each medication allows clinicians to make informed decisions that optimize seizure control while minimizing harm and maximizing quality of life for their pediatric patients.

-

## 2025 PALLIATIVE

### Lecture Notes


#### Definition and Scope of Palliative Care

Palliative care represents a fundamental shift in medical approach—from cure-focused intervention to comprehensive care addressing suffering. According to the International Association for Hospice and Palliative Care (IAHPC), palliative care is the active holistic care of individuals across all ages with serious health-related suffering due to severe illness, and especially of those near the end of life. It aims to improve the quality of life of patients, their families and their caregivers.

In the paediatric context, this definition becomes more intimate and family-centred. Palliative care for children is the active total care of the child's body, mind and spirit, and also involves giving support to the family. This holistic approach acknowledges that paediatric palliative care extends beyond the patient to encompass the entire family unit as an integrated component of care delivery.

#### Timing and Approach to Paediatric Palliative Care

A crucial distinction in paediatric palliative care lies in its timing. Contrary to the historical view that palliative care begins only at end-of-life, paediatric palliative care begins when illness is diagnosed, and continues regardless of whether or not a child receives treatment directed at the disease. This approach allows for continuity of care whether the trajectory involves curative attempts, disease modification, or purely comfort-focused measures.

The effective delivery of paediatric palliative care requires a broad multidisciplinary approach that includes the family and makes use of available community resources. Importantly, it can be successfully implemented even if resources are limited. This principle is especially relevant in resource-constrained settings, where creative adaptation of available services—whether in hospitals or communities—enables meaningful palliative support.

#### Settings for Delivery

Paediatric palliative care is not confined to institutional settings. Care may be provided in tertiary care facilities, in community health centres, and even in the child's own home. This flexibility allows families to choose environments that best suit their preferences, values, and practical circumstances, supporting the goal of maximizing quality of life across diverse contexts.

#### Core Provider Responsibilities

Health providers delivering paediatric palliative care must evaluate and alleviate a child's physical, psychological and social distress. This three-dimensional understanding of distress ensures that symptom management addresses not only bodily discomfort but also emotional suffering and social disadvantage. The comprehensive assessment of these domains forms the foundation for individualised, responsive care planning.

#### Classification of Conditions Requiring Palliative Care

Understanding the trajectory and prognosis of childhood illnesses is central to appropriate palliative care planning. Two key classification schemes help clarify the context:

**Life-Limiting versus Life-Threatening Conditions:**

A life-limiting condition is defined as a condition for which there is no reasonable hope of cure and from which children will die. These are conditions with an inherently progressive course toward death.

In contrast, a life-threatening condition is defined as a condition for which curative treatment may be feasible but can fail. Life-threatening conditions carry the possibility of cure but also risk of treatment failure and death.

This distinction is important because it affects both the emphasis of medical intervention and the psychological preparation needed by families. A child with a life-threatening condition may still pursue curative measures while simultaneously preparing for potential palliative focus. A child with a life-limiting condition requires palliative care as the primary framework, though some disease-directed treatments may still be offered for comfort or quality of life extension.

#### The ACT Classification Framework

The Association for Children's Palliative Care (ACT) has developed a four-category classification that provides more granularity in understanding which children benefit from palliative care services. This framework is widely used across paediatric settings to identify children requiring specialist palliative input and to understand the complexity of their care needs.

**ACT Category 1: Life-Threatening Conditions with Possible Cure**

ACT Category 1 comprises life-threatening conditions where curative treatment may be possible. Examples include Acute lymphoblastic leukaemia, other curable cancers, pulmonary TB, and organ failure when transplant is available. Children in this category may have intensive curative treatment as their primary focus, but they benefit from integrated palliative care that manages side effects, prepares families for possible treatment failure, and provides support should curative efforts not succeed.

**ACT Category 2: Prolonged Survival with Intensive Treatment**

ACT Category 2 encompasses conditions where premature death is inevitable, but children have long periods of wellness because of treatments. Examples include Cystic fibrosis, Duchenne's muscular dystrophy, HIV/AIDS on HAART, and renal failure where dialysis is available. Children with Category 2 conditions often enjoy extended periods of relatively good health between episodes of acute illness. Palliative care focuses on optimizing quality during wellness periods, managing complications, and preparing for inevitable progression.

**ACT Category 3: Progressive Conditions Requiring Exclusive Palliative Treatment**

ACT Category 3 includes progressive conditions where treatment is exclusively palliative. Examples are Batten disease, Spinal Muscular Atrophy (SMA), Mucopolysaccharidoses, inoperable heart disease, organ failure without transplant, Adrenoleukodystrophy (ALD), Trisomy 13 and 18, renal failure without dialysis, and HIV without HAART. For these conditions, curative treatment is not available, and palliative care forms the entire therapeutic framework. The focus is on maximizing comfort, maintaining dignity, and supporting families through progressive decline.

**ACT Category 4: Irreversible but Non-Progressive Conditions**

ACT Category 4 encompasses irreversible but non-progressive conditions with high risk of unpredictable life-threatening events. Examples include multiple disabilities following brain or spinal cord injury, complex healthcare needs, severe cerebral palsy, Foetal Alcohol Syndrome, birth asphyxia, Down's Syndrome, and Sickle Cell Anaemia. Children in this category have fixed neurological or physical impairments but may live for many years. Palliative care addresses quality of life, manages episodic crises, and prevents complications. Unlike Category 3 conditions, progression is not the defining feature—rather, the unpredictability of life-threatening events and the complexity of ongoing care needs drive palliative input.

#### The GAPS Framework: Core Domains of Paediatric Palliative Care

A comprehensive mnemonic framework guides the essential elements of paediatric palliative care: GAPS—Goals of care, Advance Care Planning, Psychosocial support, and Symptom Control. These four pillars ensure that all major dimensions of care are systematically addressed.

#### Goals of Care: Defining Care Trajectories

Goals of Care conversations establish what the care team and family are trying to achieve. These conversations must occur early and revisited regularly, as circumstances and family understanding evolve.

The three intervention levels that structure Goals of Care discussions are Cure, Rehabilitation, and Comfort. At any point in a child's illness, care may emphasise one or more of these levels. Some children may move from pursuing cure, to rehabilitation during periods of stability, to comfort as disease progresses. Others may hold multiple goals simultaneously—for example, seeking cure while ensuring comfort if cure is not achieved.

Key questions that should be considered in Goals of Care conversations include: What are you trying to achieve? (Cure? Rehab? Comfort?) and How aggressive should we be? (ICU, blood transfusions, antibiotics?). These questions help families articulate their values and priorities, and help clinicians align their interventions with family wishes.

#### Advance Care Planning: Anticipating the Future

Advance Care Planning is the process of discussing and documenting what matters most to a child and family, and what care they want as illness progresses. This includes several key components:

First, Advance Care Planning must revisit and clarify goals of care, ensuring they remain relevant to the child's current situation. Second, it specifies place of care—where the child and family wish care to be provided during different phases of illness and at the end of life. Third, it involves scenario sketching and 'what if' situations, helping families envision and prepare emotionally for possible futures rather than being shocked by them. Fourth, it addresses resuscitation decisions, including DNACPR (Do Not Attempt Cardio-Pulmonary Resuscitation) and what it means in practice for that particular child. Finally, it identifies medications required—both those to continue and those to discontinue, and what medications should be available if symptoms worsen.

#### Psychosocial Support: Addressing the Full Burden of Illness

Paediatric illness affects the child and family across emotional, spiritual, social, and economic dimensions. Psychosocial support issues that must be addressed in paediatric palliative care include:

Emotional issues such as fear and anxiety for both mother and child require skilled, compassionate assessment and intervention. Spiritual issues—including guilt and meaning—should be explored using structured approaches such as the FICA tool (Faith, Importance, Community, Address). Education access must be preserved, allowing children to continue schooling if they wish, or receive age-appropriate education adapted to their abilities. Play access and ability are essential to childhood—even seriously ill children benefit from play and age-appropriate activities that foster normalcy and joy. Financial issues such as housing and food security often emerge; families may require social work intervention to access financial support or practical resources. Bereavement support extends care beyond the child's death, providing structured grief support for surviving family members. Legacy planning allows children and families to create enduring memories and meaningful documents for the future.

#### Symptom Control: Managing Physical Suffering

The physical symptoms that most commonly require active management in paediatric palliative care are Pain, dyspnoea, cough, nausea and vomiting, thirst, and fatigue. Effective symptom control is foundational to quality of life and dignity. Each symptom requires individualised assessment, often drawing on pharmacological and non-pharmacological approaches. Pain assessment and management are particularly critical in paediatrics, as children may have difficulty expressing pain and myths about opioid use can delay necessary treatment. Dyspnoea and cough significantly affect comfort and sense of security. Nausea and vomiting can be debilitating and affect nutrition and quality of life. Thirst, often overlooked, can cause significant discomfort as illness advances. Fatigue, while seemingly less dramatic than acute symptoms, profoundly affects a child's capacity to engage with family, school, and activities that give life meaning.

#### Additional Considerations

Osteogenesis imperfecta is a condition characterised by brittle bones, and may be encountered in paediatric practice with its own palliative care considerations, particularly around pain management, prevention of fractures, and mobility support.

This course is instructed by Dr Julia Ambler, who brings clinical expertise and compassionate understanding to the teaching of paediatric palliative care principles.

-

## 2025 PMTC

### Lecture Notes


#### Overview: EMTCT and PMTCT

Eliminating Mother-to-Child Transmission (EMTCT) stands for "Preventing and ending mother to child transmission." Mother-to-Child Transmission (MTCT) is the transmission of HIV from an HIV-positive mother to her child during pregnancy, labour, delivery, or breastfeeding. Without any intervention, the transmission rate ranges from 15% to 45%, but effective interventions can reduce this below 5%.

EMTCT services are provided across a comprehensive time period from preconception through 18 months of age, or alternatively 6 weeks post cessation of breastfeeding if breastfeeding continues beyond 18 months. These services are integrated into multiple touchpoints including family planning visits, antenatal care (ANC), delivery, postnatal care (PNC), EPI (Expanded Programme on Immunisation) visits, and IMCI (Integrated Management of Childhood Illness) visits.

#### HIV Transmission Modes

HIV can be transmitted to children through multiple routes, each with distinct epidemiological significance.

**Perinatal Transmission Modes** include three primary routes: intrauterine transmission, intrapartum transmission, and breastfeeding. Intrauterine transmission accounts for 25–40% of perinatal transmission cases, while intrapartum transmission (during labour and delivery) represents 60–75% of cases. Breastfeeding contributes 5–28% of transmission risk overall, with variation based on the type of breastfeeding (exclusive breastfeeding carries lower transmission risk than mixed breastfeeding).

**Sexual Transmission** to children may occur through heterosexual contact, homosexual contact, or sexual abuse.

**Parenteral Transmission** occurs through non-sexual blood exposure, including the sharing of contaminated needles for illicit drug use or anabolic steroid injection.

**Iatrogenic Transmission** results from re-use of non-sterilized equipment in healthcare settings.

**Blood Product Transmission** is extraordinarily rare in the US, with an estimated risk of 1 in 2,000,000 for transmission from contaminated blood products.

#### Mechanisms of Mother-to-Child Transmission

**Intrauterine (In Utero) Transmission**

The majority of intrauterine transmission occurs in the third trimester. The mechanism involves breakdown of placental integrity leading to micro transfusions of viraemic maternal blood across the placenta to the fetus. Conditions that increase the risk of in utero transmission include genital tract infections and placental inflammation.

**Intrapartum Transmission**

Intrapartum transmission occurs through contact of infant mucosal membranes with HIV virus present in blood and secretions during labour and delivery. During labour contractions, microtransfusions cross the placenta from mother to fetus, contributing to transmission risk. Rupture of membranes greater than 4 hours is associated with increased risk when no antiretroviral therapy (ART) is given.

**Breastfeeding Transmission**

The risk of HIV transmission via breastfeeding varies substantially depending on the mother's infection status. In women with established HIV infection, the transmission risk is 14%. However, women who acquire HIV after delivery (primary infection/seroconversion) have a significantly higher rate of transmission at 29%. Maternal mastitis has been associated with increased breast milk HIV RNA levels and increased transmission risk via breastfeeding.

#### Clinical Manifestations in Perinatal HIV

**Early Presentation**
