At birth, there are no definitive clinical findings that can discriminate between infected and uninfected infants. Early complications that may occur include growth failure and CNS (central nervous system) disease.

**Progressive Signs and Symptoms**

Slow progression of clinical signs in pediatric HIV infection includes oral thrush, diffuse lymphadenopathy, hepatomegaly, splenomegaly, and recurrent diarrhea. Life-threatening opportunistic infections such as PCP (Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia) typically emerge at 4–6 months of life in untreated perinatal HIV.

Obscure clinical presentations may manifest as thrombocytopenia, encephalopathy, nephrotic syndrome, heart failure, or failure to thrive (FTT).

**Prognosis**

With modern antiretroviral therapy (HAART), the prognosis for perinatal HIV has dramatically improved. More than 95% of children with perinatal HIV infection are living at 16 years of age when on appropriate treatment.

#### Laboratory Testing for HIV Diagnosis

**Limitations of Antibody-Based Testing**

HIV antibody assays (ELISA, Western Blot) are unreliable in infants and young children due to persistence of transplacentally acquired maternal antibodies. Additionally, antibody tests may be falsely negative in the first weeks after acute infection.

**HIV DNA PCR (Polymerase Chain Reaction)**

HIV DNA PCR is the gold standard for early diagnosis of HIV in infants. The "D" in HIV DNA PCR stands for "Diagnosis" in neonates. Qualitative HIV-1 DNA PCR detects HIV proviral DNA within peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMCs) and can be performed on whole blood or dried blood samples. Early HIV diagnosis is critical because infected infants have high mortality and morbidity when not diagnosed and not on treatment.

Important limitations include: HIV DNA PCR will test positive even if the patient is on ART, and it will not detect very recent infection because 2/3 of MTCT occurs perinatally. However, its sensitivity improves markedly over the first months of life:
- At birth: 55% sensitivity
- 2–4 weeks of age: >90% sensitivity
- At 1, 3, and 6 months of age: 100% sensitivity

**HIV RNA Quantitation (Viral Load)**

HIV RNA quantitation measures viral load and is essential for monitoring treatment response and assessing transmission risk.

**Other Laboratory Tests**

Additional laboratory tests for HIV diagnosis and monitoring include genotype testing (to identify mutations in resistant strains), phenotype testing (in vitro sensitivity testing), and therapeutic drug monitoring.

#### EMTCT Services and Testing Algorithms

**Maternal HIV Testing**

During antenatal care, HIV testing should be performed at the first visit and at each routine basic antenatal care visit, for a total of 8 testing occasions during pregnancy. Maternal viral load testing should be done at delivery, 6 months postpartum, and 6 monthly if breastfeeding continues. The high-risk viral load threshold is defined as VL > 50 copies/ml.

**Maternal Adherence Support**

Since 2019, ART initiation guidance has included explicit recommendations for adherence counselling.

**HIV Counselling and Testing (HCT) Schedule During Pregnancy and Postpartum**

HIV Counselling & Testing should be performed:
- Every 3 months throughout pregnancy
- At labour/delivery
- At the 6 week EPI visit
- Every 6 months throughout breastfeeding

**Infant Testing Algorithms: Evolution of Protocols**

The infant HIV testing algorithms have evolved substantially across three major protocol updates.

**2015 Protocol**

In the 2015 protocol, HIV PCR testing in exposed infants was performed at:
- Birth
- 10 weeks
- 14 weeks (for high-risk infants only)
- 6 weeks post cessation of breastfeeding
- 18 months with rapid testing and a second rapid for confirmation

High-risk infants at birth were defined as those with maternal VL > 1000 copies/ml OR maternal ART started < 4 weeks prior to delivery.

**2019 Protocol**

The 2019 protocol introduced significant changes:
- HIV PCR at birth and 10 weeks (same as 2015)
- **Elimination of the 14-week test** (major change)
- 6-month PCR for all exposed infants (not just high-risk)
- Appropriate testing 6 weeks post cessation of breastfeeding
- Universal HIV testing at 18 months if child is exposed but uninfected
- PCR used as confirmatory test up to 2 years of age

High-risk infant definition changed to: Mother VL > 1000 copies/ml at delivery (or most recent viral load in last 12 weeks of ANC) OR mother with no VL documented in the last 12 weeks of ANC.

**2022 Protocol**

The 2022 protocol refined the high-risk definition to: Maternal VL < 50 copies/ml OR no known VL at birth.

**Screening and Confirmation Test Algorithms by Age**

- **<18 months**: Screening test is PCR, Confirmation test is PCR
- **18 months – 2 years**: Screening test is Rapid, Confirmation test is PCR
- **>2 years**: Screening test is Rapid, Confirmation test is Rapid

**Positive Test Confirmation and Initiation of Therapy**

When an infant <2 years has a positive PCR or positive rapid test result, confirmation should be performed with an HIV PCR test on a new sample. Critically, therapy should be initiated while awaiting the confirmatory result—do not delay treatment initiation.

#### Maternal and Infant Antiretroviral Prophylaxis

**Infant Prophylaxis Regimens by Protocol Year**

**2015 Protocol**

In the 2015 protocol, high-risk infants received:
- AZT (zidovudine) for 6 weeks
- NVP (nevirapine) for 12 weeks

**2019 Protocol**

The 2019 protocol specified:
- High-risk infants: AZT for 6 weeks and NVP for a minimum of 12 weeks
- NVP discontinuation rules: Stop NVP if mother's VL < 1000 copies/ml OR until 1 week after she stops breastfeeding

**Management When Maternal Viral Load Elevates**

If the mother's viral load elevates during the follow-up period, high-risk prophylaxis for the exposed infant should be restarted with AZT 12-hourly (for 6 weeks) and NVP daily (minimum 12 weeks), with dosages adjusted according to the infant's weight.

**Nevirapine Administration and Monitoring**

The first dose of nevirapine should be given as soon as possible after birth. Ideally, the birth PCR test should be done before administration of infant NVP, but any delay in testing should not delay the NVP administration.

NVP dosing follows strict guidelines: only one dose per 24-hour period should be given. If the baby vomits, the dose may be repeated once. 

At every infant visit, the mother's most recent viral load result should be checked to guide ongoing management decisions.

**Response to Positive PCR While on NVP**

If infant HIV PCR is positive while on NVP prophylaxis:
- Stop NVP immediately
- Confirm with a second HIV PCR test on a new sample
- Initiate ART while awaiting the second PCR test result
- Continue breastfeeding

**Breastfeeding Recommendations**

Breastfeeding in the context of EMTCT is recommended only in context of ART recommended for 24 months or longer, in line with recommendations for the general population.

#### Transmission Risk Factors and Rates

**Perinatal Transmission Risk Distribution**

The cumulative risk of perinatal HIV transmission without any intervention is 13–39%, distributed as follows:
- Intrauterine: 5%
- Peripartum: 13–18%
- Breastfeeding: 5–28%

The timing of transmission among those infected perinatally is:
- In utero: 25–40% of cases
- Intrapartum: 60–75% of cases

**Maternal Risk Factors**

Multiple maternal factors influence perinatal HIV transmission:
- HIV-1 RNA levels (viral load)
- Low CD4 lymphocyte count
- Other infections: Hepatitis C, CMV, bacterial vaginosis
- Maternal drug use
- Lack of AZT during pregnancy
- Cigarette smoking

**Obstetrical Risk Factors**

Obstetrical factors that increase transmission include:
- Length of ruptured membranes/chorioamnionitis
- Vaginal delivery (compared to caesarean delivery)
- Invasive procedures (such as scalp electrodes)
- Being the first-born of twins

**Infant Risk Factors**

Prematurity is a significant infant factor increasing transmission risk.

**Viral Load and Transmission Rates**

The relationship between maternal viral load and perinatal transmission rate is well-established from clinical data:

- **VL <400 copies/ml**: 0% transmission (confidence interval 0–11.0%, 32 cases)
- **VL 400–3,500 copies/ml**: 8.6% transmission (confidence interval 2.0–11.2%, 70 cases)
- **VL 3,500–10,000 copies/ml**: 19.4% transmission (confidence interval 10.3–28.5%, 72 cases)
- **VL 10,000–30,000 copies/ml**: 18.8% transmission (confidence interval 10.3–27.4%, 80 cases)
- **VL >30,000 copies/ml**: 34.8% transmission (confidence interval 29.5–40.1%, 115 cases)

Transmission can potentially occur at all levels of maternal viral load. Risk of transmission increases with increasing maternal viral load. However, maternal plasma and cervico-vaginal secretion viral loads do not always correlate, which may explain some variation in observed transmission despite similar plasma viral loads.

**Breastfeeding-Specific Transmission Rates**

As noted above, transmission via breastfeeding is 5–28% overall, with important variation. Women who seroconvert after delivery have a higher rate of transmission than women infected prior to delivery. The range reflects the variation between exclusive breastfeeding (lower risk) and mixed breastfeeding (higher risk).

#### Antiretroviral Safety in Infants

**General Considerations**

There is limited data on most ARVs in infants, particularly when given in combination. This requires careful clinical judgment and monitoring.

**Zidovudine (ZDV/AZT)**

ZDV is generally safe in infants but may cause transient anemia. When combined with 3TC, there may be increased hematologic toxicity.

**Nevirapine (NVP)**

Safety concerns with NVP in infants include rare cases of severe rash, hepatotoxicity, and the potential for resistance to develop in infants who become HIV infected.

**Protease Inhibitors**

Protease inhibitors are not recommended for neonates due to lack of pharmacokinetic (PK) data for most agents and possible cardiac and other toxicity (particularly with lopinavir/ritonavir [LPV/r]).

#### Summary and Key Clinical Takeaways

The prevention of mother-to-child transmission requires integrated, comprehensive services spanning from preconception through 18 months postpartum (or 6 weeks after breastfeeding cessation). Early diagnosis through HIV DNA PCR testing is critical, with sensitivity improving from 55% at birth to 100% by 1 month. Maternal viral load is the cornerstone driver of transmission risk, with transmission occurring at all viral load levels but escalating dramatically above 1,000 copies/ml. Maternal adherence to ART, with target viral load suppression below 50 copies/ml, is the key intervention preventing transmission. Infant prophylaxis with AZT and NVP, optimally timed and dosed according to weight, provides critical additional protection. Regular monitoring of maternal viral load at each visit guides ongoing prophylaxis decisions for exposed infants. The evolution of testing algorithms from 2015 through 2022 reflects accumulating evidence on optimal timing and methods for diagnosis. With these comprehensive interventions, transmission rates can be reduced from 15–45% to below 5%, and with undetectable maternal viral loads, transmission becomes extremely rare.

-

## 2025 PRESCRIBING

### Lecture Notes


#### Paediatric Physiology and Dosing Principles

Children are not simply dosed as small adults in paediatric prescribing due to fundamental physiological differences. Children differ due to immaturity of liver and kidney, which results in low clearance and less efficient drug metabolism. Additionally, children have a higher percentage body water, which creates a larger apparent volume of distribution for water-soluble drugs. These key differences necessitate special consideration when determining appropriate drug dosages for the paediatric population.

Two methods are used for dose calculation in paediatrics: Weight and Body Surface Area. Each method has distinct advantages depending on the drug being administered and the clinical context.

#### Routes of Drug Administration

Paediatric prescribing must account for multiple routes of drug administration available to clinicians: Oral, Intravenous, Parenteral, Rectal, Topical, Inhaled, and Other routes. However, three child-specific issues must be considered when selecting administration routes: Compliance, Palatability and smell, and Pain. These factors significantly impact successful drug delivery and patient adherence to therapy in the paediatric setting.

#### Drug Scheduling and Regulatory Framework

The South African regulatory system classifies medicines into distinct schedules based on their safety profile and dispensing requirements. Schedule 0 medicines are designated for Shop (unrestricted trade), allowing open purchase without pharmacy involvement. Schedule 1 and 2 medicines are dispensed at OTC pharmacy (over-the-counter) locations. These OTC medicines can be dispensed with or without prescription and can be repeated without formal prescription restrictions.

Prescription requirements escalate for Schedule 3 and above medicines, where a Prescription is required for dispensing. For Schedules 3, 4, and 5 prescriptions, the maximum repeat period is 6 months maximum. Schedule 6 substances carry a unique restriction: NO repeats allowed because of habit forming nature, potential of abuse, and risk of adverse effects. This strict regulation ensures safety and prevents misuse of potentially dangerous drugs.

Section 21 authorization in South Africa allows SAHPRA to authorise medicines not registered in South Africa for medicinal purposes, providing a pathway for access to unregistered but potentially beneficial drugs when no suitable registered alternatives exist.

#### Clinical Trial Phases and Drug Development

The clinical development pathway for new medicines progresses through defined phases. Phase 0 of clinical trials involves Microdosing in humans to study pharmacokinetics and pharmacodynamics, providing initial human safety and metabolism data with minimal drug exposure. Phase 1 of clinical trials focuses on Safety, tolerability, side effects, and dose-ranging studies in healthy volunteers (small group), establishing the drug's basic safety profile and appropriate dosing range.

Phase 2 of clinical trials assesses Safety with dosing studies (component A) and Efficacy studies in a larger group (component B), beginning to evaluate whether the drug actually works for its intended indication while continuing to monitor safety. Phase 3 of clinical trials involves Randomized controlled trials in larger groups to assess efficacy in comparison to gold standard and side effects, providing robust evidence of the drug's effectiveness relative to existing treatments.

Phase 4 of clinical trials consists of Post-marketing surveillance for safety monitoring, continuing to track the drug's safety profile in real-world use after regulatory approval and market introduction.

#### Reference Books and Professional Resources

Paediatric prescribing relies on authoritative reference materials. Frank Shann is the author of the reference book 'Drug Doses' used in paediatric prescribing, a widely recognised resource. The three main reference books recommended for paediatric prescribing in South Africa are: Drug Doses by Frank Shann, Essential Medicines List and Standard Treatment Guidelines (2023), and South African Medicines Formulary. These references provide evidence-based guidance and dosing information essential for safe paediatric medication prescribing.

#### Prescription Writing Requirements

Proper prescription documentation is critical for patient safety and legal compliance. Patient information that must be included on a prescription chart comprises Name, identification number, diagnosis, ward, allergies, and weight. The patient's weight is particularly important in paediatrics given the weight-based dosing methods frequently employed.

Drug information must be included on a prescription with specific requirements: Name of drug (DO NOT use trade names), DO NOT use abbreviations, Dose, Frequency, Duration, and Route. Using generic drug names rather than trade names ensures clarity and avoids confusion across different formulations and manufacturers. Prescriber information is required on a prescription, including Name, qualifications, HPCSA number, and date of prescription. These prescriber identifiers ensure accountability and allow verification of prescriber credentials.

Only generic drug names should be used to ensure clarity and avoid confusion when writing prescriptions. This practice prevents medication errors that could result from unfamiliar brand names and ensures that any appropriately qualified healthcare provider can dispense the medication.

#### Drug-Specific Contraindications and Off-Label Use

Certain drugs carry important contraindications in the paediatric population. Ciprofloxacin is contraindicated in children under 18 years, reflecting concerns about potential effects on developing bone and cartilage. Aspirin can cause Reye's Syndrome in children and is therefore avoided in paediatric fever management and viral illness treatment.

Off-label drug use represents a common clinical reality in paediatrics. Sildenafil usually has no approved paediatric dose, demonstrating that sometimes drugs are used off-label which are not yet approved for paediatric use. Clinicians must exercise careful clinical judgment and ensure informed consent when considering off-label medication use in children, weighing the potential benefits against unknown or unproven risks in the paediatric population.

-

## 2025 WELCOME AND INTRODUCTION

### Lecture Notes


#### Course Overview

The CMED5CH module is an expansion of knowledge and skills in child health, instructed by Dr F Naby. The course is divided into two main components: neonatal care and general paediatrics, each with distinct objectives and clinical placements.

#### Neonatal Course Objectives

Students must be competent in taking an appropriate history for the neonate and performing a thorough examination of the newborn with specific focus on gestational age assessment using the Ballard score. The course emphasizes two key principles of neonatal resuscitation: KINC (a specific resuscitation protocol) and Kangaroo Mother Care. Students must recognize the three classifications of low birth weight babies: LBW (Low Birth Weight), VLBW (Very Low Birth Weight), and ELBW (Extremely Low Birth Weight). Understanding feeding methods and volumes used in neonatal care is essential, as is familiarity with common infections and conditions occurring in the neonate.

#### Paediatrics Course Objectives

Students must be able to take an appropriate history for the clinical problem and undertake an examination of the relevant system involved. A critical objective is to recognize the features and understand the principles of management of common disorders. Students must understand triage and resuscitation of sick children, and develop communication skills with caregivers including counseling abilities. Finally, students must observe and/or perform the designated procedures as required.

#### Course Structure

The course is structured as essentially lecture week conducted online through a hybrid portal with videos uploaded for asynchronous access. Clinical exposure is divided into 2 weeks of neonatal exposure and 2 weeks of paediatrics (4 weeks total). Clinical placement occurs at five hospitals in PMB: Edendale hospital, Greys, Northdale (Paeds), Queen Nandhi, and Newcastle complex. Students will be divided equally between Greys/NDH and Edendale Hospital only in PMB.

#### Clinical Placement Requirements and Safety

During clinical placement, students must wear masks, abide to social distancing, and practice hand hygiene. A critical policy states: DO NOT PRESENT TO THE CLINICAL SETTING IF YOU ARE NOT WELL; daily symptom tool monitoring is required. Students must remind the registrars and medical officers on the date and time of tutorial at the beginning of the week to ensure tutorials are scheduled appropriately.

#### Wardwork and Tutorials

Students should not cohort and group clerk. Students are allowed to clerk patients and participate in tutorials but must adhere to designated areas. The consultant in charge assists to oversee this process. All tutorials will be conducted at bedside. Additionally, students will now participate in intakes.

#### Assessments and Grading

Assessment in this module comprises formative and summative components with a clear breakdown. Formative assessments account for 20% of the final mark and include in-block assessment of a learning portfolio of clinical cases, in-block assessment of clinical case (conducted in-person), and professionalism score. Students must write 2 learning portfolios of clinical cases: 1 paediatric and 1 neonatal. The first portfolio must be submitted on Thursday of week 2 (marked for in-block assessment). The second learning portfolio (1 neonatal or 1 paediatric case) is submitted for final assessment and the assessment will be virtual.

Summative assessments consist of two components: Clinical examination accounts for 40% of the total mark and consists of 2 clinical cases and 1 portfolio case. Theory examination accounts for 40% of the total mark and is a paper-based structured clinical examination (MCQ single best answer).

The overall assessment breakdown by percentage is as follows: Formative 20% (portfolio, clinical case, professionalism), Clinical examination 40%, and Theory 40%.

#### Pass Marks and Supplementary Examination

The overall pass mark is 50% with a subminimum of 50% separately in the clinical and theory examinations, and a pass in at least 2/3 clinical components. If a student scores 40-50%, this results in a fail, but a supplementary examination will be granted. If a student scores less than 40%, this results in a fail with NO supplementary examination granted. Students eligible for supplementary examination may sit it early next year.

## ASTHMA 2021 CONSENSUS GUIDELINE

### Lecture Notes


#### Diagnosis

Asthma in children is fundamentally defined as variable expiratory airflow limitation with chronic airway inflammation. It remains largely a clinical diagnosis, supported by lung function testing in school-aged children. The diagnostic approach incorporates clinical features, bronchodilator response, and patterns of symptom occurrence.

**Clinical Features:** Children with asthma present with episodic wheeze, shortness of breath, difficult or laboured breathing, chest tightness, and reduced activity, with or without cough. These symptoms are often the primary indicators prompting investigation.

**Bronchodilator Response:** Symptoms that improve after correct use of a rapid-acting inhaled bronchodilator strongly support an asthma diagnosis, indicating variable airway obstruction responsive to beta-2 agonists.

**Wheezing Patterns and Frequency:** Wheezing that occurs frequently (more than three episodes per year) and lasts longer (more than ten days at a time) may indicate asthma. More importantly, wheeze that does not occur only during airway infections, but also in response to other triggers between infections, provides strong diagnostic support. Events that persist after age three years, with night-time worsening and association with exercise or environmental change (such as cold air exposure), further substantiate an asthma diagnosis.

**Diagnostic Approach in Children Under Six Years:** The diagnostic process for children younger than six years follows four structured steps. Step 1 involves detailed history taking, which remains the most important component. Step 2 requires excluding alternative reasons for wheezing episodes. Step 3 involves assessing airway inflammation through a therapeutic trial with inhaled corticosteroids (ICS). Step 4 seeks objective evidence of variable expiratory airflow limitation through appropriate testing.

**ICS Therapeutic Trial:** A pragmatic therapeutic trial with correctly administered low-dose inhaled corticosteroid will confirm the presence of corticosteroid-responsive airway inflammation, supporting an asthma diagnosis. Initial treatment response must be evaluated at 6-8 weeks following initiation. Additionally, presence of allergen-specific IgE (determined through skin-prick testing or ImmunoCAP) may support an asthma diagnosis by indicating atopic sensitisation.

#### Differential Diagnosis

When a child presents with wheeze, multiple alternative diagnoses must be considered before concluding asthma. The differential encompasses infective, structural, and functional causes.

**Infective Causes:** Wheeze may be secondary to bronchiolitis, atypical infection, bacterial airway infection, laryngotracheobronchitis, or protracted bacterial bronchitis. These conditions often feature acute onset related to viral or bacterial respiratory infection.

**Structural Causes:** Anatomical abnormalities can produce wheeze including tracheal and bronchomalacia, tracheal webs, tracheal or bronchial stenosis, lymphadenopathy, tumours, vascular compression (double aortic arch, innominate artery compression, left pulmonary artery sling, patent ductus arteriosus ligament, or cardiac chamber and pulmonary artery enlargement), laryngeal clefts, retained foreign body, and H-type tracheo-oesophageal fistula. These typically feature fixed obstruction and asymmetric findings.

**Functional Causes:** A range of functional disorders can mimic asthma including wheezy phenotypes, primary ciliary dyskinesia, cystic fibrosis, gastro-oesophageal reflux disease (GORD), perceived tight chest, pulmonary oedema or cardiac disease, interstitial lung disease, bronchiolitis obliterans, bronchopulmonary dysplasia, bronchiectasis, and immunodeficiency.

**Red Flag Findings:** Clinical findings suggesting an alternative diagnosis rather than asthma include digital clubbing, growth faltering, and asymmetric wheeze. These warrant further investigation before attributing symptoms to asthma alone.

#### Diagnostic Testing

Objective evidence of variable expiratory airflow limitation supports asthma diagnosis. Multiple testing modalities are available depending on child age and cooperation.

**Peak Expiratory Flow (PEF) Assessment:** PEF variability with an average daily diurnal variability greater than 13%, when documented twice daily for two weeks, indicates variable expiratory airflow limitation. Excessive variation of PEF greater than 15% between outpatient visits using the same equipment, with or without airway infections, similarly indicates variable airflow limitation.

**Exercise Challenge Testing:** A positive exercise challenge test is defined as a decrease in PEF greater than 15% after reaching target heart rate (calculated as 0.8 × 220 minus age in years). This indicates variable airflow limitation in response to exercise-induced obstruction.

**Spirometry Assessment:** Normal FEV1/FVC ratio is greater than 0.9. When the FEV1/FVC ratio is decreased due to reduced FEV1, variable expiratory airflow limitation is indicated.

**Bronchodilator Reversibility:** A positive bronchodilator reversibility test is defined by an increase in FEV1 greater than 12% following rapid-acting beta-2 agonist administration. This demonstrates reversible airflow obstruction characteristic of asthma.

**Exercise Challenge with Spirometry:** A positive exercise challenge test using spirometry is defined as a decrease in FEV1 greater than 12% after reaching target heart rate (0.8 × 220 minus age in years).

**FEV1 Variation:** Excessive variation of FEV1 greater than 12% between outpatient visits using the same equipment, with or without airway infections, indicates variable airflow limitation.

#### Severity Classification

Asthma severity is classified into four categories based on symptoms, night-time disturbance, peak expiratory flow, and PEF variability.

**Mild Intermittent Asthma:** Characterized by symptoms occurring twice or fewer per week, night-time symptoms two or fewer per month, PEF at least 80% of predicted, and PEF variability less than 20%.

**Mild Persistent Asthma:** Defined by symptoms occurring more than twice per week, night-time symptoms more than twice per month, PEF at least 80% of predicted, and PEF variability between 20-30%.

**Moderate Persistent Asthma:** Features daily symptoms, night-time symptoms more than once per week, PEF between 60-80% of predicted, and PEF variability greater than 30%.

**Severe Persistent Asthma:** Characterized by continual symptoms throughout the day, frequent night-time symptoms (often seven nights per week), PEF at or below 60% of predicted, and PEF variability greater than 30%.

#### Treatment Framework

Asthma management follows a stepped approach, with treatment intensity adjusted based on disease severity and control. Three age-based frameworks address the developmental and pharmacological differences across the paediatric population.

**Children Under Five Years**

*Intermittent Treatment:* For children with infrequent symptoms, management consists of a short-acting beta-2 agonist (SABA) as needed and a short course of ICS (7-10 days) at the start of upper respiratory tract infection.

*Step 1 Persistent Asthma:* Daily low-dose ICS with SABA as needed for symptom relief.

*Step 2 Persistent Asthma:* Medium-dose ICS is recommended. Alternatively, in children older than four years, daily low-dose ICS/LABA combination with SABA as needed is appropriate.

*Step 3 Persistent Asthma:* Either daily medium-dose ICS/LABA combination with SABA as needed, or medium-dose ICS combined with leukotriene-receptor antagonist (LTRA).

*Step 4 Persistent Asthma:* Referral to a specialist is recommended for further assessment and management of children under six years with inadequate control on Step 3 therapy.

**Children Six to Eleven Years**

*Step 1 Persistent Asthma:* Multiple options are available: daily low-dose ICS with SABA as needed, LTRA alone, or low-dose ICS administered only when SABA is taken.

*Step 2:* Daily and as-needed low-dose ICS/LABA (formoterol) combination, or low-dose ICS/LTRA combination.

*Step 3:* Either daily and as-needed medium-dose ICS/LABA (formoterol) combination, high-dose ICS/LABA combination, or the addition of LTRA or tiotropium to existing therapy.

*Step 4:* Referral for expert assessment for anti-IgE therapy or other biologic agents, or daily and as-needed high-dose ICS/LABA combination plus LTRA.

**Children Twelve Years and Older**

The dosing regimens for adolescents align with adult recommendations. ICS doses follow age-appropriate guidelines adjusted to disease severity, with similar stepwise progression through controller combinations and biologic options for severe disease.

**2021 Guideline Update - ICS-Formoterol Approach:** In children aged six years and older with moderate to severe persistent asthma, ICS-formoterol in a single inhaler is recommended as both daily controller and reliever therapy. This represents an advancement compared with either higher-dose ICS as daily controller therapy plus SABA for quick-relief, or the same-dose ICS-LABA as daily controller therapy plus SABA for quick-relief.

**2021 Guideline Update - Respiratory Tract Infection-Triggered ICS:** In children under six years with recurrent wheezing triggered primarily by respiratory tract infections and minimal wheeze between infections, a short course (7-10 days) of daily ICS at the onset of a respiratory tract infection with as-needed SABA is recommended. In children aged six to eleven years with similar presentation, as-needed SABA and a short ICS course (7-10 days) at the start of an upper-respiratory tract infection, or daily low-dose ICS, are recommended options.

#### ICS Dosing Tables

Inhaled corticosteroid dosing varies by drug, formulation, age group, and severity classification. All doses are expressed as total daily inhaled dose in micrograms (µg).

**Children Under Five Years**

- **Beclomethasone dipropionate (HFA):** 100 µg total daily
- **Budesonide (pMDI with spacer):** 200 µg total daily
- **Budesonide (nebulised):** 500 µg total daily
- **Fluticasone propionate (HFA):** 100 µg total daily

*Note on Potency:* Fluticasone propionate may be used at half the dose of budesonide equivalent due to its higher potency.

**Children Six to Eleven Years**

- **Beclomethasone dipropionate HFA:** Low 100-200 µg, Medium 200-400 µg, High >400 µg
- **Budesonide:** Low 100-200 µg, Medium 200-400 µg, High >400 µg
- **Ciclesonide:** Low 80 µg, Medium 80-160 µg, High >160 µg (Note: Approved for ≥12 years; used off-label in younger children)
- **Fluticasone propionate:** Low 100-200 µg, Medium 200-500 µg, High >500 µg
- **Mometasone furoate:** Low 110-220 µg, Medium 220-440 µg, High >440 µg
- **Triamcinolone acetonide:** Low 400-800 µg, Medium 800-1200 µg, High >1200 µg

**Adolescents Twelve Years and Older**

- **Beclomethasone dipropionate HFA:** Low 100-200 µg, Medium >200-400 µg, High >400 µg
- **Budesonide:** Low 200-400 µg, Medium >400-800 µg, High >800 µg
- **Ciclesonide:** Low 80-160 µg, Medium >160-320 µg, High >320 µg (Registered for ≥12 years)
- **Fluticasone propionate:** Low 100-250 µg, Medium >250-500 µg, High >500 µg
- **Mometasone furoate:** Low 110-220 µg, Medium >220-440 µg, High >440 µg

**Dosing Frequency and Formulation Considerations:** Budesonide is approved for once-daily dosing in patients with mild illness. When transitioning between CFC (chlorofluorocarbon) and HFA (hydrofluoroalkane) preparations, medication package inserts should be carefully reviewed for the equivalent correct dosage, as the conversion factor may not be 1:1.

**Ongoing ICS Benefit Review:** An ongoing benefit of ICS treatment should be reviewed every three months, with ICS maintained at the lowest possible dose necessary for symptom control.

#### ICS/LABA Combination Products

Combination inhalers containing both inhaled corticosteroids and long-acting beta-2 agonists (LABA) simplify dosing and improve adherence. All products are listed with ICS/LABA doses in micrograms.

**Fluticasone Propionate/Salmeterol**
- **DPI (Accuhaler):** 100/50 µg, 250/50 µg, 500/50 µg
- **pMDI (pressurised metered-dose inhaler):** 50/25 µg, 125/25 µg, 250/25 µg

**Budesonide/Formoterol Fumarate**
- **pMDI:** 80/4.5 µg, 160/4.5 µg
- **DPI (Turbuhaler):** 80/4.5 µg, 160/4.5 µg, 320/9 µg

**Fluticasone Furoate/Vilanterol**
- **pMDI:** 100/25 µg

**Mometasone Furoate/Formoterol Fumarate**
- **pMDI:** 100/5 µg (HFA preparation); also available CFC-free: 100/5 µg, 200/5 µg

#### Control Assessment

Asthma control is the extent to which the effects of asthma can be reduced or eliminated by treatment. Assessment includes evaluation of symptom control (frequency of symptoms, reliever medication use, and activity limitation over the past week and month) and future risk assessment (likelihood of exacerbations, medication side-effects, and lung function loss).

**Well Controlled Asthma:** Characterised by absence of all four of the following in the past week: daytime asthma symptoms more than twice per week, night-time awakening due to asthma, reliever (SABA) use more than twice per week, and limitation of activity.

**Partly Controlled Asthma:** Defined by the presence of one to two of these features in the past week.

**Uncontrolled Asthma:** Characterised by three to four of these features present in the past week.

**Assessment Interval:** After initiation of treatment, asthma control must be assessed at every follow-up visit, no less frequently than every six months, with treatment adjusted accordingly.

**Treatment Review Timing:** Asthma control should be reviewed 4-6 weeks after starting treatment, with assessment of control and stepping up or stepping down of treatment depending on the level of control achieved.

#### Devices

The selection of an appropriate inhalation device is a key principle of asthma treatment, with device choice suited to the patient's age and capability. Device technique significantly impacts treatment efficacy.

**Metered-Dose Inhaler with Spacer (MDI+Spacer):** The MDI with spacer is the most commonly used asthma delivery device. Spacers are simple holding chambers with or without a valve mechanism. Medication is suspended within the spacer and then breathed in, significantly enhancing lung deposition of the drug. A spacer is recommended for all children and adults with difficult-to-control asthma. A very effective spacer device can be constructed at low cost from a 500 mL plastic cold-drink bottle, with a hole large enough to accommodate the MDI mouthpiece cut in the bottom end to form a simple non-valved spacer.

**Dry-Powder Inhalers (DPI):** DPIs are easy to use but are only suitable for older children and adults because they require a specific inspiratory flow for actuation. Measurements of peak inspiratory flow can be performed with appropriate devices to assess suitability of DPI use in individual patients.

**Nebulisers:** Home nebulisers are not recommended for routine asthma management.

#### Monitoring and Follow-Up

Systematic monitoring ensures treatment efficacy and allows timely adjustment of therapy.

**ICS Therapeutic Trial Evaluation:** When an ICS therapeutic trial is initiated to assess for corticosteroid-responsive airway inflammation, initial treatment response must be evaluated at 6-8 weeks following start.

**Ongoing Monitoring:** An ongoing benefit of ICS treatment should be reviewed every three months, with ICS maintained at the lowest possible dose necessary for symptom control. After initiation of treatment, asthma control must be assessed at every follow-up visit, no less frequently than every six months.

**Treatment Adjustment:** Review asthma control 4-6 weeks after starting treatment. Assess control and step up or step down treatment depending on the level of control achieved. This systematic approach allows optimisation of therapy while avoiding overtreatment.

#### Education and Prevention

Patient and family education is integral to successful asthma management. Prevention strategies focus on both infection control and environmental trigger management.

**Viral Infection Prevention:** Asthmatic children should keep a distance from others (social distancing, approximately 1 metre) to reduce transmission of respiratory infections. Hand washing should be performed frequently for 20-30 seconds, always after coughing or sneezing.

**Mask-Wearing:** Mask-wearing to protect people from respiratory infection in public spaces is recommended by the World Health Organization (WHO) and is endorsed for asthma patients. There is no evidence to suggest that mask-wearing is deleterious to people with asthma.

**Environmental Trigger Control:** Asthmatic children should avoid environmental tobacco smoke and other indoor air pollutants. Exposure should be controlled for indoor allergens including house-dust mites, pets, cockroach, and mould. Identification and avoidance of environmental triggers is essential for optimal asthma control.

#### Adherence and Comorbidities

Poor medication adherence and untreated comorbidities are significant barriers to asthma control.

**ICS Adherence in Children:** Treatment adherence to asthma therapy in the paediatric population is poor, with studies reporting that only one-third of children use ICS therapy appropriately. Understanding and addressing adherence barriers is critical.

**Barriers to Adherence:** Barriers to adherence may be intentional, driven by illness perceptions or medication beliefs, leading to patients and caregivers deliberately choosing not to follow treatment recommendations. Alternatively, barriers may be unintentional, related to family routines and socioeconomic factors affecting ability to access or administer therapy consistently.

**Associated Comorbidities:** Identification and treatment of associated comorbidities may significantly improve asthma control. Important comorbidities include rhinitis or sinusitis, obesity, gastro-oesophageal reflux disease (GORD), and allergic bronchopulmonary aspergillosis (ABPA). Screening and management of these conditions should be part of comprehensive asthma care.

#### Management Cycle

Effective asthma management follows a structured cycle incorporating assessment, adjustment, and review phases.

**Assess Phase:** Initial and ongoing assessment encompasses diagnosis confirmation, evaluation of current symptom control, risk factor assessment (including environmental triggers and comorbidities), medication technique observation, and adherence evaluation.

**Adjust Phase:** Based on assessment findings, adjustments may include modification of controller medication type or dose, introduction of non-pharmacological strategies (environmental control, education), and treatment of identified modifiable risk factors.

**Review Phase:** Following adjustment, review focuses on medication effects and side-effects, the child's response to treatment modifications, and measurement of symptom control and lung function.

This cyclical process ensures systematic optimisation of asthma management over time.

#### Eight Long-Term Management Goals

Comprehensive asthma management should address eight key long-term goals: (1) achievement of good symptom control, (2) maintenance of normal activity levels, (3) minimisation of future asthma-related mortality risk, (4) reduction in frequency of exacerbations, (5) maintenance of lung function and normal lung development, (6) minimisation of side-effects of treatment, (7) provision of a written action plan, and (8) consideration of the patient's own goals with regard to treatment.

#### Special Considerations

**Distinguishing ICS Response from Natural Resolution:** In children with preschool wheeze, symptoms that resolve during ICS therapy may be due to either the natural history of the wheezing disorder or the effect of treatment. To distinguish between these possibilities, treatment should be withdrawn after initial improvement. Treatment should be restarted only if symptoms recur, thus identifying those with true asthma requiring ongoing controller therapy.

**Allergen-Specific IgE Testing:** Presence of allergen-specific IgE, determined through skin-prick testing or ImmunoCAP, may support an asthma diagnosis by indicating atopic sensitisation and helping identify specific environmental triggers for individual patients.

**Comprehensive Treatment Principles:** One of the key principles of asthma treatment is to choose an appropriate device and delivery system suited to the patient's age and capability. This ensures optimal medication deposition and efficacy while maintaining adherence through age-appropriate selection.

-

## Bleeding child

### Lecture Notes


#### Haemostasis: Primary and Secondary

Haemostasis is the body's mechanism to prevent excessive bleeding and consists of two integrated processes. **Primary hemostasis** involves vascular contraction and platelet plug formation at the site of injury. The blood vessels constrict, and platelets aggregate to create an initial barrier. **Secondary hemostasis** follows, in which coagulation factors respond in a complex cascade to form fibrin strands that strengthen the platelet plug, ultimately creating a stable fibrin clot. Together, these mechanisms ensure rapid and effective blood clotting when needed.

#### Components of the Hemostatic System

An abnormal hemostatic system can cause bleeding through defects in several key components: the vasculature itself, platelets (quantitative or qualitative defects), von Willebrand factor, and the coagulation pathway, which includes fibrinolysis. Understanding which component is affected is essential for diagnosis and management.

#### Diagnostic Framework for Bleeding Disorders

When evaluating a child with bleeding, several key diagnostic questions must be asked. First, is there a significant bleeding disorder, and if so, is it hereditary or acquired? Second, is the problem primarily related to coagulation or platelets? Third, is there a factor deficiency or an antibody-mediated process? These questions guide investigations and management.

Several clinical clues help differentiate between inherited and acquired bleeding disorders. Always consider hemophilia in a boy with bleeding, as this X-linked condition is common. If the child is ill with systemic symptoms, an acquired disorder is more likely than a hereditary one. Petechiae are rare in factor deficiency alone, suggesting a platelet or vascular problem instead. Multiple bleeding sites are commoner in acquired bleeding disorders, whereas inherited disorders typically present with single-site bleeds. Finally, if only a single coagulation test is abnormal, an inherited cause is more likely.

#### History and Physical Examination

A thorough assessment of a bleeding child requires careful history-taking covering age or neonatal status, sex, whether bleeding is from a single site or multiple sites, the type of bleeding (mucocutaneous versus soft tissue), whether the child appears well or ill, family history of bleeding disorders, current medications, and recent immunisations or viral infections. These details help establish whether the bleeding is acute or chronic, localised or systemic.

Physical examination should assess whether the child appears well or unwell, the distribution and character of bleeding (single versus multiple sites), the type of lesions (mucocutaneous, soft tissue, or joint involvement), and systemic signs including splenomegaly, organomegaly, lymphadenopathy, dysmorphism, or skeletal abnormalities. Physical findings often point to the underlying mechanism.

#### Recognising Platelet, Vascular, and Coagulation Disorders by Physical Signs

Different patterns of bleeding help identify the underlying defect. **Platelet disorders** present with petechiae (which may be wet or dry), superficial ecchymosis, and mucosal bleeding, reflecting quantitative or qualitative platelet dysfunction. **Vascular disorders** cause palpable purpura, which indicates vasculitis, scurvy, or hypersensitivity reactions. **Coagulation factor deficiencies** (particularly factors VIII and IX deficiency seen in Haemophilia A and B) present with haemarthrosis (bleeding into joints), deep ecchymosis, and sometimes mucosal bleeding. Conditions that affect both platelets and coagulation factors include disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC), leukaemia, and liver disease with portal hypertension (which causes splenomegaly).

#### Understanding Petechiae, Purpura, and Ecchymoses

Petechiae, purpura, and ecchymoses all result from extravasation of blood from the vasculature into the skin or mucous membranes, but they differ in size. **Petechiae** are red, nonblanching maculopapular lesions caused by intradermal capillary bleeding and measure less than 2 mm in greatest diameter (pinpoint hemorrhages). **Purpura** measures 2 mm to 1 cm in diameter. **Ecchymoses** are larger bruises measuring more than 1 cm. These size distinctions are important for accurate description and documentation.

#### Initial Investigations

The first-line investigations for a bleeding child are straightforward but essential: a full blood count (FBC) including differential and blood smear examination, INR/PT (measuring the extrinsic coagulation pathway), and APPT (measuring the intrinsic coagulation pathway). These tests provide rapid information about platelet numbers and basic coagulation function.

#### Thrombocytopenia: Definition and Platelet Physiology

Thrombocytopenia is defined as a platelet count less than 150 x 10⁹/L. Understanding normal platelet production helps explain thrombocytopenic states. Platelets develop from hematopoietic stem cells that differentiate into megakaryoblasts and then megakaryocytes in the bone marrow under the influence of thrombopoietin. Each megakaryocyte produces approximately 4000 platelets. Platelets have a half-life of 7-10 days in circulation.

#### Clinical Significance of Platelet Counts

The risk and severity of bleeding correlates with platelet count levels. A platelet count of less than 75 x 10⁹/L impairs primary hemostasis and is typically observed after major surgery or trauma. At counts less than 50 x 10⁹/L, spontaneous bleeding may occur, predominantly seen in the skin as petechiae and purpura. When the platelet count falls below 20 x 10⁹/L, noticeable hemorrhage appears in the skin and mucosa, including epistaxis and gingival bleeding. At counts less than 10 x 10⁹/L, life-threatening hemorrhage becomes possible, with risk of acute gastrointestinal hemorrhage and intracranial hemorrhage—serious CNS complications.

#### Mechanisms of Thrombocytopenia

Thrombocytopenia results from three main mechanisms: increased platelet destruction (either immune or nonimmune), decreased platelet production (either congenital or acquired), or sequestration of platelets away from the circulation.

#### Immune Causes of Increased Platelet Destruction

Immune-mediated platelet destruction includes several important causes. **Idiopathic thrombocytopenic purpura (ITP)** is the most common cause of thrombocytopenia in children. **Neonatal allo-immune thrombocytopenia (NAIT)** occurs when a neonate inherits maternal alloimmunisation. Neonatal autoimmune thrombocytopenia can occur when maternal IgG crosses the placenta. Autoimmune and collagen vascular disorders, certain viral infections (including HIV), and drug-induced reactions also cause immune thrombocytopenia.

#### Non-Immune Causes of Increased Platelet Destruction

Non-immune causes of platelet destruction include disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC), haemolytic-uraemic syndrome (HUS), thrombotic thrombocytopenic purpura (TTP), and sepsis. These conditions consume platelets through microangiopathic haemolysis or activation of the coagulation cascade.

#### Decreased Platelet Production

Several categories of conditions impair platelet production in the bone marrow. **Primary bone marrow malignancies** such as leukaemia directly replace normal megakaryocytes. **Bone marrow failure states** including aplastic anaemia (acquired or genetic, such as Fanconi's anaemia), bone marrow infiltration by neuroblastoma or storage disorders, infections (HIV, parvovirus, CMV, tuberculosis), exposure to cytotoxic drugs, and radiation all reduce megakaryocyte numbers. **Congenital causes of selective ineffective thrombopoiesis** include TAR syndrome, Wiskott-Aldrich syndrome (WAS), congenital amegakaryocytic thrombocytopenia (CAMT), and Bernard-Soulier syndrome. **Viral causes** of selective ineffective thrombopoiesis include EBV, CMV, and HIV.

#### Platelet Sequestration and Dilution

**Hypersplenism** causes sequestration of platelets in an enlarged spleen due to infection, vascular congestion, red cell disorders such as haemolytic anaemias, or infiltrative disorders. **Kasabach-Merritt syndrome** is caused by a massive haemangioma that sequesters platelets and causes thrombocytopenia. **Dilutional thrombocytopenia** results from recent massive blood transfusion, which dilutes the patient's platelet population.

#### Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura (ITP)

ITP is the most common cause of thrombocytopenia in children, with an annual incidence of 5 per 100,000 children. The typical presentation is a sudden onset of petechiae, bruising, or mucosal bleeding in a previously healthy child with otherwise unremarkable physical findings. The bleeding often follows a viral infection or recent immunisation. **Pathogenesis** involves formation of antiplatelet antibodies (autoantibodies) that bind to the platelet surface; destruction is enhanced by Fc-receptor-mediated phagocytosis in the spleen and liver. **Immunological abnormalities** including T-cell, B-cell, and cytokine abnormalities are noted in ITP, though the exact mechanism of immune dysfunction is not fully understood.

#### HIV Infection and Thrombocytopenia

HIV-positive patients are at significant risk for thrombocytopenia; approximately 30% of HIV-positive patients develop immune thrombocytopenic purpura. The causes of increased platelet loss or destruction in HIV patients include ITP (30%), thrombotic thrombocytopenic purpura (TTP), haemolytic uraemic syndrome (HUS), hypersplenism (secondary to infection or hemophagocytosis), and drugs such as interferon and saquinavir. Decreased platelet production can result from multiple drugs (co-trimoxazole, ganciclovir, fluconazole, rifabutin, clarithromycin, didanosine), infections (HIV itself, CMV, mycobacterium tuberculosis, mycobacterium avium complex, histoplasmosis), malignancies (non-Hodgkin's lymphoma, Hodgkin's disease), and nutritional deficiencies (vitamin B12 and folate).

**Management** of thrombocytopenia in HIV patients begins with initiation of highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART) and treatment of any underlying infection. Offending drugs should be removed. Caution must be exercised with steroid therapy, as steroids may aggravate underlying tuberculosis, viral or fungal infections, and the underlying immune deficiency.

#### Management of Immune Thrombocytopenia

Management of immune thrombocytopenia is stratified by platelet count and clinical symptoms. For a platelet count between 20–50 x 10⁹/L on an immune basis with no bleeding, **observation** is appropriate. For counts greater than 20 x 10⁹/L with no mucosal bleeding, **observe and educate** the parents about warning signs. For counts greater than 20 x 10⁹/L with mucosal bleeding, **treat** with either IVIG or steroids. For counts less than 20 x 10⁹/L or in the presence of mucosal bleeding, administer **IVIG at 1 gram/kg over 6 hours OR a short course of steroids; NO PLATELET TRANSFUSIONS** are given in immune thrombocytopenia, as transfused platelets will be rapidly destroyed.

#### Epistaxis: Recognition and First Aid

Epistaxis (nosebleed) is common; 60% of the population experience at least one nosebleed in their lifetime. Correct management is important. **Actions to avoid** include placing the head between the knees (which increases intracranial pressure), having the child lie down, stuffing gauze into the nose, allowing the child to blow the nose once bleeding has stopped (which dislodges the clot), and panicking. **Correct management** involves remaining calm and keeping the patient calm, having the child sit up, leaning the head forward to allow blood to drain rather than be swallowed, pinching the soft part of the nostril firmly, and pressing toward the face for **15 minutes**, repeating if necessary.

#### Neonatal Allo-Immune Thrombocytopenia (NAIT)

NAIT is thrombocytopenia in neonates born to mothers with normal platelet counts, analogous to Rh haemolytic disease of the newborn. The condition results from maternal allo-immunisation against fetal platelet antigens. Presentation occurs with profound thrombocytopenia either present at birth or developing within hours. Clinical manifestations include petechiae and bruising; visceral bleeding can also occur. The most serious complication is intracranial hemorrhage, which occurs in 8–22% of cases and can occur in utero. Fortunately, NAIT is self-limited, typically lasting 2–3 weeks before resolving spontaneously as maternal antibodies are cleared.

#### Inherited Coagulation Disorders

Inherited coagulation disorders include haemophilia A (factor VIII deficiency), haemophilia B (factor IX deficiency), von Willebrand disease, and deficiencies of factors II, V, VII, X, XI, XII, or XIII. Dys-, hypo-, or afibrinogenemia (disorders of fibrinogen quantity or quality) also occur. Other rare inherited coagulation disorders exist but are beyond the scope of common paediatric practice.

#### Acquired Coagulation Disorders

Acquired coagulation disorders are more common in clinical practice than inherited ones. Causes include vitamin K deficiency, liver disease (which impairs synthesis of multiple factors), disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC), massive transfusion syndrome (which dilutes clotting factors), dys- or hypo-fibrinogenemia, and disorders associated with malignancy. Other rare acquired coagulation disorders also occur.

#### Interpreting Coagulation Tests: PT/INR and APPT

Coagulation test results guide diagnosis and reflect the pathway involved. An **elevated PT/INR with normal APPT** indicates a defect in the extrinsic pathway and suggests vitamin K deficiency, liver disease, warfarin therapy, or factor VII deficiency. The **PT/INR tests the extrinsic pathway**. A **normal PT/INR with elevated APPT** indicates a defect in the intrinsic pathway, suggesting factor VIII deficiency (haemophilia A), factor IX deficiency (haemophilia B), factor XI or XII deficiency, or heparin therapy. The **APPT tests the intrinsic pathway**. **Both PT/INR and APPT elevated** indicates a defect in the common pathway (common to both extrinsic and intrinsic pathways) and suggests factor II, V, or X deficiency, vitamin K deficiency, liver disease, or disseminated intravascular coagulation. When **PT/INR and APPT are both normal with a normal platelet count**, another cause for the bleeding must be sought, such as platelet function abnormalities, vascular disorders, or factor XIII deficiency.

#### Vitamin K and Factor Activation

Vitamin K plays a crucial role in coagulation by catalysing the γ-carboxylation of factors II, VII, IX, and X, thereby activating these factors. Vitamin K deficiency thus impairs the extrinsic pathway and common pathway.

#### Haemorrhagic Disease of the Newborn (HDN)

Haemorrhagic disease of the newborn (also called vitamin K deficiency bleeding) is due to vitamin K deficiency in the newborn. It classically presents between day 2 and 3 after birth. Typical bleeding sites include the umbilicus, gastrointestinal tract, nose, skin, and sites of circumcision. The most severe complication is intracranial bleeding. **Prophylaxis** with vitamin K is recommended for all newborns prior to discharge home.

#### Bleeding Time and Platelet Function Disorders

Several hemostatic disorders may present with a normal coagulation screen (normal PT/INR and APPT) and normal full blood count, making diagnosis challenging. These include platelet function abnormalities (acquired causes such as aspirin use and uremia; inherited conditions such as Glanzmann thrombasthenia), mild von Willebrand disease, factor XIII deficiency, collagen disorders, vitamin C deficiency (scurvy), and mild inherited factor deficiencies. If hemostasis is abnormal despite normal screening tests, a **bleeding time test** should be performed and the patient should be **referred to a hematologist** for further evaluation.

#### Procedures and Medications to Avoid

In children with bleeding disorders, certain invasive procedures carry high risk and should be avoided: intramuscular injection (IMI), lumbar puncture (LP), surgical cut-down, tooth extractions, suturing (if there are alternative options), and venepunctures via the jugular or femoral veins. **Aspirin should be avoided**, as it impairs platelet function. These precautions prevent unnecessary bleeding complications.

#### Emergency Management of Bleeding

When a child presents with active bleeding, the first step is to **control the bleeding** using firm direct pressure and elevation of the affected limb. Once bleeding is controlled and investigations are underway, treatment depends on the suspected underlying cause. If a **coagulation defect is suspected**, administer fresh frozen plasma (FFP). If **thrombocytopenia is suspected**, administer platelet transfusions. The overarching management principle is **prompt replacement therapy** of the deficient component to restore haemostasis.

-

## COMA  COVULSION

### Lecture Notes


#### ETAT Protocol and Emergency Assessment

The ETAT (Emergency Triage Assessment and Treatment) protocol provides a systematic framework for assessing and managing children presenting with coma and convulsions. The emergency triage signs follow the A-B-C-D approach: A represents Airway assessment, B represents Breathing assessment, C encompasses Circulation status along with evaluation for Coma and Convulsion, and D assesses for severe Dehydration. This hierarchical approach ensures that life-threatening conditions are identified and addressed in priority order.

#### Consciousness Assessment Using the AVPU Scale

A critical skill in managing comatose children is the rapid assessment of consciousness using the AVPU scale. This simple but powerful tool classifies consciousness into four levels. A (Alert) indicates the child is fully awake and responsive. V (responding to Voice) indicates the child responds when spoken to. P (responding only to Pain) indicates the child responds only to painful stimuli. U (Unresponsive) describes a child who is unresponsive to both voice and pain. Importantly, coma is clinically defined as either P or U on the AVPU scale—that is, any child responding only to pain or who is completely unresponsive is considered to be in coma and requires immediate intervention.

#### Recognition of Convulsions and Clinical Signs

The clinical presentation of a convulsing child is characteristic and recognizable. The first sign that may indicate convulsions is a sudden loss of consciousness. This is typically accompanied by involuntary movements: uncontrolled jerky movements of the limbs and face, stiffening of the arms and legs, or twitching of the face, hands, and feet. Abnormal eye movements frequently accompany motor manifestations. A key respiratory sign is the occurrence of brief apnoeas (temporary cessation of breathing) during the seizure. Recognition of these signs is essential for rapid diagnosis and treatment initiation.

#### Airway and Breathing Management in Coma and Convulsions

The first step in treating any child with coma or active convulsions is to manage the airway and breathing. Proper positioning is fundamental: infants should be placed in the neutral position, while older children are positioned in the sniffing position to optimize airway patency. If trauma is suspected, the jaw thrust maneuver should be used to open the airway without flexing the cervical spine, protecting against potential spinal injury.

Gentle suctioning is performed if needed to clear secretions, but excessive or vigorous suctioning must be avoided as it can cause additional harm. Critically, nothing should ever be placed into the mouth of a convulsing child—not even a bite block or airway adjunct—as this risks aspiration and airway obstruction. Once the airway is secured and positioning optimized, supplemental oxygen should be administered via face mask to ensure adequate oxygenation.

#### Hypoglycaemia Screening and Treatment

A crucial part of the emergency assessment of any child with coma or convulsions is checking blood glucose. This is so important that the mnemonic D-E-F-G is used: Don't Ever Forget Glucose. Hypoglycaemia is defined as a blood glucose level below 3 mmol/L (equivalent to 55 mg/dL). Once IV access is established and blood samples are drawn for emergency investigations, the glucose level should be checked immediately using point-of-care testing such as Dextrostix.

If blood glucose is confirmed to be low (< 3 mmol/L or 55 mg/dL), or if Dextrostix testing is not available, IV glucose must be administered promptly. The treatment dose is 5 ml/kg of 10% glucose solution, given rapidly by intravenous injection. After seizure control is achieved, blood glucose must be rechecked to ensure that hypoglycaemia has not recurred, as recurrent hypoglycaemia can precipitate further seizures.

#### Seizure Complications and Status Epilepticus

Prolonged seizure activity carries serious consequences. Status epilepticus is defined as a seizure lasting more than 5 minutes, and it represents a medical emergency. Prolonged seizures cause significant complications: brain swelling (cerebral edema), brain damage from neuronal cell death, aspiration of vomitus with risk of choking and airway compromise, and ultimately death if not rapidly treated. The urgency of rapid seizure termination cannot be overstated, as neurological damage begins within minutes of seizure onset.

#### First-Line Anticonvulsant Therapy: Benzodiazepines

Once airway and breathing are secured and hypoglycaemia has been excluded or treated, the focus shifts to stopping the seizure using anticonvulsant medications. Benzodiazepines are the first-line class of drugs used for seizure control in acute convulsions. Lorazepam is the first-choice benzodiazepine for acute seizure management. If Lorazepam is unavailable, Midazolam is the second choice. If neither Lorazepam nor Midazolam is available, Diazepam is the third choice.

The preferred route for benzodiazepine administration is intravenous (IV), which provides the most rapid onset of action. However, when IV access cannot be immediately obtained, alternative routes are available: both Lorazepam and Midazolam can be administered via intramuscular (IMI) or buccal routes. Diazepam, if IV access is not available, is given by the rectal (PR) route.

A critical safety consideration applies to neonates (infants less than 4 weeks old): benzodiazepines should be avoided without concurrent ventilatory support because of the high risk of apnoea (respiratory depression) in this age group.

#### Second-Line Anticonvulsant Therapy

If seizures continue despite benzodiazepine administration, the next drug of choice is Phenobarbitone (phenobarbital). Phenobarbitone is also the preferred first-line anticonvulsant for neonates (children less than 4 weeks old) when benzodiazepines cannot be used due to ventilatory constraints.

If IV Phenobarbitone is not available, Phenytoin may be used as an alternative. However, Phenytoin has specific administration requirements: it must be given as an infusion over a minimum of 30 minutes. During Phenytoin infusion, cardiac monitoring is essential. If any cardiac complications occur—such as arrhythmia, bradycardia, or hypotension—the infusion must be stopped immediately. Once the child's cardiac status has stabilized, the infusion can be resumed at a slower rate, infusing over 1 hour instead of 30 minutes.

#### Post-Seizure Management and Aftercare

After a seizure has been controlled, comprehensive post-seizure assessment and management are essential. The post-seizure assessment begins by rechecking the A-B-C parameters: Airway patency, Breathing adequacy, and Circulation status. The child must be continuously monitored for any further seizure activity, as recurrent seizures are common in certain conditions.

The child should be placed in the recovery position (on the side) to prevent aspiration of any gastric contents. Blood glucose must be rechecked to ensure hypoglycaemia has not recurred. Emergency laboratory investigations that were drawn at the time of IV access should be reviewed to identify potential underlying causes, particularly infection. If infection is suspected or confirmed, appropriate antimicrobial therapy should be initiated—antibiotics for bacterial infections or antimalarials for malaria-related seizures. Ongoing monitoring and supportive care are continued based on the identified cause and the child's clinical response.

#### Learning Objectives Summary

The core competencies for managing coma and convulsions in children are: (1) the ability to assess consciousness accurately using the AVPU coma score, (2) the ability to identify the child in coma (P or U on AVPU), (3) the ability to recognize and identify a child having convulsions, and (4) the ability to provide appropriate emergency treatment for a child presenting with either coma or convulsions. Mastery of the ETAT protocol and the systematic approach outlined above ensures optimal outcomes for these critically ill children.

-

## Cyanosis — The Blue Child

Cyanosis is the bluish discolouration of the skin and mucous membranes that results when the concentration of deoxygenated haemoglobin becomes excessive in blood, or when there is an increased concentration of abnormal haemoglobin derivatives such as methaemoglobin or sulphaemoglobin in superficial blood vessels. Clinically recognizing and differentiating the cause of cyanosis in a child requires understanding the underlying pathophysiology, the five primary mechanisms by which it arises, and the anatomical distinction between central and peripheral presentations.

The five pathophysiological mechanisms of cyanosis are alveolar hypoventilation, diffusion impairment, ventilation-perfusion mismatch, right-to-left shunting (whether intracardiac or intrapulmonary), and haemoglobinopathy limiting oxygen transport. Central cyanosis arises from reduction in arterial oxygen saturation and appears as bluish discolouration in highly vascularized areas including the lips, tongue, and mucous membranes, typically with warm extremities and normal cardiac output. Peripheral cyanosis, by contrast, is confined to the distal extremities and results from increased oxygen extraction from sluggish blood movement through capillaries due to peripheral vasoconstriction or polycythaemia; arterial oxygen saturation in peripheral cyanosis remains normal.

The clinical distinction between central and peripheral cyanosis is critical for diagnosis. In central cyanosis, the tongue is involved and the affected areas remain warm, whereas in peripheral cyanosis the tongue is spared and the extremities are cold. Clubbing is usually present with central cyanosis but absent in peripheral cyanosis. The capillary refill time is less than two seconds in central cyanosis but exceeds two seconds in peripheral cyanosis. Central cyanosis may improve with oxygen supplementation if a pulmonary cause is present, while peripheral cyanosis shows no improvement with oxygen; conversely, warming improves peripheral cyanosis but not central cyanosis. The underlying mechanisms differ fundamentally: central cyanosis reflects reduction in blood oxygen saturation, while peripheral cyanosis reflects reduction in blood flow.

Central causes of cyanosis are remembered by the PALMS mnemonic. P stands for polycythaemia, in which an absolute increase in red blood cell mass increases the concentration of deoxygenated haemoglobin. A represents altitude, where the lower partial pressure of oxygen at high elevation reduces arterial saturation. L stands for lung disease, encompassing multiple pulmonary causes. M represents methaemoglobin and metabolic disorders including hypoglycaemia. S stands for shunt, including both intracardiac and intrapulmonary right-to-left shunting lesions.

Pulmonary causes of cyanosis are subdivided into three categories by mechanism. Alveolar hypoventilation occurs when the alveoli fail to clear carbon dioxide and oxygenate blood adequately, caused by central nervous system depression from asphyxia, sedation, or intracranial pathology such as head injury, raised intracranial pressure, or intracranial haemorrhage; neuromuscular disease including Guillain-Barré syndrome with its ascending paralysis and respiratory muscle weakness, or Duchenne's muscular dystrophy with progressive muscle degeneration affecting the respiratory muscles; and airway obstruction such as choanal stenosis causing congenital narrowing of the posterior nasal aperture in neonates. Ventilation-perfusion mismatch arises when ventilated alveoli are not perfused or perfused alveoli are not ventilated, caused by extrinsic compression of the lungs from pneumothorax with air in the pleural space causing lung collapse, pleural effusion with fluid compressing lung tissue, or thoracic dystrophies as skeletal abnormalities reducing thoracic volume; intrinsic compression occurs with pneumonia as inflammation of the lung parenchyma blocking airways, lung atelectasis with alveolar collapse from surfactant deficiency or airway obstruction, hyaline membrane disease as infant respiratory distress syndrome in preterm neonates with surfactant deficiency causing alveolar collapse, and asthma with airways inflammation and bronchoconstriction. Diffusion impairment occurs when the alveolar-capillary membrane is thickened, caused by pulmonary oedema with fluid accumulation in the lung parenchyma and alveolar space, and bronchopulmonary dysplasia as chronic lung disease in premature infants from mechanical ventilation and supplemental oxygen exposure causing pulmonary fibrosis.

Cardiac causes of cyanosis are classified by their haemodynamic consequence. Lesions causing decreased pulmonary blood flow include tetralogy of Fallot, a cyanotic congenital heart defect characterized by right ventricular outflow obstruction permitting right-to-left shunting and typically presenting with "Tet" spells; and tricuspid atresia, with complete absence of the tricuspid valve causing severe reduction in pulmonary blood flow. Lesions causing increased pulmonary blood flow with cyanosis through mixing of systemic and pulmonary venous return include transposition of the great vessels, in which the aorta arises from the right ventricle and the pulmonary artery from the left ventricle, causing complete mixing of deoxygenated and oxygenated blood; total anomalous pulmonary venous return, where all pulmonary veins drain anomalously rather than into the left atrium; and truncus arteriosus with a single arterial trunk arising from the heart to supply systemic, pulmonary, and coronary circulations. Lesions causing cyanosis through severe heart failure include coarctation of the aorta with narrowing of the descending aorta causing systemic hypertension and progressive heart failure; and hypoplastic left heart with severe underdevelopment of left-sided cardiac structures manifesting as cyanosis and cardiac failure from birth.

Peripheral causes of cyanosis are remembered by the COLD mnemonic. C stands for cold, where exposure to cold temperature causes peripheral vasoconstriction reducing blood flow to extremities. O represents obstruction, including arterial or venous obstruction reducing peripheral perfusion. L stands for left ventricular failure and shock, both of which cause reduced cardiac output and peripheral hypoperfusion. D represents decreased cardiac output from any cause, whether from cardiac dysfunction, hypovolaemia, or severe sepsis.

Abnormal haemoglobin derivatives present a special category of cyanosis not explained by reduced oxygen saturation. Methaemoglobinaemia may be congenital, inherited as a haemoglobinopathy in which abnormal haemoglobin derivatives cannot carry oxygen effectively, manifesting with cyanosis from birth; or toxic and acquired following exposure to oxidizing agents including nitrites, sulfonamides, and aniline dyes that convert normal haemoglobin to methaemoglobin. Sulphaemoglobinaemia is a rare condition arising from exposure to specific oxidizing toxins that convert haemoglobin into an abnormal sulphur-containing derivative incapable of carrying oxygen, causing cyanosis refractory to standard interventions.

The hyperoxia test (100% oxygen challenge) differentiates pulmonary from cardiac causes of cyanosis by measuring the arterial partial pressure of oxygen (PaO₂) after oxygen administration. A PaO₂ greater than 300 mmHg indicates normal cardiopulmonary function. A PaO₂ between 150 and 300 mmHg indicates pulmonary disorders (including hypoventilation, ventilation-perfusion mismatch, and diffusion impairment), central nervous system disorders, or methaemoglobinaemia. A PaO₂ between 100 and 150 mmHg indicates persistent pulmonary hypertension of the newborn (PPHN) or cardiac mixing lesions with increased pulmonary blood flow. A PaO₂ less than 100 mmHg indicates cardiac lesions with parallel circulation (such as transposition of the great vessels) or cardiac mixing lesions with restricted pulmonary blood flow (such as tetralogy of Fallot or tricuspid atresia), where minimal improvement occurs despite supplemental oxygen.

Arterial blood gas reference ranges provide essential baselines for interpreting blood samples in cyanotic children. Normal pH ranges from 7.35 to 7.45. Normal partial pressure of carbon dioxide (pCO₂) is 35 to 45 mmHg. Normal serum bicarbonate (HCO₃⁻) is 22.0 to 30.0 mmol/L. Normal base excess is −3.0 to 3.0 mmol/L. Normal partial pressure of oxygen (pO₂) is 75 to 100 mmHg. Normal oxygen saturation (SaO₂) is 95 to 100%. Electrolyte values must also be considered: serum potassium 3.5 to 5.5 mmol/L, serum sodium 135 to 145 mmol/L, serum chloride 95 to 110 mmol/L, ionized calcium 1.12 to 1.30 mmol/L, serum glucose 3.6 to 7.7 mmol/L, and serum lactate 0.2 to 1.8 mmol/L. Abnormalities in these parameters may contribute to cyanosis or its underlying causes.

Investigation of cyanosis begins with pulse oximetry to establish baseline oxygen saturation and trends. Haemoglobin concentration or haematocrit is measured as cyanosis becomes visible at higher haemoglobin levels but may be masked in anaemia. Arterial blood gas analysis determines the degree of hypoxia and identifies acid-base disturbances. The hyperoxia test as described above discriminates between pulmonary and cardiac causes. A septic screen including blood culture and inflammatory markers is performed in acutely unwell infants to exclude infection as an underlying cause. Electrocardiography and echocardiography are essential to evaluate cardiac structure and function, identifying congenital lesions or functional abnormalities. Haemoglobin electrophoresis is performed when haemoglobinopathy such as methaemoglobinaemia or sulphaemoglobinaemia is suspected based on clinical findings and failure to improve with oxygen.

The visibility of cyanosis depends on multiple factors beyond oxygen saturation. Cyanosis becomes more apparent when haemoglobin concentration is high; anaemic children may not appear cyanotic despite severe hypoxia because the absolute quantity of deoxygenated haemoglobin remains low. Cyanosis becomes more apparent at low levels of fetal haemoglobin saturation, with implications for distinguishing cyanosis in newborns. Cyanosis is less apparent in patients with darker skin pigmentation, requiring heightened clinical vigilance and reliance on objective measures such as pulse oximetry in non-Caucasian populations.

Treatment of cyanosis depends on identifying and addressing the underlying cause. All cyanotic children require optimization of oxygenation through supplemental oxygen or ventilatory support if needed. Symptomatic management includes positioning to optimize respiratory mechanics, maintaining normothermia, and avoiding unnecessary agitation. Definitive therapy targets the primary pathology, whether pulmonary (treating infection, recruiting collapsed alveoli, relieving obstruction), cardiac (urgent prostaglandin infusion to maintain ductal patency in certain lesions, surgical or catheter-based intervention), haematological (treatment of methaemoglobinaemia with methylene blue), or metabolic (correcting hypoglycaemia or other derangements).

-

## DEHYDRATION

### Lecture Notes


#### Definition & Initial Assessment

Dehydration in paediatric emergency care is a state of fluid loss from body tissues requiring assessment and fluid replacement in children with diarrhoea and other causes of fluid loss. The initial assessment approach screens systematically for shock versus non-shock dehydration, as this distinction fundamentally determines the urgency and type of intervention.
