The clinician must first assess for the presence of shock (abnormal Circulation, Coma, or Convulsions) before proceeding to classify the degree of dehydration in children presenting with diarrhoeal illness.

#### ETAT Assessment Sequence

The ETAT framework provides the structured assessment sequence for all paediatric emergency presentations. The Priority Signs 3TPR-MOB — Tiny baby, Temperature, Trauma, Pallor, Poisoning, Pain (severe), Respiratory, Restless, Referral (urgent), Malnutrition, Oedema, and Burns — guide rapid initial screening. Critically, severe dehydration assessment does not occur at this initial screening stage. Rather, assessment for severe dehydration happens only after completion of Circulation/Coma/Convulsions evaluation. This sequence ensures that life-threatening shock is identified and acted upon before the more detailed dehydration classification is undertaken.

#### Classification of Severe Dehydration

Diarrhoeal illness with fluid loss is classified into three categories: non-severe, severe, and shock. A child with diarrhoea is classified as having severe dehydration when two or more of the three key clinical signs are present, provided the child is not in shock. These three cardinal signs are: lethargy or altered consciousness, sunken eyes, and a skin pinch that goes back very slowly — taking longer than 2 seconds to return to normal. The presence of any one sign alone, or the absence of diarrhoea, does not establish the diagnosis. Additionally, the assessment must identify whether the child has severe malnutrition, defined by the presence of both visible severe wasting and bilateral pitting oedema of both feet. This distinction is crucial because severely malnourished children require fundamentally different fluid management strategies than well-nourished children.

#### Shock Assessment & Management

Shock in a child with severe dehydration presents with characteristic signs: cold hands, a capillary refill time of 3 seconds or more, and a weak or fast pulse. When shock is detected, fluid resuscitation becomes the immediate priority. The initial management steps are to stop any active bleeding, provide oxygen, keep the child warm, and obtain either peripheral IV or intraosseous (IO) access. At the moment of vascular access, collect DEFG samples — the mnemonic reminding clinicians to collect samples for Don't Ever Forget Glucose measurement plus septic workup cultures. The IV or IO cannula must be fixed securely and the extremity immobilised if applicable. IV antibiotics should be considered if sepsis is suspected. Once these initial steps are complete, proceed to fluid bolus administration.

During fluid bolus administration, continuous monitoring is essential. The clinician must check the pulse and breathing rate frequently and watch vigilantly for signs of congestive cardiac failure (CCF), which may be precipitated by rapid fluid administration. Hypoglycaemia must be actively managed: if the child's glucose is less than 3 mmol/L, administer 5 mL/kg of 10% dextrose intravenously immediately. If improvement is not evident or if the child deteriorates during shock management, reassess for ongoing shock, consider administering fluid faster, seek senior advice, check glucose levels, assess for and treat severe malnutrition if present, and consider antibiotics if sepsis is suspected.

#### Malnutrition Modifications

Children with severe malnutrition have precarious metabolic and circulatory status far more than well-nourished children, making their fluid and sodium handling distinctly different. Standard fluid rates can precipitate cardiac failure in this population. The fundamental modification is the rehydration rate: whereas a well-nourished child with severe dehydration (but no shock) receives 10 mL/kg/hr over 4 hours, a severely malnourished child receives the same volume per kilogram — 10 mL/kg/hr — but extended over 8 hours. This slower, lower-volume regimen gives the precarious cardiovascular system time to accommodate the fluid without triggering circulatory failure. Additionally, glucose status must be monitored and managed rigorously in malnourished children, as hypoglycaemia is a major risk in this vulnerable population. All severely malnourished children require active glucose assessment and management as part of their rehydration protocol.

#### Plan A: Oral Rehydration Therapy

Plan A attempts rehydration via the oral route, making use of oral rehydration solution (ORS). The dosing is 5 mL/kg administered via nasogastric tube or oral intake every 15 minutes. This regimen is continued for 2 hours, after which the child is reassessed for clinical improvement. If the child is improving after this 2-hour period, oral rehydration is continued for another 2 hours, extending the trial to 4 hours total before considering escalation to intravenous therapy. If the child vomits everything and shows no improvement after the initial 2 hours, Plan A has failed and the clinician should proceed to Plan B (intravenous fluids). The nasogastric tube is a valuable tool in this context, providing non-invasive fluid access when IV access is difficult or when a child cannot drink sufficient volumes orally. ORS is delivered as a continuous infusion or as 5 mL/kg every 15 minutes — never as a single 20 mL/kg bolus per hour, which overwhelms the GI tract and commonly triggers vomiting.

#### Plan B: IV Fluid Therapy

When oral rehydration with Plan A has failed — either because the child vomits everything or because no improvement occurs after 2 hours — intravenous fluid therapy is initiated. For a child with severe dehydration but no severe malnutrition, give IV dextrose-saline at 10 mL/kg/hr for 4 hours. The child must be reassessed every 1–2 hours during this infusion. As soon as the child is able to drink, begin offering oral ORS simultaneously; the intravenous rate is then progressively slowed while oral intake is gradually increased. This overlapping transition is safer than abruptly stopping IV fluids. Once dehydration has been fully corrected and the IV rate is low, proceed to the maintenance phase.

For a child without severe malnutrition who is not improving on the standard 10 mL/kg/hr regimen, rapid rehydration at 20 mL/kg/hr over 4 hours may be considered. This higher rate is used only in well-nourished children and is never appropriate for malnourished children. In contrast, a severely malnourished child receives 10 mL/kg/hr over 8 hours — the same infusion rate as for well-nourished children, but extended over twice the duration to allow safe fluid accommodation.

#### Plan C & Alternative Access Routes

When peripheral intravenous access cannot be established, two alternative routes are available. Intraosseous access allows infusion of dextrose-saline at 10 mL/kg/hr for 4 hours, using the same fluid and rate as peripheral IV. If intraosseous access is also unavailable or unsuccessful, nasogastric fluid administration becomes the next option. For NG delivery, give ORS at 20 mL/kg/hr for 6 hours. This is best delivered as a continuous infusion, which is preferred over intermittent bolus dosing for better tolerance. Importantly, the 20 mL/kg/hr dose must never be given as a single 20 mL/kg bolus per hour; divided dosing as a continuous drip prevents gastric overdistension and vomiting.

#### Fluid Rates & Weight Banding

Calculating fluid requirements in paediatric emergency care requires speed and accuracy at the bedside. The standard approach uses the child's actual weight in kilograms to determine millilitres per hour, but weight banding provides a practical shortcut that reduces calculation errors. Weight banding involves estimating the child's weight to the nearest practical band (e.g. 5 kg, 10 kg, 15 kg) and using that rounded figure to look up doses and infusion rates. This approach significantly decreases the likelihood of tenfold errors when calculations are performed rapidly during an emergency.

Concrete examples illustrate how weight banding works in practice. A child weighing 7.3 kg is placed in the 5–10 kg weight band; the clinician uses 10 kg (or sometimes 8 kg as an intermediate) for calculations. A child weighing 3.1 kg falls in the infant band and calculations are based on 3–5 kg. In each case, the weight band is used consistently throughout the fluid calculation to derive the hourly rate, and the same weight band is used to double-check drip rates.

#### Drip Rate Calculations

Converting millilitres per hour to drops per minute requires knowing the calibration of the IV giving set in use. Most hospital drip sets are either 60 drops/mL or 15 drops/mL. Understanding the relationship between volume and drop rate is essential for safe manual infusion control.

For a 60 drops/mL dropper: volume per hour in millilitres equals drops per minute. An infusion of 100 mL/hr runs at exactly 100 drops per minute. An infusion of 146 mL/hr runs at 146 drops per minute.

For a 15 drops/mL dropper: divide the volume in millilitres per hour by 4. An infusion of 100 mL/hr runs at 100 ÷ 4 = 25 drops per minute. An infusion of 146 mL/hr runs at 146 ÷ 4 ≈ 36.5 drops per minute.

Practical case examples demonstrate application. A 7.3 kg child (weight band 10 kg) requiring Plan A oral rehydration receives ORS 5 mL/kg per 15 minutes: 5 × 7.3 ≈ 36.5 mL, rounded to 40 mL per 15 minutes using the weight band. If this child later requires Plan B IV therapy at 20 mL/kg/hr for 4 hours: 20 × 7.3 = 146 mL/hr (weight band 160 mL/hr). Using a 60 drops/mL dropper, set the rate at 146–160 drops per minute. Using a 15 drops/mL dropper, set the rate at 34–40 drops per minute.

An 11 kg child with severe dehydration and severe malnutrition receives Plan B at the slower malnutrition rate: 10 mL/kg/hr × 8 hours = 110 mL/hr (weight band 80 mL/hr). Using a 60 drops/mL dropper, set at 80 drops per minute. Using a 15 drops/mL dropper, set at 20 drops per minute. The duration is 8 hours, not 4 hours, due to malnutrition.

A 3.1 kg infant (weight band 3–5 kg) requiring rapid infusion at 20 mL/kg/hr for 4 hours: 20 × 3.1 = 62 mL/hr (weight band 60 mL/hr). Using a 60 drops/mL dropper, set at 60–62 drops per minute. Using a 15 drops/mL dropper, set at 15 drops per minute. A critical safety consideration applies to tiny infants: ensure that no more than 100 mL can run through suddenly, as rapid over-infusion is catastrophic in this population.

A 10 kg child who cannot tolerate IV access and requires nasogastric infusion receives ORS at 20 mL/kg/hr for 6 hours: 20 × 10 = 200 mL/hr (weight band 240 mL/hr). Using a 60 drops/mL dropper, set at 200–240 drops per minute. Using a 15 drops/mL dropper, set at 50–60 drops per minute.

#### Equipment Safety & Manual Infusion

Manual infusion controllers and roller clamps present significant safety hazards that must be understood and mitigated. Roller controllers, once adjusted to a desired rate, tend to slow progressively over time due to friction changes and fluid viscosity variations. Dial-flow systems are affected by the height of the drip bag above the infusion site: a higher bag creates greater hydrostatic pressure, driving fluid faster than the dial setting indicates; a lower bag reduces pressure and slows flow below the dial setting. The position of the limb and the degree of vascular resistance at the cannula site further alter the actual delivered rate; occlusion or increased resistance slows flow, while movement or relaxation may speed it up.

These variables make manual control unreliable. A clinician cannot reliably set a roller controller and walk away — the rate will change unpredictably. Manual systems provide no audible or visual alarm if the flow goes too fast or too slow; the clinician must maintain constant bedside vigilance. Most importantly, if a roller clamp becomes dislodged or slips, catastrophic infusion of a sudden large volume can occur, particularly dangerous in infants. The solution is twofold: (1) use electronic infusion pumps wherever possible, as they deliver the set rate regardless of drip height, limb position, or vascular resistance, preventing overdose; and (2) if manual control must be used, continuously observe the drip chamber, protect the roller or dial from accidental displacement, and be prepared to intervene immediately if unexpected changes occur.

#### Hypoglycaemia Management

Hypoglycaemia is a serious and common complication in dehydrated children, particularly those with severe malnutrition. During shock management or fluid bolus administration, if the child's capillary glucose is less than 3 mmol/L, immediate treatment is required. Administer 10% dextrose at 5 mL/kg intravenously. This is the specific concentration and dose — not other dextrose concentrations, and not higher volumes. The glucose check should be repeated after 10–15 minutes to confirm resolution of hypoglycaemia.

#### Monitoring During Rehydration

Throughout rehydration, continuous clinical monitoring ensures safety and detects complications early. While fluid bolus is being administered (in shock cases), measure pulse and breathing rate frequently — at least every 15–30 minutes. Observe vigilantly for signs of congestive cardiac failure, particularly in children receiving rapid fluid infusion or in those with severe malnutrition. The appearance of respiratory distress, rales on auscultation, hepatomegaly, or peripheral oedema signals CCF and warrants slower infusion rates and possible diuretic therapy.

Reassess the child's clinical status every 1–2 hours during IV rehydration. Look for improvement in lethargy, restoration of sunken eyes, and normalisation of skin turgor. If improvement is occurring as expected, continue the planned regimen. If the child is not improving or is deteriorating, stop and reassess: check for persistent or recurrent shock, measure glucose, verify the IV rate is running as prescribed, assess weight change, and consider antibiotics if sepsis is suspected. When improvement is evident and the child begins to tolerate oral intake, begin transitioning to the next phase.

#### Transition from IV to Oral

The transition from intravenous to oral fluids should not be abrupt. As the child's dehydration improves and oral intake becomes tolerated, gradually slow the IV infusion rate while simultaneously increasing oral ORS intake. This overlapping approach allows continuous hydration support while the child adapts to full oral intake. Once the child is drinking well and maintaining hydration, the IV rate can be discontinued and oral maintenance fluids become the sole source of fluid replacement.

#### Maintenance Phase & Observation

Once severe dehydration has been fully corrected and the child is stable on low-rate IV fluids (or if oral rehydration succeeds), proceed to the maintenance phase. At this stage, give preventive ORS at 10 mL/kg orally after each stool. This is not a fixed schedule but rather a stool-based regimen: each time the child passes stool, offer 10 mL/kg of ORS to replace ongoing losses and prevent recurrent dehydration. The child must be observed for a minimum of 6 hours post-hydration to ensure that dehydration is maintained at resolution and that no complications have developed. During this observation period, continue monitoring vital signs, reassess clinical signs of hydration, and ensure adequate oral intake. Once the child has been stable for the full observation period with maintained hydration and no complications, discharge planning with family education about continued ORS use, feeding, and return precautions can begin.

-

## Emergency care for children- the seriously ill child

### Lecture Notes


#### Introduction: The Framework for Emergency Care

Emergency care for seriously ill children rests on four fundamental components. First is **triage** — the process of finding which children urgently need care. Second is **provision of emergency care** through resuscitation and life support. Third comes **post-resuscitation care**, ensuring the child remains stable. Finally comes **stabilisation and transport** to appropriate definitive care. These four pillars work together to save lives.

Triage, in particular, is not a diagnosis. Rather, it is a rapid sorting process aimed at identifying life-threatening problems requiring immediate intervention. Triage must be quick, focusing on finding danger rather than determining what disease caused that danger. Importantly, **all staff can perform triage** — nurses, doctors, clerks, gatekeepers, and cleaners. Triage should occur as soon as the child arrives, **before reception or administration**, whether in the outpatients queue, emergency room, ward, or emergency treatment area (a designated space with access to drugs and supplies).

The WHO developed the ETAT system to standardize this approach: E = Emergency, T = Triage, A = Assessment, T = Treatment.

#### The ABCC-C-D Framework: Identifying Life-Threatening Problems

The ABCC-C-D framework is the cornerstone of emergency assessment, identifying six critical life-threatening problems:

- **A = Airway** — Is the airway open and clear?
- **B = Breathing** — Is the child breathing effectively?
- **C = Circulation** — Are there signs of adequate perfusion?
- **C = Coma** — What is the consciousness level?
- **C = Convulsion** — Is the child fitting?
- **D = Dehydration** — What is the fluid status?

If ANY sign of ABCC-C-D is present, emergency treatment must start immediately without delay. The presence of any of these problems mandates movement to the emergency treatment area for urgent intervention.

#### Understanding the Six Final Pathways to Death

Most children die through one of six final pathways, understanding which helps guide prevention and management:

1. **Respiratory obstruction** — Examples include croup, asthma, and bronchiolitis. The airway becomes partially or completely blocked, preventing air entry.

2. **Respiratory depression** — The child's breathing effort becomes inadequate, seen in post-ictal states, meningitis, or poisoning. The brain fails to drive adequate breathing.

3. **Alveolar or chest wall failure** — The lungs cannot expand or exchange gas properly. Causes include pulmonary contusion, flail chest, and traumatic or congenital diaphragmatic hernia.

4. **Fluid loss** — Severe dehydration from conditions like gastroenteritis or major trauma (femoral or pelvic fractures) leads to circulatory collapse.

5. **Fluid maldistribution** — Shock occurs not from absolute fluid loss but from improper distribution within blood vessels. Meningococcal shock and anaphylaxis exemplify this mechanism.

6. **Heart failure** — The heart fails as a pump. Causes include local anaesthetic toxicity and congenital heart disease.

#### Assessment of Airway and Breathing

To assess whether a child's airway is patent, ask these key questions: Is the child breathing? Is there noisy breathing (secretions, stridor, snoring, grunting)? Is the child blue?

**Signs of severe respiratory distress** include fast breathing (fast for age), severe chest-indrawing (see below), grunting, difficulty feeding, and visible fatigue. These signal that the child is working too hard to breathe and cannot sustain this effort indefinitely.

Three clinical signs warrant special attention:

**Stridor** is a high-pitched, musical breathing sound heard without a stethoscope, typically during inspiration. It indicates partial upper airway obstruction from conditions like croup, epiglottitis, or foreign body aspiration.

**Grunting** is a groaning sound at the end of expiration. It represents the child's attempt to maintain positive end-expiratory pressure to keep alveoli open — a sign of severe respiratory effort and hypoxaemia.

**Chest wall indrawing** (also called intercostal, substernal, or suprasternal indrawing) occurs when the chest wall draws inward during inspiration. This indicates increased work of breathing due to airway obstruction or reduced lung compliance, showing the child is struggling to move air.

#### Assessment of Circulation

To assess circulation, check whether the child has **warm hands**. If not, measure **capillary refill time** by pressing on a fingernail or toe and counting how long colour takes to return. A capillary refill greater than 2 seconds indicates poor circulation and shock. Also assess whether the pulse is weak and fast.

**Compensated shock** presents with cold hands, cold feet, capillary refill greater than 2 seconds, weak pulses, and pale skin — but the child remains alert and vital organs still receive adequate perfusion. This is the body's attempt to preserve core organ function.

**Decompensated shock** shows all signs of compensated shock plus altered consciousness (responds only to Pain or is Unresponsive on the AVPU scale), severe hypotension, absent pulses, severe bradycardia, or cardiac arrest. The body's compensatory mechanisms have failed.

Vital sign changes signalling movement into shock include cold hands, capillary refill greater than 2 seconds, weak or absent pulses, and altered consciousness. Emergency conditions presenting as shock include septic shock (from infection or meningitis), hypovolaemic shock (from diarrhoea or bleeding), cardiogenic shock (from heart failure), anaphylactic shock, and conditions with fluid maldistribution.

#### Assessment of Consciousness and Convulsions

The **AVPU scale** rapidly assesses consciousness:
- **A = Alert** — Eyes open, aware
- **V = Responds to Voice** — Opens eyes or responds to verbal commands
- **P = Responds to Pain** — Opens eyes or moves only to painful stimuli
- **U = Unresponsive** — No response to any stimulus

When a child responds only to Pain or is Unresponsive, this constitutes **coma** — a life-threatening emergency.

**Floppiness** (decreased muscle tone) in a seriously ill child is a grave sign indicating severe systemic illness, shock, severe infection, or neurological compromise. A floppy child requires emergency intervention.

**Convulsion detection** varies by age. Generalised convulsions (whole body shaking) are easy to see. Subtle signs, especially in neonates, may include eye flickering or deviation, hand or foot movements, mouth movements, repetitive movements, or apnoea. Active convulsion at the time of presentation is an emergency requiring immediate airway assessment and seizure management.

#### Assessment of Dehydration

To assess severe dehydration in a child with diarrhoea, ask: Is the child lethargic or unconscious? Does the child have sunken eyes? Does the skin pinch go back very slowly?

The **skin pinch test** involves pinching skin (typically on the arm) and observing how long it takes to return to normal. If it takes greater than 2 seconds to return, this indicates moderate to severe dehydration. A normal skin pinch returns immediately.

Clinical findings of severe dehydration include:
- Lethargy or unconsciousness
- Sunken eyes
- Skin pinch that takes greater than 2 seconds to return
- Weak or absent pulses
- Cold hands

These signs reflect the child's profound fluid loss and circulatory compromise.

#### Priority Signs: The 3TPR-MOB Mnemonic

Beyond the life-threatening signs of ABCC-C-D, children also need priority (early but not emergency) assessment if they display any of the 3TPR-MOB signs:

**3T = Tiny baby, Temperature, Trauma**
- **Tiny baby** — Any child less than 3 months of age presenting to emergency care needs priority assessment due to their special vulnerability and higher risk of serious infection.
- **Temperature** — A child who is hot to touch (temperature >38 degrees Celsius) needs priority assessment.
- **Trauma** — Any child with significant trauma warrants priority placement.

**P = Pallor, Poisoning, Pain (severe)**
- **Pallor** — Severe pallor indicates severe anaemia. Assess by comparing the child's palm with the mother's palm; if the child's palm is much paler, this is a priority sign.
- **Poisoning** — Any suspected poisoning requires priority assessment.
- **Pain (severe)** — Severe pain warrants priority care.

**R = Respiratory (NOT severe), Restless, Referral**
- **Respiratory** — Not severe respiratory distress (which would be emergency), but concerning respiratory findings requiring clinical judgment and experience.
- **Restless** — Lethargy or irritability; responding to voice only (V on AVPU); continuously irritable, crying, and inconsolable — these indicate altered consciousness or behaviour.
- **Referral** — Children requiring urgent referral to a higher facility.

**MOB = Malnutrition, Oedema, Burns**
- **Malnutrition** — Look rapidly at the child's arms, legs, and chest. **Marasmus** is severe wasting where the child appears very thin with minimal subcutaneous fat. This is a priority sign.
- **Oedema** — Bilateral foot oedema, with or without hair and skin changes suggesting **Kwashiorkor** (protein malnutrition), is a priority sign. Bilateral foot oedema can signal heart failure, kidney problems (nephrotic syndrome), liver dysfunction, or nutritional deficiencies.
- **Burns** — Any significant burn injury requires priority assessment.

#### The EPQ Triage Action System

Once assessment is complete, triage decisions direct the child's pathway:

**E = Emergency** — Take the child immediately to the emergency treatment area.

**P = Priority** — Take the child to the front of the queue.

**Q = Queue** — No emergency or priority signs are present. The child is non-urgent and can wait their turn.

The key distinction between E and P: Emergency children have signs of ABCC-C-D or other life-threatening conditions requiring immediate treatment. Priority children have concerning signs (3TPR-MOB) requiring early assessment but not immediate emergency intervention.

#### Clinical Scenarios: Applying the Framework

**Scenario 1** (3-year old with diarrhoea): Airway and breathing normal. Hot but cold hands. Capillary refill 1.5 seconds. Alert. Skin pinch takes 3 seconds. Assessment: Cold hands, prolonged capillary refill, and slow skin turgor are signs of moderate dehydration. Triage: **P (Priority)** — requires early assessment but not emergency-level shock.

**Scenario 2** (4-year old, recent seizure): Convulsed 1 hour ago. Fast breathing, no cyanosis, no respiratory distress. Very hot, responds quickly to questions. No diarrhoea or vomiting. Assessment: Recent seizure with fever suggests possible febrile convulsion, a priority sign. Maintained alertness and normal breathing prevent emergency classification. Triage: **P (Priority)**.

**Scenario 3** (1-year old, recurrent seizures): Seizures at home and outside clinic, now unconscious. Breathing wet and noisy, drooling. Looking blue. Assessment: Coma (unresponsive), airway obstruction signs (wet breathing, drooling), cyanosis, and recurrent convulsions — multiple emergency signs. Triage: **E (Emergency)** — immediate emergency treatment needed.

**Scenario 4** (2-year old, actively convulsing): Actively convulsing at presentation. Assessment: Active convulsion is an emergency sign requiring immediate airway assessment and seizure management. Triage: **E (Emergency)**.

**Scenario 5** (9-month girl, choking): Choking on a bead necklace, brought by nurse. Assessment: Choking represents acute airway obstruction. Triage: **E (Emergency)** — immediate airway intervention required.

**Scenario 6** (3-year old boy, 14kg, severe respiratory distress): Pale, floppy, difficulty breathing. 3-day cough. Fast breathing, heavy severe chest indrawing. Airway patent. Alert. Assessment: Severe chest indrawing, difficulty breathing, pallor, and floppiness all indicate severe respiratory distress. Triage: **E (Emergency)**.

**Scenario 7** (4-month baby, 5kg, shock): Fever, rapid breathing, refusing feeds, 2 episodes vomiting and watery diarrhoea. Cold hands. Capillary refill 6 seconds. Femoral pulse fast and weak. No chest indrawing. Assessment: Cold hands, capillary refill greater than 2 seconds, weak fast pulse, and significant fluid loss from diarrhoea/vomiting indicate severe dehydration and circulatory shock. Triage: **E (Emergency)** — immediate resuscitation needed.

**Scenario 8** (18-month boy, 11kg, fever and abdominal pain): Unwell 2 days, feverish. Abdominal pain, fast breathing. Airway fine, no chest indrawing. No diarrhoea history. Assessment: No emergency signs (ABCC-C-D absent) and no priority signs. Fever present but child is alert and breathing is fast without distress. Triage: **Q (Queue)** — non-urgent.

**Scenario 9** (10-week baby, 3.5kg, fever): Hot, crying a lot, won't suckle. Airway, breathing, circulation normal. No diarrhoea or dehydration. Assessment: Tiny baby (<3 months) with fever is a priority sign. Triage: **P (Priority)**.

**Scenario 10** (14-month girl, altered consciousness and distress): Sleeping all day. Irritable when awoken, rouses to loud voice or arm shaking. Warm hands but pale. No diarrhoea or vomiting. Deep breathing, lower chest wall indrawing. Assessment: Responds to voice only (V on AVPU), indicating altered consciousness. Chest wall indrawing indicates respiratory distress. Both are priority signs. Triage: **P (Priority)**.

**Scenario 11** (1-year girl, 6.5kg, malnutrition and dehydration): 2-day history diarrhoea and vomiting. Restless and irritable. Airway and breathing normal. Warm hands. Responds to voice. Skin pinch 4 seconds. Sunken eyes. Skin and bones appearance. Assessment: Severe malnutrition (skin and bones) and moderate-to-severe dehydration (sunken eyes, slow skin pinch) are priority signs. Triage: **P (Priority)**.

**Scenario 12** (4-year boy, sore ear): Well-appearing and playful. Assessment: No emergency or priority signs. Triage: **Q (Queue)** — non-urgent.

**Scenario 13** (6-year boy, chest pain): Breathing normally, not shocked, conversant. Assessment: Normal breathing, normal circulation, maintained alertness. No emergency or priority signs. Triage: **Q (Queue)** — non-urgent.

**Scenario 14** (7-week boy, fever): Fever 1 day. Looking around, breathing normally, no shock or dehydration. Assessment: Tiny baby (<3 months) with fever. Triage: **P (Priority)**.

**Scenario 15** (7-year girl, mild asthma): Mild wheeze, bright and alert. Assessment: No respiratory distress, mild symptoms, maintained alertness. No emergency or priority signs. Triage: **Q (Queue)** — non-urgent.

**Scenario 16** (18-month girl, croup): Cough, runny nose, barking cough, stridor, agitated. Assessment: Stridor indicates airway obstruction, an emergency sign. Triage: **E (Emergency)** — emergency airway assessment required.

**Scenario 17** (2-year girl, refusing to walk): Won't walk 1 day. Crying but consolable, breathing normally, not shocked. Assessment: Normal breathing, normal circulation, consolability (not continuously irritable). No emergency or priority signs. Triage: **Q (Queue)** — non-urgent.

#### Systematic Triage Framework

The practical triage workflow follows these steps:
1. Look for Emergency signs (ABCC-C-D)
2. Move to ER and treat any signs found
3. Look for Priority signs (3TPR-MOB)
4. Place priority patients at the front of the queue
5. Move on to the next patient

This systematic approach ensures no child is missed while maintaining efficiency.

#### Emergency Treatment and Resuscitation

When a child is triaged as Emergency, rapid treatment must begin. The essential actions immediately after identifying an emergency child are:

1. **Secure the airway** — Open the airway, position the child appropriately, and clear secretions.
2. **Provide oxygen and ventilation** — Deliver high-flow oxygen; use bag-mask ventilation if needed.
3. **Establish IV access and begin fluid resuscitation** — Insert IV lines and start appropriate fluids based on the clinical problem.
4. **Monitor continuously** — Use pulse oximetry, blood pressure, heart rate, and other vital signs monitoring.
5. **Initiate specific emergency care** — Administer medications (such as adrenaline for anaphylaxis, glucose for hypoglycaemia, antibiotics for sepsis).
6. **Arrange transport to higher-level care** — Stabilise and transport to a secondary or tertiary hospital for definitive care.

The emergency treatment area must be equipped and staffed immediately. Essential equipment and supplies include:
- Oxygen delivery equipment
- Airway management equipment (oral airways, bag-mask ventilation devices)
- IV catheters and resuscitation fluids
- Medications for resuscitation (adrenaline, glucose, antibiotics)
- Monitoring equipment (pulse oximeter, blood pressure cuff)
- Emergency drugs appropriate for paediatric doses

#### Clinical Considerations in Dehydration and Malnutrition

**Severe dehydration** requires recognition of both loss and compensation signs. The skin pinch test remains a bedside clinical tool: pinch skin on the arm and observe the return time. A normal skin pinch returns immediately; greater than 2 seconds indicates moderate to severe dehydration. This correlates with up to 10% of body weight loss in children.

**Marasmus** (wasting) and **Kwashiorkor** (protein malnutrition with oedema) represent opposite presentations of severe malnutrition. Marasmus affects children with overall caloric deficiency and appears as severe thinness. Kwashiorkor affects children with protein deficiency and appears with bilateral foot oedema, moon facies, and hair changes. Both warrant priority assessment, though neither alone indicates emergency-level shock if circulation and airway are intact.

#### Differential Diagnosis in Emergency Presentations

**Severe respiratory distress** may result from pneumonia, bronchiolitis, asthma, croup, epiglottitis, foreign body aspiration, pulmonary oedema (from heart failure), pneumothorax, or any condition causing airway obstruction or reduced lung compliance.

**Shock presentations** arise from septic shock (infection or meningitis), hypovolaemic shock (diarrhoea or bleeding), cardiogenic shock (heart failure), anaphylactic shock, or conditions with fluid maldistribution.

#### Stabilisation and Transport

Once emergency treatment has begun, rapid stabilisation and transport to definitive care (secondary or tertiary hospital) is critical. Delays in transport lead to worse outcomes in seriously ill children. The goal is to initiate life-saving treatment and then transfer the child to a facility capable of definitive care without further delay.

#### Key Teaching

The best time to start learning about emergency care for children is ten years ago. The next best time is now. Every clinician working with children should master the ABCC-C-D framework, the 3TPR-MOB priority signs, and the systematic triage approach. Lives depend on rapid recognition and appropriate action.

-

## EMERGENCY CARE FOR CHILDREN

### Lecture Notes


#### Overview of Emergency Care

Emergency care for children comprises four main components working in sequence: first, triage to find the children who need emergency care; second, provision of emergency care through resuscitation and life support; third, provision of post-resuscitation care; and fourth, stabilization and transport. Triage is not a diagnostic process but rather a critical sorting mechanism designed to quickly identify life-threatening problems and prioritize which children need immediate intervention. The goal of triage is to be quick and accurate in finding life-threatening emergencies.

Child mortality in South Africa remains a significant public health concern. Current estimates show high variation depending on the source: the United Nations estimates 69 per 1000 births, the South African Actuarial Society estimates 76 per 1000 births, while the Millennium Development Goal (MDG) 4 target aims for 20 per 1000 live births. In Area 2 data spanning 2005 to the present, almost one third of children die within the first 24 hours of admission (total deaths recorded: 4564), underscoring the importance of rapid identification and treatment of critical conditions in the emergency setting.

#### Triage Systems: ETAT and 3TPR-MOB

The ETAT system provides a structured framework for emergency triage, with each letter representing a distinct phase: E for Emergency, T for Triage, A for Assessment, and T for Treatment. This systematic approach ensures that emergency identification, sorting, evaluation, and intervention occur in a logical sequence.

The 3TPR-MOB system is a complementary mnemonic that helps identify priority signs during triage. The "3T" group includes Tiny baby (defined as less than 3 months old), Temperature (greater than 38 degrees Celsius, assessed as "hot to touch"), and Trauma. The "3P" group covers Pallor (severe pallor assessed by comparing the child's palm with the mother's palm, indicating severe anaemia), Poisoning, and Pain (severe). The "3R" group captures Respiratory distress (assessed by clinical judgment and experience), Restlessness (the child is continuously irritable, crying, and inconsolable—corresponding to V on the AVPU scale), and Referral (urgent). Finally, MOB identifies Malnutrition, Oedema, and Burns as additional priority concerns.

#### Assessment Frameworks: ABCC-C-D and AVPU

The ABCC-C-D assessment system provides a comprehensive checklist for identifying emergency conditions in pediatric patients. It evaluates:
- **A (Airway)**: Patency and protection of the airway
- **B (Breathing)**: Respiratory effort and adequacy
- **C (Circulation)**: Perfusion status and cardiac output
- **C (Coma)**: Level of consciousness
- **C (Convulsion)**: Presence of seizure activity
- **D (Dehydration)**: Fluid status and signs of hypovolemia

The AVPU scale assesses consciousness level with four categories: A (Alert), V (responds to Voice), P (responds to Pain), and U (Unresponsive). Coma is defined as a state where the child responds only to pain (P) or is unresponsive (U). Recognition of these levels is critical for determining urgency of intervention.

#### Vital Signs and Clinical Red Flags

Capillary refill time is a key indicator of peripheral perfusion and shock status. Normal capillary refill is less than 2 seconds; capillary refill greater than 2 seconds indicates poor circulation and shock. Additional signs of poor circulation include cold hands, weak and fast pulse, and the combination of these signs suggests significant hemodynamic compromise.

Temperature greater than 38 degrees Celsius is considered a priority sign and warrants close observation for serious infection, particularly in very young infants. Severe respiratory distress is characterized by fast breathing, severe chest-indrawing, grunting, difficulty feeding, and signs of tiredness—features that demand immediate emergency intervention. Airway obstruction presents with distinctive signs including stridor, snoring, grunting, or noisy breathing with secretions, any of which requires urgent airway management.

Severe dehydration is identified by three cardinal signs: lethargy or unconsciousness, sunken eyes, and skin pinch that goes back very slowly (skin turgor loss). These signs indicate significant fluid loss and potential shock. Any child with ANY sign of ABCC-C-D abnormality should immediately receive emergency treatment without delay.

#### Priority Signs and Triage Categories

Triage personnel must distinguish between emergency signs and priority signs, as this distinction determines urgency of care and queue placement. Emergency signs represent immediate life threats; priority signs indicate high-risk conditions that require close monitoring and prompt evaluation but may not require immediate resuscitation.

The triage categories themselves are simple but critical: **E (Emergency)** means the child must be taken immediately to the emergency treatment area for resuscitation and life support; **P (Priority)** means the child should be placed at the front of the queue for rapid evaluation; and **Q (Queue)** means the child has no emergency or priority signs and can wait their turn for routine care.

Assessment of the respiratory priority sign requires clinical judgment and experience—it identifies children with concerning respiratory patterns but not the severe chest-indrawing that would constitute an emergency sign. The restlessness priority sign (assessed on AVPU as V—responds to Voice) represents a child who is continuously irritable, crying, and inconsolable, a behavioral indicator of significant systemic illness.

Severe pallor is assessed by direct comparison of the child's palm with the mother's palm; if the child's palm is markedly paler, this suggests severe anaemia warranting priority assessment. Similarly, trauma in any form should elevate a child to priority triage even if other signs are absent. Urgent referral from another facility should always result in priority triage classification.

#### Final Pathways to Death

Understanding the mechanisms by which children die in emergency settings helps guide assessment and intervention. Six major final pathways to death are recognized:

**Respiratory Obstruction**: Caused by anatomical blockage of the airway, common examples include croup, asthma, and bronchiolitis. These conditions prevent air from reaching the lungs and require urgent airway management.

**Respiratory Depression**: Occurs when the brain fails to drive adequate breathing efforts, seen in post-ictal state (immediately after seizures), meningitis, and poisoning. These children may have patent airways but insufficient ventilation.

**Alveolar or Chest Wall Failure**: Occurs when the lungs themselves or the chest wall cannot expand adequately to enable gas exchange. Examples include pulmonary contusion, flail chest, and diaphragmatic hernia (either traumatic or congenital).

**Fluid Loss**: Results from hypovolemia due to loss of intravascular volume, commonly from gastroenteritis with severe diarrhea or vomiting, and from internal hemorrhage associated with femoral or pelvic fractures.

**Fluid Maldistribution**: Occurs when fluids leave the intravascular space despite normal total body water, as seen in meningococcal shock (with endotoxin-induced capillary leak) and anaphylaxis.

**Heart Failure**: Occurs when the heart cannot maintain adequate cardiac output, either from direct myocardial toxicity (as with local anaesthetic toxicity) or from structural abnormalities (congenital heart disease).

#### Malnutrition Assessment

Two distinct patterns of protein-energy malnutrition warrant urgent identification and management in the emergency setting.

**Kwashiorkor** presents with bilateral foot oedema, which may be accompanied by hair and skin changes. The oedema involves both feet and indicates a particular form of protein deficiency despite caloric intake. Recognition of these changes is critical because the child may not appear severely wasted despite serious nutritional compromise.

**Marasmus** represents severe wasting and is readily visible when rapidly inspecting specific body areas: the arms, legs, and chest should be examined for signs of severe wasting. Unlike kwashiorkor, marasmus presents with visible muscle and fat loss and a "skin and bones" appearance. Both conditions warrant priority triage and nutritional rehabilitation.

#### ETAT Workflow and Implementation

Triage should occur as soon as the child arrives at the facility, even before reception and administrative processes. Triage can be performed at any location where a child might present: the outpatients queue, the emergency room, the ward, or the emergency treatment area itself.

Importantly, ALL STAFF are trained and empowered to perform triage—not just nurses and doctors, but also clerks, gatekeepers, and cleaners. This universal responsibility ensures that no critically ill child is missed due to administrative barriers.

The ETAT workflow follows a systematic process: First, look for emergency signs and move any child with these signs immediately to the emergency treatment area, treating any signs found while there. Second, look for priority signs in the remaining children. Third, place all priority patients at the front of the queue so they are evaluated promptly. Fourth, move on to the next patient in the queue. This four-step workflow ensures that emergency cases are identified and treated first, followed by priority cases, while non-urgent cases proceed through normal queue order.

#### Emergency Treatment Area Resources

The emergency treatment area must be properly equipped to provide immediate life support. At minimum, it requires a dedicated room or trolley with immediate access to drugs and medical supplies necessary for emergency resuscitation and life support. This dedicated space ensures that critically ill children receive treatment without delay and that equipment and medications are available when needed.

#### Scenario-Based Learning: E/P/Q Classification Examples

Applying triage classification to realistic clinical scenarios helps consolidate learning and develop pattern recognition skills necessary for rapid decision-making in the emergency setting.

**Scenario 1**: A 3-year-old girl presents hot to touch but with cold hands, capillary refill of 1.5 seconds, alert, with 4 loose stools per day and skin pinch taking 3 seconds to return. **Classification: P (Priority)**. This child shows signs of dehydration (prolonged skin turgor) with possible early shock (cold hands), though capillary refill remains normal. Priority triage allows prompt fluid resuscitation before frank shock develops.

**Scenario 2**: A 4-year-old boy had a convulsion 1 hour ago, now presents with fast breathing, no cyanosis or severe distress, is very hot, and responds appropriately to questions but has no diarrhea or vomiting. **Classification: P (Priority)**. Recent febrile seizure with fever and altered consciousness warrants priority triage due to high risk of recurrent seizure and underlying serious infection.

**Scenario 3**: A 1-year-old had seizures both at home and outside the clinic, is now unconscious with wet noisy breathing, drooling, and cyanosis. **Classification: E (Emergency)**. This child meets multiple emergency criteria: coma (unresponsive), airway compromise (drooling and wet breathing), cyanosis, and ongoing or recent seizure activity. Immediate emergency treatment is essential.

**Scenario 4**: A 2-year-old male is actively convulsing at presentation. **Classification: E (Emergency)**. Active seizure is an absolute emergency requiring immediate seizure management and airway protection.

**Scenario 5**: A 9-month-old girl presents choking on a bead from an old necklace. **Classification: E (Emergency)**. Foreign body aspiration is an immediate airway emergency requiring rapid intervention to prevent complete obstruction and hypoxia.

**Scenario 6**: A 3-year-old boy presents pale and floppy, with difficulty breathing, 3-day cough history, weight 14 kg, fast breathing with severe chest-indrawing, patent airway, but alert. **Classification: E (Emergency)**. Severe respiratory distress (severe chest-indrawing) combined with signs of shock (pallor, decreased responsiveness/"floppy") suggest severe pneumonia with sepsis, requiring immediate emergency care.

**Scenario 7**: A 4-month-old baby presents with fever, rapid breathing, refusing breastfeeds, 2 episodes of vomiting and diarrhea, weight 5 kg, cold hands, capillary refill 6 seconds (>2s), fast weak femoral pulse, but no chest-indrawing or abnormal lung sounds. **Classification: E (Emergency)**. This infant is in shock (cold hands, markedly prolonged capillary refill of 6 seconds, weak rapid pulse) with signs of sepsis (fever, vomiting, diarrhea). This represents septic shock in a young infant, requiring immediate resuscitation.

**Scenario 8**: An 18-month-old boy has been unwell for 2 days with abdominal pain and fast breathing, weight 11 kg, fine airway sounds, no chest-indrawing, no diarrhea. **Classification: Q (Queue)**. While fever and tachypnea are present, the child shows no signs of severe distress, clear airway, and no respiratory compromise. This child can safely wait in queue for routine evaluation.

**Scenario 9**: A 10-week-old baby will not suckle, is crying a lot, very hot, weight 3.5 kg, with normal airway, breathing, and circulation, no diarrhea or dehydration signs. **Classification: P (Priority)**. This tiny baby (less than 3 months old) with fever and irritability represents high risk of serious occult infection (meningitis, bacteremia, urinary tract infection) and warrants priority assessment despite appearing well otherwise.

**Scenario 10**: A 14-month-old girl has been sleeping all day and is irritable when awoken, rouses only to loud voice and arm shaking (V on AVPU—responds to voice only), has warm hands but is pale, no diarrhea or vomiting, but shows deep breathing with lower chest-wall indrawing. **Classification: P (Priority)**. This child demonstrates altered consciousness (responds to voice only = V on AVPU, approaching coma), respiratory distress with chest-indrawing, and signs of shock (pallor), suggesting possible sepsis with early septic shock.

**Scenario 11**: A 1-year-old girl has a 2-day history of diarrhea and vomiting, weight 6.5 kg, is restless and irritable, airway and breathing are normal, hands are warm, AVPU is V (responds to voice), skin pinch takes 4 seconds to return, sunken eyes are present, and severe malnutrition is noted. **Classification: P (Priority)**. This child demonstrates severe dehydration (sunken eyes, prolonged skin turgor) combined with severe malnutrition. The combination of dehydration and malnutrition increases risk significantly and warrants priority assessment.

**Scenario 12**: A 4-year-old boy presents with sore ear, is well-appearing and playful. **Classification: Q (Queue)**. No emergency or priority signs are present; this is a routine, non-urgent presentation.

**Scenario 13**: A 6-year-old boy presents with chest pain, breathing is normal, child is not shocked, and is conversant. **Classification: Q (Queue)**. Despite the concern raised by chest pain complaint, the absence of emergency or priority signs and a well-appearing child allow routine queue placement.

**Scenario 14**: A 7-week-old boy presents with fever for 1 day, is looking around appropriately, breathing is normal, with no signs of shock or dehydration. **Classification: P (Priority)**. This tiny baby (less than 3 months old) with fever warrants priority triage despite appearing well, due to the high risk of serious infection at this age.

**Scenario 15**: A 7-year-old girl with asthma presents with mild wheeze, is bright and alert. **Classification: Q (Queue)**. Mild wheeze with no severe respiratory distress and an alert, well-appearing child indicates routine, non-emergency asthma exacerbation.

**Scenario 16**: An 18-month-old girl presents with cough and runny nose, has a barking cough and stridor, and is agitated. **Classification: E (Emergency)**. Stridor with the characteristic barking cough of croup, combined with agitation indicating respiratory compromise, requires emergency airway management.

**Scenario 17**: A 2-year-old girl will not walk (1-day history), is crying but consolable, breathing is normal, and she is not shocked. **Classification: Q (Queue)**. No emergency or priority signs are evident; consolability and normal vital function allow routine queue placement.

---

This comprehensive overview provides the foundation for safe, systematic triage and emergency care of pediatric patients, enabling rapid identification of life-threatening conditions and appropriate prioritization of care resources.

-

## EPILEPSY

### Lecture Notes


#### Definitions and Epidemiology

A seizure, or convulsion, is a paroxysmal, time-limited change in motor activity and behavior that results from abnormal electrical activity in the brain. This fundamental definition underpins our understanding of epilepsy, which is a chronic brain disorder characterized by an increased predisposition to seizures, along with neurobiologic, cognitive, psychological, and social consequences. Importantly, epilepsy is considered to be present when two or more unprovoked seizures occur at an interval greater than 24 hours apart.

Seizures are remarkably common in the pediatric population, occurring in approximately 10% of children in the pediatric age group. However, a crucial epidemiological point to remember is that less than one-third of seizures in children are actually caused by epilepsy. This distinction is vital because it means the majority of children presenting with seizures may not have epilepsy itself.

There are multiple provoking factors that can trigger seizures in children, including high fever, infection, syncope, head trauma, hypoxia, toxins, cardiac arrhythmia, and various metabolic abnormalities. Understanding these factors is essential for appropriate management and prevention of future events.

#### Etiology and Causes of Seizures

The causes of seizures in children are diverse and multifactorial. Infection represents a major category, including meningitis and encephalitis. Developmental and structural abnormalities of the brain, trauma, and intracranial hemorrhage are significant structural causes. Hypoxia-related seizures can occur in the perinatal and early childhood period. Metabolic abnormalities constitute a broad category of causes, including hypocalcemia, hypomagnesemia, hypoglycemia, hyper- and hyponatremia, kernicterus in the newborn period, and both amino acid and organic acid disorders. Drug withdrawal, particularly in infants exposed to sedative or opioid medications in utero, is another important cause to consider.

#### Seizure Classification

Seizures are classified into two major categories: partial (focal) and generalized seizures. Partial seizures are further subdivided based on the presence or absence of consciousness impairment. Simple partial seizures occur with consciousness retained, and may present with motor manifestations such as focal jerking, sensory symptoms, autonomic phenomena (such as palpitations or pupillary changes), or psychic manifestations (such as fear or hallucinations). Complex partial seizures involve impairment of consciousness, which may present as a simple partial seizure followed by impaired consciousness, or with consciousness impaired from the onset. Partial seizures with secondary generalization represent a partial seizure that progresses to become a generalized seizure.

Generalized seizures include a variety of types: absence seizures (both typical and atypical forms), generalized tonic-clonic seizures, tonic seizures, clonic seizures, myoclonic seizures, and atonic seizures. These seizures involve both cerebral hemispheres from the onset and typically result in bilateral motor manifestations or loss of consciousness.

In newborns, seizures may take subtle forms due to the relatively undeveloped cerebral cortex. Manifestations include facial grimacing, nystagmus, intermittent apneic episodes, myoclonus, sudden loss of muscle tone, eye blinking, chewing movements, and lip smacking. These subtle presentations can easily be missed if the examiner is not specifically attuned to recognizing neonatal seizures.

#### First-Time Seizure Evaluation

When a child presents with a first-time seizure, a systematic approach is essential. Initial assessment begins with the primary survey: assessment of airway, breathing, and circulation (A, B, C), supplemented by measurement of temperature, blood pressure, and blood glucose. It is critical to check for life-threatening causes during this phase, including meningitis, systemic sepsis, accidental or non-accidental head trauma, and ingestion of drugs or toxins.

A detailed history from an eyewitness is invaluable and should include a specific description of events. It is helpful to ask the eyewitness to imitate the movements observed. The history should cover what the child was doing before the episode, what happened before the seizure began, what occurred during the seizure, and what happened after. Inquire about an aura or behavior preceding the seizure, such as epigastric discomfort, pain, or fear. Specific details about the seizure itself are important: posture, whether tongue biting occurred, whether urinary incontinence happened. The post-ictal state should be characterized—whether the child slept afterward or experienced headache. Also determine the state of consciousness during the episode, the duration of the seizure, the timing (whether it occurred while awake, asleep, or on awakening), and any triggering or precipitating factors.

Physical examination should focus on searching for organic causes and complications. Measure height, weight, and head circumference. Perform a thorough general examination looking for dysmorphic features or neurocutaneous lesions such as those seen in neurofibromatosis or tuberous sclerosis. Conduct a comprehensive neurological examination including fundoscopic examination to assess the retina.

Investigations for a first-time seizure are substantial and include blood work: U&E (urea and electrolytes), creatinine and minerals, glucose, HIV status, full blood count, blood culture and/or urine culture, and toxicology screening. Lumbar puncture should be performed unless contraindicated, and a CT scan of the head should be obtained.

Regarding antimicrobial therapy, always consider the possibility of infection. Antimicrobials should be given to all children less than 18 months with fever, to all children less than 6 months regardless of fever, and to all children greater than 18 months who are unwell or have a persistently altered mental state. When a metabolic disorder is suspected—particularly in children younger than 18 months presenting with myoclonic seizures and delayed development—this should be specifically investigated. If lumbar puncture is contraindicated, an urgent CT scan should be performed. The approach to aborting a seizure follows the same protocol as status epilepticus management.

#### Seizure Mimics and Differential Diagnosis

Several conditions can mimic seizures and must be distinguished from true seizures. Benign sleep myoclonus is a condition in which jerking movements occur exclusively while asleep. Sandifer syndrome presents with dystonia and stiffness of the head and neck, often associated with cerebral palsy. Shuddering attacks are movements seen in toddlers, characterized by distal flapping movements that occur when the child becomes excited. Reflex anoxic seizures result from noxious events that lead to vagally mediated bradycardia and asystole, resulting in brief collapse. Cyanotic breath holding is a form of vasovagal syncope. Pseudoseizures are psychogenic in origin. Recognizing these mimics prevents unnecessary treatment with antiepileptic drugs.

#### Epilepsy Classification and Syndromes

When classifying a child with epilepsy, three key classification questions should be asked: First, what is the cause of the epilepsy—is it idiopathic, symptomatic, or cryptogenic? Second, where in the brain is affected—are the seizures generalized or focal in origin? Third, which seizure types are present—are they generalized or focal seizures? An example of correct epilepsy classification would be "idiopathic generalized epilepsy with tonic-clonic seizures" or "remote symptomatic epilepsy with focal motor seizures secondary to previous TB meningitis."

Several specific epilepsy syndromes deserve particular attention in pediatric practice. West Syndrome, also known as infantile spasm, usually starts in infancy and presents with brief jerks of head and trunk flexion lasting 1 to 2 seconds, with the limbs flexing or extending, usually followed by crying. EEG shows a characteristic pattern called hypsarrhythmia. Delayed treatment has worse neurological outcome, so urgent referral is essential.

Childhood Absence Epilepsy accounts for 5 to 10% of all epilepsy in children. A distinctive feature of this syndrome is that seizures are precipitated by hyperventilation, which can be used diagnostically.

Rolandic Epilepsy occurs in children aged 3 to 13 years. Seizures start during or soon after sleep with facial twitching or grimacing while the child remains conscious, but seizures can then secondarily generalize with a tonic-clonic seizure. Sleep EEG shows bilateral centrotemporal spikes, which are characteristic.

Juvenile Myoclonic Epilepsy is an important syndrome occurring in the age range of 10 to 16 years.

#### Antiepileptic Drug Therapy: General Principles

The decision to start anticonvulsant therapy should be made after two or more seizures have occurred and after careful consideration of the diagnosis. Before deciding to treat, the risk of side effects must always be weighed against the benefits of seizure control. Once the decision to treat is made, it is reassuring that 80% of children with epilepsy can be controlled with one drug, making monotherapy the initial approach.

During treatment, ongoing monitoring is crucial. Maintain a seizure diary to track frequency and nature of breakthrough seizures. Perform thorough neurodevelopmental assessment to identify any developmental delay that might be related to seizures or drug effects. Assess growth and nutritional status, as many antiepileptic drugs can affect these parameters.

An important long-term consideration is that most antiepileptic drugs increase the risk of osteoporosis. The "START LOW, GO SLOW" principle is fundamental to prescribing anticonvulsant drugs: start with low doses and increase gradually to minimize side effects. Educating the patient and family is essential. Provide a patient information leaflet, address practicalities such as schooling and lunch-time doses, plan for high-risk activities such as swimming, develop a seizure management plan with the caregiver and child, emphasize treatment adherence and the importance of using a seizure diary, and warn about common and serious side effects of antiepileptic drugs.

Holistic management extends beyond pharmacotherapy. Address associated behavioral problems and learning disability by utilizing educational psychology and occupational therapy services. Connect families with local support groups, such as Epilepsy South Africa.

#### Sodium Valproate

Sodium valproate is a first-line agent for generalized epilepsy. However, it is contraindicated in childhood absence epilepsy and juvenile myoclonic epilepsy, despite these conditions being generalized epilepsies. Sodium valproate should be avoided if possible in patients with hepatic impairment, inborn errors of metabolism, or those receiving concomitant anti-retroviral therapy. An important drug interaction is that sodium valproate raises the blood level of AZT and also interacts with EFV (efavirenz).

The starting dose of sodium valproate is 10 mg/kg/day, divided into 3 doses for syrup preparations or 2 divided doses for tablets. The therapeutic dose range is 25 to 40 mg/kg/day. Side effects of sodium valproate include increased appetite and weight gain, tremor, ataxia, hepatotoxicity, thrombocytopenia, and teratogenicity with risk of neural tube defects in offspring.

#### Carbamazepine

Carbamazepine is a first-line agent for focal seizures and for some forms of generalized epilepsy. It has significant drug interactions: certain antibiotics such as erythromycin interact with carbamazepine, as do anti-retroviral drugs. The starting dose of carbamazepine is 5 mg/kg/day, divided into 2 doses for both syrup and tablet preparations. The therapeutic dose range is 10 to 20 mg/kg/day. Side effects include ataxia, drowsiness, confusion, agitation, rashes, diplopia, thrombocytopenia, and the rare but serious Stevens-Johnson syndrome.

#### Status Epilepticus Management

The cardinal principle in managing status epilepticus is to STOP THE SEIZURE EARLY. The longer a seizure lasts, the more difficult it becomes to control, and the greater the risk of complications including permanent neurological damage, systemic complications, and mortality.

**STEP 1: Initial Resuscitation.** Ensure that the patient has a patent airway. Administer 100% oxygen by non-rebreather face mask and monitor oxygen saturation. Assess cardiovascular status, attach appropriate monitors, and obtain intravenous access. Check blood glucose immediately. If glucose is low, administer 5 ml/kg of 10% dextrose intravenously and start an infusion of 5% dextrose with 0.9% saline.

**STEP 2: Abort the Seizure.** If the seizure has not spontaneously stopped within 5 minutes, proceed to benzodiazepine administration. Three benzodiazepines are appropriate choices, and one should be selected:

Lorazepam is given at a dose of 0.1 mg/kg intravenously or intraosseously over a few minutes, with a maximum dose of 4 mg.

Alternatively, midazolam may be used at a dose of 0.2 to 0.3 mg/kg over a few minutes, with a maximum dose of 7.5 mg.

Diazepam is another option, given at 0.3 mg/kg intravenously or intraosseously over a few minutes, with a maximum dose of 10 mg.

If intravenous access is not available, alternative routes include rectal diazepam at 0.5 mg/kg with a maximum dose of 20 mg, buccal midazolam at 0.5 mg/kg, or lorazepam given intramuscularly at 0.1 mg/kg.

**STEP 3: Benzodiazepine Repeat.** If seizures do not stop after 10 minutes of the initial benzodiazepine dose, or if they reappear within 1 hour after stopping, repeat the benzodiazepine exactly as given in STEP 2.

**STEP 4a: Phenytoin.** If seizures continue after 10 minutes of benzodiazepine therapy, administer phenytoin at a dose of 20 mg/kg in 5 ml/kg of normal saline, given intravenously over 30 minutes. However, if the patient is already on oral phenytoin at baseline, omit this step and proceed directly to STEP 4b. During phenytoin infusion, careful monitoring is essential: watch closely for cardiac arrhythmias, hypotension, and bradycardia. If any of these complications occur, stop the infusion immediately and contact the pediatric intensive care unit.

**STEP 4b: Phenobarbitone.** If seizures do not stop after 10 minutes of phenytoin infusion, or if the patient was already on oral phenytoin at baseline (requiring bypass of STEP 4a), administer phenobarbitone. Phenobarbitone is the drug of choice in children younger than 2 years with status epilepticus. The dose is 20 mg/kg given intravenously or intraosseously over 5 to 10 minutes.

**STEP 5: Phenobarbitone Repeat.** If the child remains convulsing 30 minutes after starting either phenobarbitone or phenytoin, repeat phenobarbitone at a reduced dose of 10 mg/kg intravenously or intraosseously over 5 to 10 minutes. If the child is still convulsing after a further 10 minutes, repeat phenobarbitone again at 10 mg/kg intravenously or intraosseously over 5 to 10 minutes. After this final repeat dose, if seizures have not been controlled, telephone the pediatric intensive care unit immediately for further management of what has now become refractory status epilepticus.

The timing of progression through these steps is critical. Benzodiazepine repeat is triggered if seizures do not stop after 10 minutes of the initial dose or if they reappear within 1 hour. Progression from benzodiazepines to phenytoin is triggered if seizures continue after 10 minutes of benzodiazepine therapy. Progression from phenytoin to phenobarbitone is triggered if seizures do not stop after 10 minutes of phenytoin infusion. Finally, pediatric intensive care unit referral is indicated for continued seizures after STEP 5, representing a status epilepticus episode lasting more than 30 minutes from initiation of treatment.

-

## ETAT - Coma and Convulsions

### Lecture Notes


#### Learning Objectives

The ETAT framework for managing children in coma and convulsions establishes four core competencies: (1) assess consciousness using the AVPU score to systematically evaluate mental status; (2) identify the child in coma by recognizing which AVPU states indicate loss of consciousness; (3) identify the child having convulsions by recognizing the characteristic clinical features; and (4) treat both coma and convulsions using evidence-based emergency protocols. These objectives guide the entire approach to these life-threatening presentations.

#### Emergency Triage Signs (ETAT Framework)

ETAT identifies six life-threatening signs requiring immediate assessment and intervention. The mnemonic A-B-C-D emphasizes the priority sequence: **Airway** integrity must be established first, followed by **Breathing** adequacy, then **Circulation** status. Beyond these fundamentals, three additional emergency signs demand immediate recognition: **Coma**, **Convulsion**, and **Dehydration (severe)**. Any child presenting with one or more of these signs requires urgent triage and rapid treatment. The ETAT framework provides a standardized language that enables clinicians to quickly communicate severity and prioritize limited resources.

#### Assessing Consciousness: The AVPU Score

The AVPU scale provides a rapid, reproducible method for assessing consciousness in the emergency setting. **Alert (A)** indicates the child is awake and aware, able to interact appropriately with their environment. **Voice response (V)** means the child does not spontaneously interact but will respond when called to by name or when verbal stimuli are applied. **Pain response (P)** indicates the child only responds to pain stimulation, such as pressure on a fingernail bed or sternal rub—no response occurs to voice alone. **Unresponsive (U)** describes the child who does not respond to voice and is also unresponsive to pain, either through shaking or painful stimuli.

Critically, **AVPU scores of P and U indicate coma**. A child scoring P (responding only to pain) or U (unresponsive) is unconscious and requires urgent airway management, investigation, and treatment. The P and U states represent deepening levels of impaired consciousness that place the child at severe risk for aspiration, brain swelling, and death.

#### Clinical Presentation of Convulsions

A convulsion is characterized by a distinctive and unmistakable cluster of signs. The cardinal feature is a **sudden loss of consciousness** that occurs simultaneously with uncontrolled jerky movements of the limbs and/or face. Additional features may include stiffening (tonic contraction) of the arms and legs, involuntary twitching of the face, hands, or feet, abnormal eye movements (typically divergent or rolling), and brief episodes of apnoea during the tonic phase. The sudden onset, loss of consciousness, and involuntary motor activity together make convulsions clinically distinctive from other causes of altered behavior or movement.

**Status epilepticus** is defined as a seizure lasting longer than 5 minutes and represents a medical emergency. Prolonged seizures place the child at severe risk for multiple complications: brain swelling (cerebral edema), irreversible brain damage from neuronal cell death, aspiration of gastric contents with subsequent airway obstruction or aspiration pneumonia, hypoxia from respiratory compromise, and death. Any seizure lasting more than 5 minutes must be treated urgently with anticonvulsant medication.

#### Management of the Child in Coma: Airway and Breathing

The first and most critical step in managing a comatose child is to establish and maintain a patent airway while ensuring adequate ventilation. Begin by **positioning the child appropriately**. For infants, use a **neutral head position**—avoid hyperextension of the head, which can paradoxically obstruct the infant airway. For older children, the **sniffing position** (mild extension of the head at the atlanto-occipital joint) optimizes airway diameter and breathing ease.

In any child where cervical spine injury is suspected (trauma history, mechanism of injury), **protect the cervical spine** during positioning maneuvers. Use the **jaw thrust** technique to open the airway without extending the neck—this forward displacement of the mandible lifts the base of the tongue away from the posterior pharynx. Gently **suction the oropharynx** to remove secretions, blood, or gastric contents that could obstruct breathing. **Administer oxygen via a well-fitting face mask** to achieve the highest oxygen concentration possible and prevent hypoxia during the critical early period.

Once immediate airway patency is assured, place the unconscious child in the **recovery position**—lying on their side with the lower arm supporting the head, upper leg flexed, and mouth slightly open. This position drains fluid from the mouth, prevents the tongue from falling back and obstructing the airway, and greatly reduces the risk of aspiration of vomitus should the child vomit.

#### Management of the Child in Coma: Checking and Treating Hypoglycaemia

Immediately after securing the airway and breathing, **check blood glucose using Dextrostix** (rapid bedside glucose testing). Hypoglycaemia is defined as blood glucose **< 3 mmol/litre (< 55 mg/dl)** and is a common and treatable cause of altered consciousness and coma in children. The principle "**D-E-F-G: Don't Ever Forget Glucose**" emphasizes that missing hypoglycaemia can result in permanent brain damage while its treatment is simple, safe, and effective.

If the child is hypoglycaemic, **immediately administer IV glucose at a dose of 5 ml/kg of 10% glucose solution given as a rapid IV injection**. This rapidly raises blood glucose and may dramatically improve consciousness. Never delay treating hypoglycaemia while awaiting other investigations. Hypoglycaemia is the only metabolic emergency for which treatment should precede complete diagnosis.

#### Management of Convulsions: The Three-Step Approach

The management of a convulsing child follows a systematic three-step approach. **Step 1: Manage the airway and breathing.** Position the child safely, suction gently to clear the airway, and provide oxygen via face mask. **Critically, do not place anything into the mouth**—this includes oral airways, tongue depressors, or fingers. Such objects can obstruct the airway, cause aspiration, or cause dental trauma. The child will not swallow their tongue; protection of the airway is accomplished through positioning and gentle suctioning, not by force-feeding objects into the mouth.

**Step 2: Check blood glucose immediately.** Use Dextrostix to measure blood glucose. If the glucose is < 3 mmol/l, **administer IV glucose at 5 ml/kg of 10% solution** rapidly by IV injection. Hypoglycaemia is a common cause of or contributor to convulsions in children, particularly those who are malnourished or have prolonged illnesses.

**Step 3: Stop the seizure with anticonvulsant medication.** Once airway, breathing, and glucose have been addressed, administer an anticonvulsant drug to terminate the seizure activity.

#### First-Line Anticonvulsants

**Lorazepam** is the first choice anticonvulsant, a short-acting benzodiazepine given by the **IV route**. If IV access cannot be obtained, lorazepam may be given by **IM (intramuscular) or buccal routes**.

**Midazolam** is the second choice if lorazepam is unavailable. It has the advantage of multiple routes: **IV, IM, or buccal**. The buccal and IM routes are particularly valuable in settings where IV access is difficult or delayed.

**Diazepam** is the last choice of the benzodiazepine group, used only when both lorazepam and midazolam are unavailable. Diazepam may be given by **IV injection or rectal (PR) administration only**. Importantly, diazepam cannot be given by IM or buccal routes, which limits its utility in difficult IV access situations.

#### Second-Line Anticonvulsants: When Benzodiazepines Fail

If the seizure continues despite appropriate benzodiazepine administration, **phenobarbitone is the drug of next choice**. This is a long-acting barbiturate that provides sustained seizure control but takes longer to achieve peak effect than benzodiazepines.

**Phenytoin is an alternative** if phenobarbitone is unavailable. Phenytoin must be given as an **IV infusion over 30 minutes** to allow for safe metabolism and to reduce the risk of cardiac arrhythmias. If during the infusion the child develops an arrhythmia, bradycardia, or hypotension, **immediately stop the phenytoin infusion**. Once the child is clinically settled, the infusion may be resumed at a slower rate, **infusing over 1 hour instead of 30 minutes**.

#### Special Considerations in Neonatal Convulsions

Neonates (infants less than 4 weeks of age) have different anticonvulsant requirements than older children. **Phenobarbitone is the first-choice anticonvulsant for neonates** with convulsions, not the benzodiazepines used for older children.

Benzodiazepines carry a significant risk in neonates: they may cause respiratory depression and apnoea (temporary cessation of breathing), particularly in sick infants. **Benzodiazepines should not be used in neonates without ventilatory support**—that is, neonates must be intubated and on mechanical ventilation before benzodiazepines are administered. In neonatal convulsions where IV access is not immediately available, phenobarbitone remains the safest first-line agent.

#### Vascular Access and Continued Seizure Activity

During convulsion management, establishing reliable vascular access is essential for administering medications and drawing blood for investigations. An IV line should be placed as soon as possible. If the child continues to convulse despite initial anticonvulsant therapy and IV access has not been achieved, an **intraosseous (IO) line should be placed** through the proximal tibia or humerus. This provides rapid access to the central circulation, allowing delivery of medications and fluids even when peripheral veins are unavailable or difficult to access. The IO line permits both blood drawing for laboratory investigations and administration of glucose and additional anticonvulsant medications.

#### Post-Seizure Management and Monitoring

Once the seizure has stopped, the work is not complete. **Recheck A-B-C** (airway, breathing, circulation) to ensure these remain adequate, as post-seizure drowsiness and residual medication effects can compromise respiratory efforts. **Monitor closely for further seizures**—children who have one seizure in an acute illness are at high risk of recurrence.

**Treat for infection**, particularly meningitis, which is one of the most common causes of convulsions in children. A lumbar puncture to obtain cerebrospinal fluid for analysis and culture should be performed urgently (after ensuring the child is stable and after excluding contraindications such as signs of increased intracranial pressure or focal neurological signs).

**Recheck blood glucose** to ensure hypoglycaemia is not recurring or persisting, which would increase the risk of further seizures or brain damage. If glucose remains low, administer IV glucose again. **Provide comprehensive after-care** including monitoring, treatment of the underlying cause, nutritional support, and careful observation for complications.

#### Summary of Management Priorities

The key to successful management of coma and convulsions in children is systematic application of ETAT principles. After establishing the ABC (Airway, Breathing, Circulation), immediately assess consciousness using AVPU and identify whether coma or convulsion is present. Remember **D-E-F-G: Don't Ever Forget Glucose**—check blood glucose with Dextrostix and treat hypoglycaemia immediately with 5 ml/kg of 10% IV glucose solution. For convulsions, administer first-line benzodiazepines (lorazepam, then midazolam, then diazepam) via the most accessible route. Know the second-line agents (phenobarbitone and phenytoin) for convulsions that do not respond to benzodiazepines.
