Remember that neonates require phenobarbitone as first-line therapy, not benzodiazepines. After the seizure stops, recheck A-B-C and glucose, monitor for recurrence, investigate for infection, and provide supportive care. This systematic approach saves lives.

-

## ETAT - Dehydration

### Lecture Notes


#### ETAT Integration and Triage Placement

The ETAT (Emergency Triage, Assessment and Treatment) algorithm provides a systematic approach to identify and manage dehydration in children. The framework uses the 3TPR-MOB priority assessment system: Tiny Baby, Temperature, Trauma, Pallor, Poisoning, Pain, Respiratory Distress, Restlessness, Referral Urgency, Malnutrition, Oedema, and Burns. When a child presents with diarrhoea, dehydration assessment occurs after evaluation of emergency signs via the Circulation/Coma/Convulsions (CCC) pathway. For children without diarrhoea, the triage proceeds directly to 3TPR-MOB priority assessment.

Emergency signs requiring immediate intervention include hands that are not warm, capillary refill time greater than 2 seconds, weak or rapid pulse, coma, and active convulsions. These signs supersede all other assessments and trigger immediate management of shock and stabilization before dehydration-specific therapy.

#### Clinical Assessment of Severe Dehydration

Assessment for severe dehydration in children with diarrhoea occurs after the CCC (Circulation/Coma/Convulsions) assessment is completed. The three key clinical signs that indicate severe dehydration are: lethargy, sunken eyes, and a skin pinch that takes longer than 2 seconds to return to normal. The skin pinch assessment specifically requires that the skin fold, when pinched, remains elevated rather than quickly returning to normal position.

Concurrent assessment for severe malnutrition is essential during the dehydration evaluation. Signs that indicate severe malnutrition assessment is needed include visible severe wasting and oedema of both feet. Children with severe malnutrition present a unique clinical challenge because they have a precarious metabolic and circulatory status that significantly alters the approach to rehydration.

Clinical decision-making in severe dehydration management requires consideration of multiple domains: differentiation between shock and severe dehydration, selection of fluid type, determination of administration route (oral versus intravenous), and understanding the distinction between diarrhoeal loss and plasma equivalent fluid replacement. Blood glucose must be checked in all children with severe malnutrition and dehydration, as hypoglycaemia below 3 mmol/L is a serious complication requiring immediate treatment with 5 mL/kg of 10% dextrose.

#### Oral Rehydration (Plan A)

Oral rehydration with oral rehydration solution (ORS) is the preferred initial fluid route for mild to moderate dehydration because it is cheaper, safer, and more physiologic than intravenous therapy when the child is able to tolerate oral intake. ORS should be given as soon as possible after assessment of dehydration.

The ORS dosing regimen for mild-moderate dehydration (Plan A) is 5 mL/kg administered every 15 minutes. After each diarrhoeal stool, an additional 10 mL/kg of ORS is given. Reassessment of the child's hydration status occurs after 1-2 hours of ORS therapy. If the child is rehydrated after this interval, maintenance Plan A fluids are continued. If oral rehydration fails—indicated by persistent vomiting, inability to tolerate oral intake, or inadequate clinical response—the child is escalated to intravenous or nasogastric fluid therapy.

#### Intravenous Fluid Therapy

When intravenous access is obtained, the fluid composition used for severe dehydration treatment is 1/2 Dextrose Saline (1/2DS) with 5% dextrose in water. The infusion rate and duration depend on whether severe malnutrition is present.

**For severe dehydration without malnutrition:** IV fluid is administered at 20 mL/kg/hour for 4 hours. Reassessment for effectiveness and for signs of shock occurs within 1-2 hours of starting therapy. If shock persists after initial fluid administration, the rate may be increased and senior advice should be sought. Antibiotics should be considered based on clinical presentation, and blood glucose should be checked.

**For severe dehydration WITH severe malnutrition:** The infusion rate is much slower and longer: 10 mL/kg/hour for 8 hours. This slower approach is critical because rapid rehydration in severely malnourished children can precipitate metabolic collapse from sudden fluid and electrolyte shifts, leading to cardiovascular instability. Sodium handling requires careful management in these children, as their requirements differ from non-malnourished children. Blood glucose must be monitored closely and hypoglycaemia (below 3 mmol/L) treated immediately with 5 mL/kg of 10% dextrose intravenously.

#### Alternative Routes: Nasogastric and Intraosseous

When intravenous access cannot be obtained, two alternative routes are available.

**Nasogastric (NG) fluid therapy:** ORS is delivered via nasogastric tube at 20 mL/kg/hour for 6 hours. Alternatively, continuous infusion or administration of 5 mL/kg every 15 minutes may be preferred in some settings. NG infusion is indicated when IV access has failed and when oral or ORS is not tolerated or is failing due to persistent vomiting or failed oral trial. Weight-based example: A 3.1 kg child requiring NG therapy receives 20 mL/kg/hour = 20 × 3.1 = 62 mL/hour (weight band 60 mL/hour). A 10 kg child receives 20 × 10 = 200 mL/hour (weight band 240 mL/hour).

**Intraosseous (IO) fluids:** When neither IV nor NG access is feasible, intraosseous infusion at 10 mL/kg/hour for 4 hours is an alternative route for delivering rehydration fluids.

#### Drip Rate Calculations and Weight-Based Examples

Accurate calculation of drip rates is essential for safe fluid delivery, particularly when electronic infusion devices are unavailable and manual roller controllers must be used.

**Conversion formulas for manual drip control:**
- For a 60 drops/mL dropper: Drops per minute = mL per hour
- For a 15 drops/mL dropper: Drops per minute = mL per hour divided by 4

**Weight-based clinical examples:**

*ORS administration (5 mL/kg/15 minutes):* A 7.3 kg child with severe dehydration requires 5 × 7.3 = 36.5 mL/15 minutes. Using a weight band, this rounds to 40 mL/15 minutes for practical administration.

*IV therapy without malnutrition (20 mL/kg/hour × 4 hours):* An 11 kg child receives 20 × 11 = 220 mL/hour, which rounds to a weight band of 240 mL/hour. Using a 60 drops/mL dropper, this equals 240 drops/min. Using a 15 drops/mL dropper, this equals 240 ÷ 4 = 60 drops/min.

*IV therapy with malnutrition (10 mL/kg/hour × 8 hours):* An 11 kg child receives 10 × 11 = 110 mL/hour, rounded to a weight band of 80 mL/hour. Using a 60 drops/mL dropper, this equals 80 drops/min. Using a 15 drops/mL dropper, this equals 80 ÷ 4 = 20 drops/min.

*Nasogastric fluid delivery (20 mL/kg/hour × 6 hours):* A 10 kg child receives 20 × 10 = 200 mL/hour, rounded to a weight band of 240 mL/hour. Using a 60 drops/mL dropper, this equals 200-240 drops/min. Using a 15 drops/mL dropper, this equals 50-60 drops/min.

#### Manual Drip Controllers: Limitations and Safety Considerations

Manual roller controllers have several inherent limitations that affect their reliability and safety for fluid delivery, particularly in resource-constrained settings.

**Mechanical limitations:** Manually set roller clamps tend to slow down progressively over time, requiring frequent monitoring and adjustment to maintain the target drip rate. The flow rate depends critically on the height of the IV bag above the infusion site—greater height increases the rate, and changes in bag height necessitate controller readjustment.

**Positional factors:** The position of the child's limb at the infusion site can affect flow by occluding vessels or altering vascular resistance, changing the infusion rate unpredictably. Vigilant physical inspection and regular rate checks are required throughout infusion.

**Safety hazards of unattended use:** Manual drip controllers cannot reliably be set and left unattended. They lack alarms for too-fast or too-slow flow, creating risk of catastrophic rapid bolus delivery or inadequate rehydration from under-infusion. A maximum bolus of less than 40 mL/kg must be enforced to prevent injury.

**Preparation safety for small children:** For children weighing less than 5 kg, 200 mL vacolitres must be prepared with caution. Before hanging, 100 mL should be removed from the vacollitre to limit the maximum possible bolus in case of accidental free-fall of fluid.

**Preferred delivery systems:** Rather than relying on manual roller controllers alone, burettes or sachet systems are the preferred choice for safer, more controlled rehydration, particularly in young children.

#### Electronic Infusion Pumps and Equipment Standards

Electronic infusion pumps provide substantially greater safety than manual controllers. They allow reliable setting of flow in millilitres per hour, provide audible and visual alarms for flow deviation (too fast or too slow), and permit safe unattended infusion when properly programmed and monitored.

**Institutional priority:** Institutions should prioritize ensuring an adequate supply of electronic drip minders or syringe pumps with proper maintenance schedules. Reliance solely on manual controllers for paediatric rehydration represents a significant patient safety risk.

#### Training and Skill Development

Competency in rehydration management requires practice with rate calculations and conversion formulas. **Oral rate drills** involve calculating ORS delivery rates—for example, millilitres per 15-minute interval—to ensure accurate dosing in children managed without intravenous access. **Nasogastric rate drills** similarly involve practice calculations for NG fluid delivery, converting millilitres per hour to drops per minute using the appropriate dropper calibration (60 or 15 drops/mL) for 20 mL/kg/hour regimens.

-

## LATEST KZN COVID GUIDELINES

### Lecture Notes


#### Screening and Triage

COVID-19 screening represents three fundamental changes to paediatric management. First, screening must be incorporated into general screening for tuberculosis, HIV, developmental status, and nutritional assessment. Second, routine testing of all children requiring admission is not recommended; targeted testing of symptomatic children is mandatory instead. Third, children's wards no longer require compartmentalisation into non-COVID, COVID, and PUI (persons under investigation) sections.

During triage, implement respiratory screening for fever, cough, difficulty breathing, or sore throat within the preceding 14 days. These are the key symptoms warranting COVID-19 assessment. Once a positive respiratory screen is documented, children over 2 years old and their caregiver should receive a surgical mask at the triage stage.

Patients presenting with excessive cough and sputum production require prioritised single-room placement and must wear masks. Cough hygiene must be reinforced: cover the nose and mouth with tissue or the crook of the arm when sneezing, coughing, or blowing the nose. Hand hygiene is equally critical—wash hands and keep contaminated hands away from eyes, nose, and mouth.

#### Social Distancing and Isolation Measures

Children presenting with respiratory symptoms should maintain a social distancing distance of 1-2 metres and remain within their designated space. For suspected COVID-19, isolation must occur in a side ward with strict infection prevention and control (IPC) precautions.

#### Testing and Case Definition

A suspected COVID-19 case is defined as any person presenting with an acute (≤14 days) respiratory tract infection or other clinical illness compatible with COVID-19. This includes individuals with cough, sore throat, shortness of breath, anosmia (loss of smell), or dysgeusia (loss of taste), with or without associated fever, weakness, myalgia, or diarrhoea. Routine SARS-CoV-2 testing for asymptomatic patients is not recommended.

Targeted testing is appropriate for children with suspected COVID-19 requiring admission who have severe pneumonia, MIS-C, or other suspected COVID-19 related disease. The diagnostic test of choice is SARS-CoV-2 PCR.

#### Multisystem Inflammatory Syndrome in Children (MIS-C)

MIS-C, also known as PIMS-TS or Kawasaki-like syndrome, typically occurs after resolution of acute COVID-19 infection. The WHO case definition applies to children and adolescents aged 0-19 years.

MIS-C diagnosis requires fever for more than 3 days plus at least two of the following systemic manifestations: (1) rash or bilateral non-purulent conjunctivitis or mucо-cutaneous inflammation of the oral mucosa, hands, or feet; (2) hypotension or shock; (3) features of myocardial dysfunction, pericarditis, valvulitis, or coronary abnormalities (evidenced by echocardiography or elevated troponin); (4) evidence of coagulopathy, including abnormal PT, PTT, or elevated d-dimers; (5) acute gastrointestinal manifestations such as diarrhoea, vomiting, or abdominal pain.

Inflammatory markers must be elevated (ESR, C-reactive protein, or procalcitonin). No other obvious microbial cause for inflammation can be present, excluding bacterial sepsis and staphylococcal or streptococcal shock syndromes. Evidence of COVID-19 is required: RT-PCR, antigen test, serology positivity (when available), or likelihood based on clinical context.

#### Mild Disease Management

Children with mild COVID-19 should receive symptomatic treatment at home or in an isolation facility. Caregivers must be provided with patient information pamphlets and receive guidance on suitable infection prevention and control practices. Special investigations and imaging are not routinely indicated. Routine micronutrient or vitamin supplementation has limited evidence and is not recommended.

Routine steroid use is potentially harmful in mild disease, although steroids should be considered if indicated for non-COVID-19 conditions (such as asthma or laryngotracheo-bronchitis). There is documented increased morbidity associated with steroid use in children with influenza and bronchiolitis, so caution is warranted.

#### Oxygen Therapy and Severe Disease Management

Children with moderate or severe COVID-19 require admission to a children's ward isolation cubicle or side ward for supportive in-patient care. Every hospital must establish an isolation cubicle for respiratory cases.

The target oxygen saturation (SpO2) is >92%. A systematic oxygen therapy ladder guides escalation:

**Step 1**: If unable to maintain SpO2 >92% in room air, add nasal prong oxygen (NPO2) at 2 L/min.

**Step 2**: If unable to maintain SpO2 >92% on NPO2 at 2 L/min, change to a 40% face mask (pink) at 8 L/min.

**Step 3**: If unable to maintain SpO2 >92% on a 40% face mask (pink) at 8 L/min, change to a 60% face mask (orange) at 10 L/min.

**Step 4**: If unable to maintain SpO2 >92% on a 60% face mask at 10 L/min, change to a non-rebreather face mask at 15 L/min and contact the local regional hospital/PICU.

**Step 5**: If unable to maintain SpO2 >92% on a non-rebreather face mask at 15 L/min, consider high-flow humidified nasal cannula (HFNC) subject to appropriate facility availability and contact the local regional hospital/PICU.

#### Antimicrobial Therapy

For suspected bacterial co-infection, first-line antibiotic options are: Amoxicillin 45 mg/kg per dose, 12 hourly orally for 5 days, OR Ampicillin 50 mg/kg per dose intravenously 6 hourly. Gentamicin 6 mg/kg intravenously daily for 5-10 days may be added if indicated.

#### Corticosteroid Therapy

Steroids should only be used in consultation with the local paediatrician or regional hospital. They are indicated in children with confirmed COVID-19 who require nasal prong oxygen (NPO2).

**Dexamethasone**: 0.15 mg/kg daily intravenously for 10 days.

**Hydrocortisone**: 1-2 mg/kg per dose intravenously 6 hourly for 10 days.

**Prednisone**: 1 mg/kg daily orally for 10 days.

The paediatric department of the local regional hospital must be consulted before prescribing steroids.

#### Antiviral Therapy and Respiratory Support

Remdesivir is not recommended for use in children with COVID-19 until more information becomes available regarding its efficacy and safety at this age.

If nebulisers are necessary, use a metered-dose inhaler (MDI) with a spacer as an alternative delivery method.

#### Diagnostic Investigations

Complete blood count (FBC) findings may show normal or reduced neutrophil, lymphocyte, and platelet counts, often with reduced neutrophils or lymphocytes and reversal of the neutrophil:lymphocyte ratio (age-dependent).

C-reactive protein (CRP) is normal or elevated in severe disease.

Procalcitonin (PCT) is typically normal or low; elevation suggests bacterial co-infection.

Lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) may be elevated in severe disease.

Coagulation profile abnormalities include prolonged INR/APTT, and d-dimers are increased in severe disease.

Chest X-ray typically demonstrates bilateral patchy nodular or speckled ground glass opacities, although other radiological patterns have been described.

#### Infection Control and Personal Protective Equipment

Equipment decontamination requires a 1000 parts per million (ppm) dilution of detergent or disinfectant solution.

For routine care of COVID-19 patients, healthcare workers should wear surgical masks, aprons, and +/- visors. For aerosol-generating procedures, N95 masks and visors are required. Staff require a mask for routine care but an N95 mask, apron, and shield for aerosol-generating procedures.

#### Referral Criteria and Consultation

Consult the regional hospital's paediatric department when: (1) the child requires ≥40% oxygen to maintain SpO2 above 92%; (2) the child's condition destabilises (requiring immediate consultation); (3) the child meets criteria for MIS-C.

The KZN Provincial Paediatric COVID-19 Hotline is available at 0800 006603.

#### Discharge and De-isolation Criteria

Children can be discharged from hospital once they no longer require supplementary oxygen. Before discharge, ensure holistic care and review the immunisation status, nutritional status, HIV risk, and tuberculosis risk of the child.

De-isolation timelines are: Children with mild COVID-19 can be de-isolated 7 days from symptom onset. Children with moderate or severe COVID-19 can be de-isolated 7 days after they achieve clinical stability (cessation of oxygen).

#### Epidemiological Context

At the time of guideline publication, the dominant SARS-CoV-2 variants circulating were sub-variants of Omicron (B.1.1.529).

-

## Medical Professionalism

### Lecture Notes


## Introduction: Defining Professionalism

Medical professionalism describes the skills, attitudes, values and behaviours common to those undertaking the practice of medicine. According to the World Medical Association (WMA), it encompasses the maintenance of competence for a unique body of knowledge and skill set, personal integrity, altruism, adherence to ethical codes of conduct, accountability, a dedication to self-regulation, and the exercise of discretionary judgment. This definition captures the essence of what it means to practise medicine at the highest standards.

The University of Ottawa offers a complementary perspective: professionalism embodies the relationship between medicine and society as it forms the basis of patient-physician trust. It attempts to make tangible certain attitudes, behaviours, and characteristics that are desirable among the medical profession. Central to this understanding is the concept of calling—a set of values, behaviours, and relationships that underpins the trust the public has in doctors. This calling is not merely an occupational choice but a commitment to principles that extend far beyond the technical practice of medicine.

#### The Foundation of Trust

Understanding professionalism begins with recognizing that it is fundamentally about trust. Patients entrust doctors with their most intimate health information and their physical safety. The public grants the medical profession special privileges and autonomy based on the assumption that practitioners will act in their patients' best interests, maintain rigorous standards, and remain accountable to society. This trust is earned through demonstrated commitment to ethical practice, competence, and continuous improvement.

## The Social Contract and Professional Role

Professionalism is the moral understanding among medical practitioners that gives reality to the social contract between medicine and society. This contract grants the medical profession a monopoly over the use of its knowledge base, the right to considerable autonomy in practice and the privilege of self-regulation. In exchange, society places particular expectations on practitioners: they must act with integrity, demonstrate competence, prioritize patient welfare, and remain accountable to those they serve.

The CanMEDS framework provides a structured understanding of how physicians fulfill this social contract. As Professionals, physicians are committed to the health and well-being of individual patients and society through ethical practice, high personal standards of behaviour, commitment to the profession, profession-led regulation, and maintenance of personal health. The Professional Role is grounded in a professional identity and is guided by codes of ethics and a commitment to clinical competence, ongoing professional development, integrity, honesty, altruism, respect for diversity, the adoption of appropriate attitudes and behaviours, and promotion of the public good.

The commitments and elements of the Professional role form the basis of the social contract between a physician and society. In return, society grants physicians the privilege of profession-led regulation, with the understanding that they are accountable to those served, to society, and to the profession itself. This accountability is not a burden but a cornerstone of medical practice—it ensures that the profession maintains its standing and trustworthiness across generations.

## HPCSA Section 27A: Core Requirements for Professional Practice

The Health Professions Council of South Africa (HPCSA) Section 27A outlines eight fundamental requirements that all health practitioners must meet. These requirements provide clear, enforceable standards that embody the broader principles of professionalism.

#### Requirement (a): Acting in the Best Interests of Patients

A practitioner shall at all times act in the best interests of his or her patients. This foundational requirement places patient welfare at the center of every clinical decision. It requires practitioners to advocate for their patients, prioritize their needs, and ensure that personal, financial, or institutional considerations do not compromise patient care. In paediatrics, this imperative is particularly pronounced, as children are vulnerable patients who depend entirely on their healthcare providers to champion their welfare.

#### Requirement (b): Respecting Confidentiality, Privacy, Choices, and Dignity

A practitioner shall respect patient confidentiality, privacy, choices and dignity. Confidentiality is a cornerstone of the therapeutic relationship. Patients must feel secure that the sensitive information they share with their practitioners will be protected. Beyond confidentiality, practitioners must respect patients' autonomy, honour their choices regarding treatment, and treat them with the dignity that every person deserves. This respect extends to all aspects of practice, including how information is stored, communicated, and used.

#### Requirement (c): Maintaining High Standards of Personal Conduct and Integrity

A practitioner shall maintain the highest standards of personal conduct and integrity. Professional integrity means being truthful, keeping promises, avoiding conflicts of interest, and conducting oneself honourably in all circumstances. Integrity is not situational—it applies whether a practitioner is in the clinic, hospital, community, or beyond. It encompasses financial dealings, interactions with colleagues, and personal conduct that affects professional standing.

#### Requirement (d): Providing Informed Consent and Information

A practitioner shall provide adequate information about the patient's diagnosis, treatment options and alternatives, costs associated with each such alternative and any other pertinent information to enable the patient to exercise a choice in terms of treatment and informed decision-making pertaining to his or her health and that of others. Informed consent is a fundamental right and a legal requirement. Practitioners must communicate in language that patients understand, ensure comprehension, and allow time for questions. In paediatrics, this extends to families and guardians who make decisions on behalf of children, with age-appropriate involvement of the child according to their capacity.

#### Requirement (e): Maintaining Professional Knowledge and Skills

A practitioner shall keep his or her professional knowledge and skills up to date. Medical science advances continuously, and practitioners have an ethical obligation to remain current. This requirement demands engagement in continuing professional development, evidence-based medicine, and reflection on practice. Failure to maintain competence puts patients at risk and violates the trust society has placed in the profession.

#### Requirement (f): Maintaining Effective Communication

A practitioner shall maintain proper and effective communication with his or her patients and other professionals. Clear communication prevents errors, builds trust, and ensures collaborative care. Whether communicating diagnoses, explaining treatment plans, discussing risks, or liaising with colleagues, practitioners must be clear, compassionate, and culturally sensitive. In paediatrics, communication skills are essential when working with children and families from diverse backgrounds.

#### Requirement (g): Obtaining Informed Consent

Except in an emergency, a practitioner shall obtain informed consent from a patient or, in the event that the patient is unable to provide consent for treatment, from his or her next of kin. This requirement protects patient autonomy and prevents non-consensual treatment. In emergencies, implied consent applies, but practitioners must explain their actions afterward. For children, guardians provide consent, though older children may provide assent when developmentally appropriate.

#### Requirement (h): Maintaining Accurate Patient Records

A practitioner shall keep accurate patient records. Comprehensive, accurate documentation is essential for continuity of care, legal protection, and quality improvement. Records should be legible, timely, and contain sufficient detail to allow another practitioner to understand the patient's history, findings, and management. Good record-keeping reflects accountability and professional standards.

## The CanMEDS Framework: Competence and Commitment

Professional competence extends beyond technical skill. According to CanMEDS, professional competence is the habitual and judicious use of communication, knowledge, technical skills, clinical reasoning, emotions, values, and reflection in daily practice for the benefit of the individual and community being served. This definition integrates the cognitive, technical, emotional, and ethical dimensions of medical practice.

The CanMEDS Professional role is structured around four major commitments: to patients, to society, to the profession, and to self. Each commitment encompasses specific competencies and responsibilities that physicians must develop and maintain throughout their careers.

#### Commitment to Patients

The CanMEDS Commitment to Patients encompasses altruism, bioethical principles and theories, commitment to excellence in clinical practice and mastery of the discipline, compassion and caring, confidentiality and its limits, integrity and honesty, moral and ethical behaviour, professional boundaries, and respect for diversity. This commitment reflects the primacy of the patient-physician relationship. Altruism is central—practitioners must consistently place patient welfare ahead of personal gain. Bioethical knowledge provides the framework for navigating complex clinical dilemmas, while compassion and caring humanize the practice of medicine.

Professional boundaries are particularly important. Physicians must maintain appropriate relationships with patients, avoiding dual relationships and conflicts of interest that could compromise objectivity or exploit patients' vulnerability. Understanding confidentiality and its limits—including mandatory reporting, child protection, and public health concerns—is essential for ethical practice.

#### Commitment to Society

The CanMEDS Commitment to Society includes commitment to the promotion of the public good in health care, social accountability, and understanding the social contract in health care. Physicians are not isolated practitioners but members of a healthcare system serving entire populations. This commitment requires advocating for health equity, understanding social determinants of health, and contributing to population health initiatives. In paediatrics, practitioners must consider the broader social context—poverty, education, family stability—that affects child health and development.

#### Commitment to Profession

The CanMEDS Commitment to Profession encompasses accountability to professional regulatory authorities, codes of ethics, commitment to patient safety and quality improvement, commitment to professional standards, awareness of conflicts of interest (personal, financial, administrative), knowledge of medico-legal frameworks governing practice, and responsibility to the profession including obligations of peer assessment, mentorship, collegiality, and support.

This commitment recognizes that physicians are stewards of the profession. They must support colleagues, mentor junior practitioners, engage in honest peer feedback, and contribute to maintaining professional standards. Understanding medico-legal frameworks and regulatory requirements protects both patients and practitioners. Conflict of interest management—whether personal, financial, or administrative—ensures that decisions are made in patients' best interests rather than practitioners' interests.

#### Commitment to Self

The CanMEDS Commitment to Self includes applied capacity for self-regulation—assessing and monitoring of one's thoughts, behaviours, emotions and attention for optimal performance and well-being—commitment to disclosure of error and/or adverse events and their impact, mindful and reflective approach to practice, professional identity and career development and transitions, resilience for sustainable practice, and responsibility to self including personal care in order to serve others.

Professional sustainability requires self-care. Practitioners cannot serve others effectively if they are burnt out, physically unwell, or emotionally compromised. Self-regulation involves honest self-assessment, seeking feedback, recognizing limitations, and pursuing support when needed. The capacity to disclose errors and learn from them is essential for patient safety and professional growth. Building professional identity and resilience helps practitioners navigate the challenges inherent in medical practice over a lifetime.

## Professional Attributes and Identity

Professional identity is more than a credential or job title—it is a deep integration of values, knowledge, skills, and behaviours into one's sense of self. Professionals develop this identity through education, mentorship, experience, and reflection. In medical education, students and trainees gradually internalize the norms, values, and ethical commitments of the profession, developing what might be called a "healer-professional model."

Professionalism requires the integration of clinical competence with ethical commitment, emotional intelligence, and social awareness. It is about becoming a certain kind of person—one who can be trusted with vulnerable patients, who prioritizes their welfare, who maintains integrity under pressure, and who contributes to the profession's reputation and standing. This transformation is neither quick nor automatic; it requires conscious effort, regular reflection, and ongoing engagement with professional values.

#### Professional Boundaries

Professional boundaries establish appropriate limits on practitioner-patient relationships. These boundaries protect both patients and practitioners. They prevent exploitation, maintain therapeutic effectiveness, and ensure that the relationship remains focused on the patient's needs. Inappropriate relationships—whether romantic, financial, or otherwise—violate professional boundaries and undermine trust.

## Unprofessional Behaviour: Understanding Violations

Understanding professionalism also requires recognizing what it is not. Unprofessional behaviour encompasses a range of actions that violate the trust placed in practitioners and the standards of the profession.

#### Unprofessional Behaviour Related to Patient Care

Unprofessional conduct in patient care includes criminal behaviour or conviction, behaviour that suggests a disregard for wellbeing of patients or members of the public and/or their dignity and rights, failing to honour clinical commitments, treating patients when not fit to do so, and agreeing to undertake a procedure for which the person lacks the necessary training, expertise or competence. These violations directly harm patients and breach the fundamental duty of beneficence. Treating patients while impaired—whether by illness, fatigue, substance use, or impaired judgment—puts patients at risk. Similarly, undertaking procedures beyond one's competence violates the duty to act in patients' best interests.

#### Unprofessional Interpersonal Behaviour

Unprofessional interpersonal behaviour includes being rude, abusive or disrespectful, showing a disregard for the time and effort of those relying on them (such as consistently bad timekeeping), making gratuitous criticism of colleagues or others, and engaging in inappropriate relationships with patients or employees. These behaviours damage the professional environment, undermine teamwork, and erode trust. Disrespect toward colleagues, staff, or patients violates the principle of dignity and creates toxic workplaces that compromise patient safety.

#### Unprofessional Behaviour Related to Standards and Conduct

Practitioners who accept unsatisfactory clinical standards, demonstrate an irresponsible or apathetic attitude, show laziness, sloppiness or lack of attention to detail, resist feedback, fail to maintain continuing professional development, or engage in dishonest business/financial dealings are failing in their professional obligations. These behaviours suggest that practitioners have lost sight of their commitment to excellence and accountability.

#### Unprofessional Behaviour Undermining Public Trust

The most serious category involves actions that directly undermine public confidence in the profession. This includes putting one's own financial or personal interests above patient care, any abuse of the doctor-patient relationship, anything that undermines public confidence in the profession, and anything that undermines the reputation or standing of the profession. These violations strike at the heart of the social contract and erode the profession's legitimacy.

## Professional Practice Areas: Contemporary Considerations

Modern medical practice encompasses many areas where professionalism must be actively considered and applied.

#### Digital Presence and Online Conduct

Social media presence and online persona, as well as photographs and online content, have become significant professional concerns. What practitioners share publicly affects their professional standing and the profession's reputation. Practitioners must consider how their online presence reflects on their professionalism and be mindful of confidentiality even in informal digital spaces.

#### Clinical Education and Student Development

Student learning and clerking of patients requires particular professionalism. Students must be supervised adequately, their competence must be assessed honestly, and patients must be informed about and consent to student involvement. Professionalism in education means mentoring trainees, providing constructive feedback, and modeling professional behaviour.

#### Pharmaceutical and Commercial Relationships

Interaction with pharmaceutical companies requires transparency and careful management of conflicts of interest. While physicians benefit from industry research and innovation, they must maintain independence in prescribing and clinical decisions. Financial relationships must be disclosed, and clinical judgment must never be compromised by industry interests.

#### Evidence-Based Practice and Clinical Decision-Making

Evidence-based medicine reflects professionalism—using the best available evidence to guide practice, recognizing uncertainty, and remaining open to new evidence. This approach respects patients by providing scientifically justified care while acknowledging the complexity of individual clinical situations.

#### Legal and Regulatory Compliance

Knowledge of rules, regulations and laws governing medical practice is essential. Practitioners must understand their legal obligations, scope of practice, and regulatory requirements. This knowledge protects both patients and practitioners.

#### Business and Self-Interest Management

Awareness of business and self-interest considerations helps practitioners recognize potential conflicts. Whether establishing a private practice, negotiating contracts, or making clinical decisions with financial implications, practitioners must ensure that business considerations do not compromise patient care or professional integrity.

#### Healthcare Economics

Understanding costs of medical care and the allocation of healthcare resources reflects mature professionalism. Practitioners should practice cost-effective medicine, be aware of expensive interventions, and consider resource allocation in clinical decisions—all while maintaining patient welfare as the primary concern.

#### Conflict Resolution

Professional skill in conflict resolution—whether with colleagues, patients, or administrative bodies—prevents disputes from escalating and maintains professional relationships. This includes the capacity to acknowledge when one is wrong and to take responsibility for errors.

#### Personal Life Boundaries

Finally, practitioners must establish boundaries between their personal lives and professional obligations. While maintaining personal health and relationships is essential, practitioners must be available and reliable in their professional commitments. The integration of personal and professional life is a lifelong challenge requiring ongoing reflection and adjustment.

## Conclusion: The Living Practice of Professionalism

Professionalism is not a state that is achieved and then maintained passively. Rather, it is a living practice—a continuous commitment to ethical behaviour, competence, accountability, and service. It is built on the foundation of trust between individual practitioners and their patients, and between the profession collectively and society.

For paediatricians and all physicians, professionalism means remembering that every patient encounter is an opportunity to affirm the values and commitments that define the profession. It means acting with integrity even when no one is watching, maintaining competence even when practice is busy, respecting patient autonomy even when time is short, and remaining accountable to those we serve.

The WMA definition, the HPCSA Section 27A requirements, the CanMEDS framework, and the concept of calling all point toward a unified vision: medicine is a profession of service, characterized by specialized knowledge, ethical commitment, self-regulation, and accountability. Practitioners who embrace this vision contribute not only to individual patient wellbeing but to the profession's standing and the public's trust.

As the field of paediatrics evolves and new challenges emerge, the fundamental principles of professionalism remain constant. They provide a moral compass that guides decision-making, a framework for reflecting on practice, and a commitment to excellence that defines what it means to be a physician.

-

## PORTFOLIO AND SCIENTIFIC WRITING

### Lecture Notes


#### Learning Objectives

The paediatric portfolio is designed to integrate bedside learning with textbook learning, emphasizing the application of theory to real clinical problems encountered in practice. Your primary objectives in this assignment are fourfold:

First, you will integrate bedside learning with book learning by focusing your learning on specific questions relating to real clinical problems. This means moving beyond passive consumption of information to active engagement with patient care scenarios and using them as the foundation for deeper understanding.

Second, you will improve the quality of your clinical interviewing and examination skills through careful documentation and reflection on your encounters with patients. Each case you document should represent an opportunity to refine your communication and physical examination techniques.

Third, you will develop your clinical reasoning skills, particularly in planning appropriate investigation and treatment strategies and formulating follow-up plans based on clinical findings and diagnostic reasoning.

Fourth, you will engage in reflection on the multiple dimensions of illness affecting your patient, considering not only the biological aspects but also the psychological, social, and ethical dimensions of clinical care.

#### Portfolio Structure and Requirements

The portfolio should contain information about patients you have clerked and managed during your ward rotation. Your selection of cases is important: choose patients who were either clerked on intake or newly admitted to the ward, as these cases will showcase your full engagement with the clinical process. You are required to document your patient records on standard A4 notepads, maintaining handwritten clerking notes that serve as the original case notes. These must be of high quality and fully represent your clinical work.

Your portfolio must consist entirely of your own original work. You are required to sign a declaration regarding plagiarism, confirming that all content is your own. Importantly, you may not use your formal case presentation as a portfolio case; these submissions must be separate pieces of work.

By the end of the second week of the block, you must submit one case in the format specified by the template available on Moodle. The template is mandatory, and you must adhere to it strictly. You should use recommended textbooks for additional detail and context, and you must develop your science writing skills throughout the process.

#### Formatting Rules and Technical Requirements

All portfolio submissions must comply with strict formatting specifications:

- Maximum length: 4 pages of A4-sized paper
- Font: Times New Roman
- Font size: 12 point
- Line spacing: Single spacing
- You will be penalized for not adhering to these specifications

These requirements apply uniformly to all submissions, and deviation from them may result in mark deductions.

#### Submission Timeline and Deadlines

Your portfolio case is due for midblock review, with submission scheduled for 8:00 AM on the second Friday of the block. This deadline is firm: all students not meeting the deadline will not be given deferred examination periods (DPs) initially. However, an appeal for a condoned DP may follow, during which the Department will specify whether they will accept your late submission.

If your submission is condoned as late, know that your marks will be capped at 50%, regardless of the quality of your work. Additionally, unsuitable submissions will be marked down and may receive a failing mark. The midblock portfolio is assessed according to departmental guidelines, and informal feedback will be provided to you. Students are encouraged to arrange a feedback session with the assessor to discuss their performance and areas for improvement.

#### Marking Allocation

The portfolio is marked out of a total of 100 points, with marks distributed across six major components:

1. **History: 25 marks** - Your ability to document a detailed, relevant clinical history using appropriate templates and your own words.

2. **Examination: 25 marks** - Your recording of physical examination findings, including anthropometry and nutritional assessment with interpretation.

3. **Assessment: 25 marks** - Your problem list, differential diagnosis formulation, and clinical reasoning. This is the largest weighted component.

4. **Plan: 10 marks** - Your proposed investigations and management strategy, including interpretation of results.

5. **Follow-up and Ethics: 10 marks** - Your structured follow-up documentation and critical reflection on the case's ethical dimensions and lessons learned.

6. **Science Writing: 5 marks** - Your adherence to proper writing standards, grammar, spelling, and formatting conventions.

**Total: 100 marks**

#### Clinical Case Report Sections

#### 1. History (25 marks)

A comprehensive history forms the foundation of your case report and represents one-quarter of your total mark. The history should be detailed and constructed according to BASIC templates and guidelines applicable to paediatric practice. However, templates are not fixed; you should adapt your approach based on the patient presentation. For example, neonates require the SAM (Subjective, Antenatal, Maternal) template rather than the standard BASIC framework.

Use your own words when documenting the history. Rather than copying verbatim from ward notes, synthesize information to demonstrate your understanding of the patient's problems. Focus on relevant information that reflects your clinical thought process: what information matters, and why did you pursue it?

Document the problems present at the time you saw the patient, and briefly note old or resolved problems in a "Previous Medical History" section. Avoid using long, complex sentences; instead, aim for clarity and conciseness. This approach demonstrates better clinical communication skills.

Ensure that the patient has a caregiver present during history taking when possible, as caregivers provide essential contextual and developmental information in paediatric cases. Consult Coovadia's textbook and other standard references for additional lists and frameworks, such as the Neonatal First Examination checklist.

**Example of Poor History Documentation:**
An antenatal history written as a single paragraph: "The mother said that she had been to the clinic and that the doctor had told her that she has hypertension. The doctor then put her onto medication and asked her to return in 2 weeks for another check-up. The mother said she was HIV positive and that she had started on ARVs during her pregnancy. She used to eat pears but now she no longer likes them."

**Example of Good History Documentation:**
The same information presented concisely:
- Mother: Hypertension on treatment
- HIV infected on ARVs

The second version is superior because it uses bullet points, removes irrelevant details (the pears), and presents key medical information at a glance.

#### 2. Examination (25 marks)

Physical examination findings represent another quarter of your total mark and should be documented with the same attention to detail as the history. Begin with anthropometry and nutritional assessment, providing not just the measurements but also your interpretation of what they signify for this particular patient.

Include general observations with your interpretation of their significance. Then document the general examination findings that are relevant to the presenting complaint. Proceed to systems examination, examining the relevant system first before moving to other systems. For example, if the patient presents with respiratory distress, examine the respiratory system in detail first, then complete examination of other systems to rule out contributing factors.

Document important positive findings (signs that are present) and important negative findings (expected findings that are notably absent). This demonstrates comprehensive examination and systematic thinking. Ensure that the signs you record are those you actually found during your examination of the patient. Do not include signs noted three weeks ago in previous examinations; document what you personally observed.

#### 3. Assessment (25 marks)

The assessment section carries equal weight to history and examination, reflecting the importance of clinical reasoning. Your assessment comprises two components: the problem list and the differential diagnosis.

**Problem List:**
Create a current, accurate, and thorough problem list that reflects competent and holistic care. List at least three important and relevant causes of the presenting complaint, demonstrating that you have considered multiple dimensions of the patient's illness.

**Differential Diagnosis:**
Provide at least four common differential diagnoses relevant to the presenting complaint. For each differential diagnosis, provide supporting evidence both for and against the diagnosis, drawing exclusively from the history and examination findings you have documented. Each piece of supporting evidence must be traceable to your own clinical findings.

Include a severity assessment for each differential diagnosis, considering the acuity of presentation and the potential consequences if that diagnosis were missed. This assessment demonstrates higher-order clinical reasoning and clinical judgment.

#### 4. Plan (10 marks)

Your plan section should address both investigations and management, representing 10% of your total mark.

**Investigations:**
Propose investigations that will aid in confirming your diagnosis. Include not just a list of tests but also your interpretation of the results. Ensure that all proposed investigations are relevant to your differential diagnoses; avoid ordering tests simply because they are available.

**Management:**
Outline both medical and non-medical management strategies. Your proposed management should align with accepted protocols and handbooks in paediatric practice. Be aware that in ward practice, you may observe several different treatment styles and approaches. Your role is to document evidence-based management while remaining aware of practical variations in clinical practice.

#### 5. Follow-Up and Ethics (10 marks)

Follow-up documentation should include two structured follow-up visits. Each follow-up should include both subjective and objective components, followed by your assessment and an updated plan based on the patient's progress.

Beyond clinical follow-up, use this section to reflect on the ethics and critical lessons from the case. Document what you learned from managing this patient. Consider the impact of the illness on the child, their family, and potentially on society or public health. This reflection demonstrates integration of clinical knowledge with professional values and demonstrates the personal growth that portfolio work is intended to foster.

#### 6. Science Writing (5 marks)

Scientific writing standards, worth 5% of your grade, encompass multiple dimensions:

**Writing mechanics:**
- Grammar: Ensure correct sentence structure, verb agreement, and tense consistency
- Spelling: Proofread carefully; spelling errors reflect poorly on professionalism
- Punctuation: Use punctuation correctly to enhance clarity
- Abbreviations: Use only standard medical abbreviations; define non-standard ones on first use

**Style and tone:**
- Avoid euphemisms and vague language (for example, "passed away" is less precise than "died"; similarly, be clear in your terminology)
- Avoid slang and informal expressions
- Use appropriate capitalization; avoid random capitalization
- Attend to gender-neutral language where appropriate

**Layout and formatting:**
- Organize content into clear sentences and paragraphs
- Use bullet points and lists for clarity where appropriate
- Apply Times New Roman font, size 12, with single spacing consistently throughout

Proper science writing demonstrates professionalism and enhances the clarity of your clinical reasoning for readers reviewing your work.

#### Plagiarism and Originality

All work submitted in your portfolio must be original. Before submission, your case notes should be reviewed and signed off by a consultant or senior medical officer at the teaching hospital, confirming that the notes and your analysis represent your own work.

You are required to complete and sign a plagiarism declaration confirming the originality of your submission. Your submission will be registered, and this registration serves as documentation of your declaration. Plagiarism is a serious academic offense and will result in significant consequences.

#### Resources and Templates

Several resources are available to support your portfolio work:

- **Moodle:** The course management system contains the mandatory template for case report formatting
- **Neonatal and Paediatric Clerking Templates:** Standard templates for documenting neonatal and paediatric patients are available through the course
- **Recommended Textbooks:** Coovadia's textbook and other referenced resources provide comprehensive guidance on paediatric history, examination, and assessment
- **Departmental Guidelines:** Additional lists and frameworks (such as the Neonatal First Examination checklist) are available from departmental resources

Use these resources actively throughout your portfolio preparation to ensure high-quality, evidence-based case documentation.
