## RESP EXAM BY DR HLOPHE

### Lecture Notes


#### Introduction and Communication

Before beginning your examination of a paediatric patient, establish rapport with both the child and their parents or guardians. Introduce yourself clearly, stating your name and your role. This foundational step is essential for building trust, particularly with anxious children. Following your introduction, confirm key identifying information: ask for and verify the child's full name and date of birth. These details are not merely administrative—they ensure patient safety and help you identify any developmental landmarks or age-related norms against which you'll interpret your findings.

#### General Appearance and Initial Observation

A systematic general observation begins the moment you set eyes on your patient. Assess the child's activity level and alertness: does the child appear engaged and interactive, or quiet and listless? Lethargy may signal serious illness. Next, observe the child's weight distribution and overall nutritional status, noting whether they appear a healthy weight for their stated age and height. 

Look for cyanosis, a bluish discolouration of the skin that can arise from two distinct mechanisms: poor peripheral circulation due to hypovolaemia with peripheral vasoconstriction, or inadequate oxygenation of blood such as occurs with right-to-left cardiac shunting. Pallor—paleness of the skin—suggests either underlying anaemia from blood dyscrasia or chronic disease, or poor perfusion from conditions like congestive cardiac failure. 

Pay particular attention to syndromic features: assess the child's stature (tall or short) and look for distinctive facial features that may point to underlying genetic conditions. This systematic observation guides your subsequent focused examination.

#### Respiratory Symptoms: Cough and Voice Changes

The character of a cough provides valuable diagnostic clues. A cough accompanied by wheeze suggests asthma or viral-induced wheeze, particularly in young children where viral wheeze is common. A productive cough—one where sputum is being cleared—indicates lower respiratory tract infection. A characteristic barking or seal-like cough points toward croup or laryngomalacia. In contrast, a dry, non-productive cough is typical of allergies or tuberculosis. Voice changes warrant equal attention. Hoarseness indicates laryngitis, while a distinctive "hot potato" quality to the voice (as if speaking with a hot object in the mouth) suggests peritonsillar abscess.

Stridor, a high-pitched breathing sound reflecting turbulent airflow through narrowed upper airways, comes in two varieties. Acute stridor is seen with croup, foreign body aspiration, bacterial tracheitis, and epiglottitis—conditions requiring prompt recognition. Chronic stridor suggests laryngomalacia or subglottic stenosis, conditions that may be developmental or acquired. Understanding these distinctions helps you triage urgency appropriately.

#### Hands and Pulse Assessment

Examination of the hands reveals numerous signs relevant to respiratory and cardiac pathology. Pallor in the hands specifically suggests poor peripheral perfusion, a hallmark of congestive heart failure. Cyanosis of the hands indicates underlying hypoxaemia. Hand tremor, particularly if fine and rapid, suggests recent beta-2 agonist use such as salbutamol in an asthmatic child. The presence of eczema, particularly on the hands, indicates atopy and significantly increases the likelihood of asthma and allergic rhinitis in the child.

Finger clubbing—a distinctive soft tissue swelling of the terminal phalanx—is a sign of chronic lung disease. The three conditions most commonly associated with clubbing in children are bronchiectasis, cystic fibrosis, and primary ciliary dyskinesia. To assess for clubbing, use Schamroth's window: ask the child to place the tips of their index fingers back-to-back, nails facing each other. In healthy individuals, a small diamond-shaped space is visible between the nails. When clubbing develops, this characteristic window is lost. In younger children who cannot cooperate with this test, simply inspect the fingers for telltale soft tissue swelling of the terminal phalanx.

Pulse assessment varies by age. In older children and adolescents, palpate the radial pulse using the tips of your index and middle fingers to assess both rate and rhythm. Measure for a full 60 seconds to ensure accuracy, as heart rate irregularities may be intermittent. In infants and very young babies, however, assess the femoral pulse instead—it is more reliable and easier to locate than the radial pulse in this age group.

#### Face, Eyes, Ears, and Nose

Examine the face systematically. Nasal flaring—outward movement of the nostrils with each breath—indicates increased work of breathing and should prompt you to look for other signs of respiratory distress. Micrognathia, an undersized jaw, is seen in genetic syndromes including Pierre Robin, Noonan, and Marfan syndromes, and can predispose to airway obstruction.

The eyes offer diagnostic clues. Inspect the conjunctiva by gently pulling down the lower eyelid. Conjunctival pallor suggests underlying anaemia. Hearing loss should raise your suspicion for primary ciliary dyskinesia, a systemic disorder affecting mucociliary clearance.

Nasal findings are equally informative. A deviated nasal septum may contribute to breathing difficulties or impaired airway patency. Nasal polyps are associated with atopy and with cystic fibrosis and warrant investigation in the appropriate clinical context.

#### Mouth and Upper Airways

Central cyanosis—bluish discolouration specifically of the lips and/or tongue—indicates hypoxaemia and requires urgent evaluation. The causes include persistent pulmonary hypertension, bronchospasm from asthma, and lower respiratory tract infection. Central cyanosis differs from peripheral cyanosis and demands immediate attention.

Examine the palate by visual inspection of its full extent, looking for any cleft. Tonsillar hypertrophy indicates a history of recurrent tonsillitis and raises concern for possible airway obstruction, particularly if severe.

#### Chest Wall and Respiratory Rate

The chest wall itself provides important structural and dynamic information. Pectus excavatum—a distinctive caved-in or sunken appearance of the chest—is associated with Marfan syndrome. Pectus cariniatum, in contrast, shows protrusion of the sternum and ribs, and is associated with Noonan syndrome. Asymmetry of chest wall movement suggests underlying pneumothorax or consolidation and warrants further investigation.

Harrison's sulcus, seen as indrawing or grooving of the chest wall, results from long-term diaphragmatic tug in children with poorly controlled asthma. Barrel chest, or hyper-expansion of the thorax, can be associated with both acute and chronic asthma and with chronic respiratory obstruction.

Respiratory rate must be age-appropriate. Tachypnoea is a sensitive sign of respiratory compromise in children. The normal ranges by age are as follows:

- **Newborns**: 40–60 breaths per minute
- **1 week to 3 months**: 30–50 breaths per minute
- **3 months to 2 years**: 30–40 breaths per minute
- **2 to 10 years**: 14–24 breaths per minute
- **Over 10 years**: 12–20 breaths per minute

#### Work of Breathing and Cardiac Assessment

Increased work of breathing manifests through multiple signs that the experienced clinician recognises together. Watch for difficulty speaking in full sentences or difficulty feeding—both suggest the child is devoting significant energy to breathing. Expiratory grunting, a sound made as the child exhales against a partially closed glottis, represents an attempt to maintain positive end-expiratory pressure and keep alveoli open; its presence signals increased work of breathing.

Recession—inward indrawing of the chest wall during inspiration—occurs at multiple levels. Look for tracheal tug (visible pulling at the trachea in the neck), supraclavicular recession (above the collarbones), intercostal recession (between the ribs), and subcostal recession (below the rib cage). Accessory muscle use includes nasal flaring, abdominal breathing (using the belly muscles rather than the diaphragm), and head bobbing with respiration—all indicate the child is struggling to breathe.

The apex beat, where you can palpate the heartbeat at the chest wall, is normally located in the 5th intercostal space in the midclavicular line. Displacement of the apex beat reflects underlying cardiac or pulmonary pathology. Leftward displacement occurs with cardiomegaly (an enlarged heart), right pleural effusion, or right tension pneumothorax. Rightward displacement is seen with dextrocardia (the heart on the right side of the chest), diaphragmatic hernia, left pleural effusion, or left tension pneumothorax. The pattern of displacement helps localise pathology.

#### Chest Expansion and Percussion

To assess chest expansion, place your hands on the chest wall just below the nipples, wrap your fingers around the sides of the thorax, and bring your thumbs together in the midline without touching the skin. During inspiration, the thumbs should move symmetrically upward and outward; during expiration, they should move downward and inward. Reduced movement of one thumb indicates reduced chest expansion on that side, suggesting lung collapse, pneumonia, or restrictive lung disease.

Tracheal deviation, assessed by palpating the trachea at the sternal notch, indicates displacement from midline. In pneumothorax and large pleural effusions, the trachea deviates away from the pathology—that is, toward the opposite side. Conversely, in lobar collapse or following pneumonectomy, the trachea deviates toward the pathology on the affected side. This distinction aids in differential diagnosis.

Percussion is a skill requiring careful technique. Position your non-dominant hand on the chest wall with the middle finger firmly pressed against the thorax. Using a brisk wrist swing, strike the middle phalanx of that middle finger with the middle finger of your dominant hand. Immediately remove the striking finger to avoid muffling the resulting percussion note. 

Percuss systematically across the chest: the supraclavicular region (lung apices), infraclavicular region, the chest wall at 3–4 locations bilaterally, and the axilla. Always compare findings side-to-side to detect asymmetry.

A resonant percussion note is the normal finding in healthy lung. Dullness to percussion indicates increased tissue density and may reflect cardiac dullness, consolidation, tumour, or lobar collapse. Stony dullness—a particularly flat, lifeless percussion note—is pathognomonic for pleural effusion, where fluid in the pleural space dampens vibrations. Hyper-resonance, conversely, indicates decreased tissue density and strongly suggests pneumothorax, where air in the pleural space enhances resonance.

#### Auscultation: Breath Sounds and Added Sounds

Normal breath sounds heard on auscultation are vesicular in quality—a soft, rustling sound reflecting air moving through small airways and alveoli. Bronchial breath sounds, by contrast, are harsh and high-pitched, similar to what you hear when auscultating directly over the trachea. Bronchial breath sounds are characterised by equal duration of inspiration and expiration with a pause between phases. When you hear bronchial breath sounds over the periphery of the lung, they indicate consolidation—the lung tissue is filled with fluid or pus rather than air, so sound transmits directly without the normal filtering effect of air-filled alveoli.

Quiet or diminished breath sounds indicate reduced air entry and suggest either pleural effusion or pneumothorax, where air or fluid outside the lung prevents normal air movement.

Added sounds heard during auscultation carry specific meanings. Wheeze is a continuous, coarse, whistling sound produced in the respiratory airways during breathing. Wheeze indicates obstruction to airflow and is classically associated with asthma and bronchiectasis. Stridor, as mentioned earlier, is a high-pitched sound reflecting turbulent airflow through narrowed upper airways; it can be acute (croup, foreign body, bacterial tracheitis, epiglottitis) or chronic (foreign body, subglottic stenosis).

Coarse crackles are discontinuous, brief, popping lung sounds. Unlike wheeze, which is continuous, crackles sound like the crackling of Velcro or pop-pop-popping. Coarse crackles indicate the presence of fluid or secretions in larger airways and are heard in pneumonia, bronchiectasis, and pulmonary oedema.

#### Vocal Resonance

Vocal resonance assessment complements auscultation of breath sounds. Ask the child to repeat a word or number consistently—"99" is commonly used—while you listen over different areas of the chest wall with your stethoscope. Compare the same location on both sides of the chest to detect asymmetry.

The volume at which the child's speech is transmitted to your stethoscope depends on the acoustic properties of the lung tissue beneath. Increased tissue density—as occurs with consolidation, tumour, or lobar collapse—transmits voice sounds more readily, resulting in increased vocal resonance. Conversely, the presence of fluid or air outside the lung (pleural effusion or pneumothorax) acts as a barrier, reducing the transmission of voice sounds and resulting in decreased vocal resonance. These findings help you localise and characterise pathology alongside your other examination findings.

#### Lymph Node Assessment

Lymph node examination requires proper technique to be informative. Position yourself behind the child and use both hands to palpate the neck. This allows you to assess for asymmetry in size, consistency, and mobility of lymph nodes between the two sides—asymmetry may be more significant than absolute size.

Use the pads of your second, third, and fourth fingers (not the fingertips) to palpate. A critical safety consideration applies: do not compromise cerebral blood flow by pressing too hard or for too long on the neck vessels.

You should systematically assess the following lymph node chains: submental (beneath the chin), submandibular (under the jaw), pre-auricular (in front of the ears), post-auricular (behind the ears), superficial cervical, deep cervical, posterior cervical, and supraclavicular (above the collarbones). Enlargement of these nodes may reflect infection, inflammation, or other pathology and should be correlated with the clinical presentation.

#### Oedema

Oedema—abnormal accumulation of fluid in tissues causing swelling—can be a sign of significant systemic disease. Begin your assessment by asking the parents whether they have noticed that the child looks puffy or swollen. This observation from those who see the child daily is invaluable.

On inspection, examine the limbs, the sacral area (the lower back), and the face for signs of oedema. The distribution and location of oedema depend on the child's age and mobility status. In mobile children, gravity pulls fluid downward, so dependent oedema appears in the lower legs and feet. In infants and non-mobile children, oedema may be more prominent in the sacral area or face. The presence of oedema warrants investigation into its cause: it may indicate renal disease, cardiac disease with poor perfusion, or other systemic conditions affecting fluid balance.

---

This comprehensive examination, conducted systematically and interpreted in clinical context, forms the foundation of paediatric respiratory assessment and guides appropriate investigation and management.

-

## RSV 2023 ALERT FOR HEALTHCARE WORKERS

### Lecture Notes


#### Epidemiology

Respiratory syncytial virus (RSV) is the most common cause of bronchiolitis and lower respiratory tract infection (LRTI) among young children. During the 2023 southern hemisphere season, which commenced in week 6 (the week starting 6 February 2023), RSV detection rates among children aged less than 5 years were at moderate levels and continued to rise steeply throughout the season. The 2023 detection rate was rising more sharply than the mean circulation observed between 2014-2019, although it remained within the historical range of what had been seen in the past.

In contrast, the northern hemisphere experienced a significant RSV 2022-2023 season from November to February, during which there were reports of increased severe RSV-associated illness and hospitalisation, including in children greater than 1 year of age—a departure from the typical pattern of RSV disease predominantly affecting younger infants.

#### Transmission

RSV is highly contagious with transmission occurring mainly by respiratory droplets. This mode of transmission is central to infection control strategies and explains why isolation measures are critical during periods of high RSV circulation.

#### Clinical Presentation

Infants aged less than 6 months are at particular risk of severe RSV disease, which may manifest with hypoxia, severe respiratory distress (characterised by tachypnoea, nasal flaring, or lower chest retractions), inability to feed, or apnoea requiring hospitalisation. The physical examination findings of severe respiratory distress are tachypnoea, nasal flaring, and lower chest retractions.

Very young infants may present atypically, with only irritability, decreased activity, and breathing difficulties as the primary symptoms, making clinical recognition challenging in this age group. It is important to note that whilst the majority of infants with RSV-associated bronchiolitis do not require hospitalisation, certain children—particularly those with underlying risk factors—are at significant risk of severe disease requiring hospital admission.

#### Risk Groups

Multiple risk factors increase the likelihood of severe RSV-associated disease. These include prematurity, congenital heart disease, chronic lung disease of prematurity, neurological disease, age less than 6 months, immunodeficiency, and lack of breastfeeding. Healthcare workers should maintain a high index of suspicion for severe disease when these risk factors are present.

#### Prevention

Infection control measures are paramount in reducing RSV transmission. Isolation of children with influenza-like symptoms—such as not attending crèches or schools for a few days—is an effective prevention strategy given RSV's high contagiousness via respiratory droplets. Teaching children and caregivers responsible for infants to practice sneeze and cough hygiene is a recommended prevention measure that can be implemented across healthcare and community settings.

Prophylactic antibiotic use is not recommended for children with upper respiratory tract infections, as RSV is a viral infection and routine antibiotics provide no benefit.

#### Palivizumab

Palivizumab is a monoclonal antibody administered monthly throughout the RSV season to infants and children at high risk of severe RSV disease. Clinical evidence has demonstrated that palivizumab is effective for prevention of severe RSV disease in high-risk infants and children. However, practical limitations exist: the therapy is associated with high costs and requires monthly intramuscular injections throughout the entire RSV season, which may limit access and adherence in some settings.

#### Surveillance

Surveillance of RSV circulation relies on multiple data sources. Weekly reports documenting RSV circulation are available from pneumonia surveillance data, with weekly respiratory pathogen surveillance reports published by the National Institute for Communicable Diseases (NICD). The Moving Epidemic Method (MEM) is employed as a sequential analysis tool using the R Language to calculate the duration, start, and end of the annual epidemic based on data from the previous 5 years. For RSV 2023, surveillance thresholds were based on 2015-2019 data, providing a validated reference frame for detecting unusual activity.

#### Seasonal Patterns

RSV and influenza have distinct seasonal patterns. RSV typically circulates first, with average season onset at the end of February (range: early February to mid-March) based on 10-year historical data excluding the 2020-2021 period. Influenza season follows later in the calendar year, providing a temporal window for differential diagnosis and resource planning. This predictable seasonal offset is crucial for healthcare planning, as RSV surges precede influenza season.

#### Diagnostic Guidance

Clinicians should consider RSV in the differential diagnosis for severe respiratory illness, especially in young children. Diagnostic evaluation should include assessment of risk factors and careful evaluation for signs of severe disease such as hypoxia and respiratory distress. Given the northern hemisphere experience of increased severe disease in 2022-2023 and the anticipated surge in the 2023 southern hemisphere season, clinicians and paediatric hospitals or intensive care units should anticipate an increase in paediatric admissions and prepare by allocating adequate resources to respond effectively to the surge in RSV cases.

-

## THE BLUE CHILD 2021

### Lecture Notes


#### Definition and Pathophysiology of Cyanosis

Cyanosis is a bluish discoloration, especially of the skin and mucous membranes, due to excessive concentration of deoxyhemoglobin in the blood. It represents one of the most clinically important signs in pediatric practice, particularly in neonates and infants presenting with respiratory or cardiac compromise. Understanding cyanosis requires knowledge of the normal physiology of oxygen transport and the pathologic mechanisms that disrupt it.

Two fundamental mechanisms result in cyanosis. The first is systemic arterial oxygen desaturation, which occurs when insufficient oxygen is delivered to the arterial blood due to pulmonary or cardiac pathology. The second mechanism is increased oxygen extraction by the tissues, resulting in elevated levels of deoxyhemoglobin in the venous circulation and capillary beds. Recognition of which mechanism predominates is essential for accurate diagnosis and appropriate management.

#### Hemoglobin Biochemistry and Thresholds

Normal hemoglobin exists in two main chemical forms. Reduced hemoglobin, symbolized HbFe+2, is hemoglobin unbound to oxygen and is responsible for the characteristic bluish coloration associated with cyanosis. A critical threshold exists for clinically apparent central cyanosis: 5 g/dL of reduced (deoxygenated) hemoglobin must be present in the capillaries, which translates to approximately 3.4 g/dL in arterial blood. This threshold is important because it is absolute and does not correlate with oxygen saturation or hemoglobin concentration alone; a severely anemic infant may be profoundly hypoxic without appearing cyanotic, while a polycythemic infant with relatively normal oxygenation may appear cyanotic.

Methemoglobin, symbolized HbFe+3, is the oxidized form of hemoglobin that imparts an intense bluish tinge to the skin. Normally, about 2% of hemoglobin is in the form of methemoglobin; this small proportion is maintained by reducing enzymes present in red blood cells. When this balance is disrupted and methemoglobin accumulates, cyanosis can occur even with normal arterial oxygen saturation, making differentiation from other causes of cyanosis diagnostically important.

#### Central Versus Peripheral Cyanosis

Central cyanosis represents bluish discoloration of the skin and mucous membranes in core areas, particularly the lips and tongue, due to systemic arterial oxygen desaturation. When central cyanosis is present, it indicates a primary defect in oxygenation at the systemic level. The mucous membranes are involved because they receive poorly oxygenated blood.

Peripheral cyanosis, by contrast, appears as bluish discoloration only of the extremities or fingers and is caused by increased oxygen extraction from sluggish blood movement through the capillary beds. In peripheral cyanosis, the systemic arterial oxygen saturation may be entirely normal; the mechanism is increased oxygen extraction resulting in a wide arteriovenous oxygen difference and accumulated deoxygenated blood on the venous side of the capillary beds. This distinction has significant diagnostic implications, as peripheral cyanosis indicates problems with tissue perfusion or local blood flow rather than primary pulmonary or cardiac oxygenation failure.

#### Systemic Causes of Central Cyanosis

The etiologies of central cyanosis are organized by physiologic system. Respiratory system causes include pneumonia, bronchiolitis, bronchospasm (asthma), pulmonary hypertension, pulmonary embolism, and hypoventilation. These conditions result in inadequate ventilation, diffusion impairment, or ventilation-perfusion mismatch, leading to arterial oxygen desaturation.

Cardiovascular causes of central cyanosis include cyanotic heart disease, congenital heart disease, heart failure, and valvar heart disease. Right-to-left shunting allows deoxygenated blood to bypass the lungs and enter the systemic circulation directly, causing profound cyanosis. Non-shunting cardiac lesions such as severe heart failure may cause cyanosis through secondary pulmonary edema and ventilation-perfusion mismatch.

Central nervous system causes produce cyanosis through hypoventilation and include intracranial hemorrhage, drug overdose (particularly heroin), tonic-clonic seizures, and other seizure disorders. These conditions impair the neural drive to respiration, reducing minute ventilation and causing hypoxia.

Blood disorders causing central cyanosis include methemoglobinemia, polycythemia, hemoglobinopathies, and CO poisoning (carbon monoxide intoxication). Methemoglobinemia is of particular clinical importance and is discussed in detail below.

Environmental and other causes of central cyanosis include high altitude greater than 2400 m, where reduced atmospheric oxygen pressure decreases alveolar and arterial oxygen tension; hypothermia, which reduces metabolic oxygen demand but may impair central nervous system function; and obstructive sleep apnea, which causes recurrent hypoxic episodes.

#### Methemoglobinemia

Methemoglobinemia deserves special emphasis as a cause of cyanosis that can be rapidly life-threatening and requires specific treatment. Several drugs are known to induce methemoglobinemia by oxidizing the iron in hemoglobin from the ferrous (Fe+2) to ferric (Fe+3) state. Common culprits include nitrite and nitrate compounds such as nitroglycerin, topical anesthetics including benzocaine (particularly when used for pharyngeal anesthesia), and dapsone (used in certain infections and dermatologic conditions).

When methemoglobinemia develops, the classic presentation is cyanosis that does not respond to oxygen therapy. The diagnosis should be suspected in any cyanotic patient who does not improve with supplemental oxygen. The specific treatment involves reducing agents such as methylene blue or ascorbic acid, depending on the clinical severity and the presence of glucose-6-phosphatase deficiency.

#### Mechanisms of Peripheral Cyanosis

Peripheral cyanosis occurs in the setting of normal systemic arterial oxygen saturation but is characterized by increased oxygen extraction from tissues. This creates a wide arteriovenous oxygen difference, with increased deoxygenated hemoglobin accumulating on the venous side of the capillary beds.

Causes of peripheral cyanosis due to reduced cardiac output include heart failure and hypovolemia, both of which reduce blood flow to the periphery and increase peripheral tissue oxygen extraction. Vascular causes include arterial obstruction from peripheral vascular disease or Raynaud phenomenon, and venous obstruction from deep vein thrombosis. Non-cardiac causes include cold exposure, which causes peripheral vasoconstriction and increased local oxygen extraction, and sepsis, in which severe illness and possible septic shock reduce peripheral perfusion.

#### Respiratory Gas Exchange: The Foundation

To understand cyanosis fully, one must recall the five steps of respiratory gas exchange. These are: ventilation, the exchange of gas between the atmosphere and the alveoli; diffusion across the alveolar-capillary membrane, where oxygen enters the blood and carbon dioxide is eliminated; transport of gases in the blood by hemoglobin and dissolved oxygen; diffusion from the capillaries to the cells at the tissue level; and finally, the use of oxygen and production of carbon dioxide within the cells. Disruption at any of these five steps can lead to hypoxia and potential cyanosis.

#### Diagnostic Approach to Cyanosis

The clinical approach to cyanosis follows a systematic methodology. History and clinical assessment form the foundation, including the age of onset, triggers, and associated symptoms. Physical examination includes auscultation for cardiac murmurs, palpation of femoral artery pulses (to assess for coarctation or ductal-dependent systemic flow), measurement of blood pressure in all four limbs, the oxygen test (administration of 100% oxygen to assess response), and chest imaging for cardiomegaly.

The hypoxia test, or "hyperoxia test," is particularly valuable. In infants with central cyanosis from pulmonary disease, administering 100% oxygen typically results in improvement of saturation above 90-95%, as the lungs are capable of oxygenating blood when adequate oxygen is supplied. In contrast, infants with cyanotic heart disease remain desaturated or hypoxemic even when given 100% oxygen, because deoxygenated blood bypasses the lungs via right-to-left shunting. This simple test is one of the most powerful diagnostic tools available at the bedside.

Pulse oximetry provides rapid, non-invasive assessment of peripheral oxygen saturation and is based on pulsatile absorbance. Deoxygenated blood absorbs more red light, while oxygenated blood absorbs more infrared light. Two wavelengths of light are passed through an arterial bed, and the ratio of infrared and red light transmitted to the photodetector is determined and converted to a saturation percentage. It is essential to recognize that pulse oximetry measures saturation, not content, and may be unreliable in peripheral cyanosis where perfusion is poor.

Chest X-ray is a key investigation. The cardiothoracic ratio (CTR) is calculated by measuring the widest diameter of the heart and dividing by the widest internal diameter of the thorax. A cardiothoracic ratio greater than 50% on a PA (posteroanterior) view indicates cardiomegaly and suggests a cardiac etiology for cyanosis. The X-ray also assesses pulmonary parenchyma for evidence of pneumonia, pulmonary edema, or other lung disease.

Additional investigations for suspected congenital heart disease include blood gas analysis (to assess degree of hypoxia and acid-base status), lactate (elevated in shock states), CRP (C-reactive protein, to assess for infection), and FBC (full blood count, to assess hemoglobin and white blood cell count).

#### Clinical Features: Distinguishing Pulmonary from Cardiac Etiologies

The clinical presentation helps differentiate the primary cause of cyanosis. Features suggesting congenital heart disease in a cyanotic infant include cyanosis with minimal or no respiratory distress (the infant appears "blue but comfortable"), presence of cardiorespiratory failure, the infant remaining desaturated or hypoxemic even when given 100% oxygen, and notably, the absence of a murmur (many serious cyanotic lesions produce no murmur).

In contrast, features suggesting pulmonary disease in a cyanotic infant include cyanosis accompanied by respiratory distress (the infant is "blue and uncomfortable"), with tachypnea and rib retractions evident. Critically, the infant's saturations improve significantly on 100% oxygen, and there is clinical evidence of respiratory failure. The presence of respiratory distress is a major distinguishing feature.

A mixed presentation may occur with respiratory distress and cyanosis that can be confused with infectious etiologies such as sepsis, pneumonia, meconium aspiration, or respiratory distress syndrome (RDS). In these cases, detailed history, examination for cardiac signs, and the hyperoxia test are crucial to differentiate.

#### Management of Pulmonary Cyanosis

The initial management for suspected pulmonary disease is straightforward: supply oxygen via appropriate means (nasal prongs for mild hypoxia, face mask for moderate hypoxia, or CPAP/mechanical ventilation for severe cases) and start antibiotics if infection is suspected. This approach addresses both the hypoxia and potential infectious etiologies.

#### Tetralogy of Fallot: A Case Example

Tetralogy of Fallot illustrates the clinical presentation of a significant cyanotic heart lesion. A typical case involves a 3-month-old infant with fast breathing, cyanosis since birth, a loud murmur audible on auscultation, warm periphery (indicating adequate cardiac output), spell-like attacks characterized by acute episodes of respiratory distress and hypercyanosis, and notably, normal growth and development. The warm periphery and maintained growth indicate the infant is compensating well despite the anatomic defect.

Diagnostic confirmation of Tetralogy of Fallot involves history and physical examination, the oxygen test (demonstrating minimal improvement with supplemental oxygen), chest X-ray (showing characteristic "boot-shaped" heart), blood gas analysis (documenting the degree of hypoxia), lactate (typically normal unless there is associated shock), CRP (to rule out infection), and FBC (to assess hemoglobin, as polycythemia often develops in response to chronic hypoxia).

#### Emergency Management of Cyanotic Heart Disease: The Prostaglandin E1 Protocol

A critical clinical adage guides emergency management: "In doubt, give prostin AND antibiotics. This is a lifesaving intervention." This phrase encapsulates the importance of not delaying treatment while awaiting definitive diagnosis in a cyanotic neonate who may have ductal-dependent congenital heart disease.

The significance of maintaining ductal patency in cyanotic heart disease cannot be overstated. Opening and maintaining ductal patency is the only medical intervention that can restore adequate systemic cardiac output in ductal-dependent lesions, which are those in which either systemic or pulmonary blood flow depends on right-to-left shunting through the patent ductus arteriosus. Examples include critical aortic stenosis (ductal-dependent systemic output) and critical pulmonary stenosis or pulmonary atresia (ductal-dependent pulmonary output).

Prostaglandin E1 (PGE1) is the pharmacologic agent used to maintain ductal patency. The dosing is 0.05 to 0.1 mcg/kg/min, infused intravenously. This infusion is started emergently, immediately upon suspicion of ductal-dependent heart disease, and continued until a cardiac echocardiogram can be obtained to define the anatomy and guide further management. The therapeutic window is narrow; delayed administration can result in closure of the ductus arteriosus and sudden severe deterioration, while prolonged use of PGE1 risks side effects such as apnea and systemic hypotension.

PGE1 should be considered emergently in neonates presenting in shock within the first 2 weeks of life with suspected ductal-dependent systemic output. The clinical scenario is a term or near-term infant who appears well at birth but deteriorates during the first days to weeks of life with signs of shock, poor perfusion, metabolic acidosis, and cyanosis. Rapid diagnosis and initiation of PGE1 therapy can be lifesaving, providing time for echocardiographic diagnosis and definitive surgical intervention.

#### Conclusion

The blue child represents a spectrum of pathophysiology ranging from simple hypoxia from pulmonary disease to complex cardiac anatomy with ductal dependence. Mastery of the definitions, mechanisms, diagnostic approach, and management algorithms outlined in this lecture provides the framework for safe and effective care of these critically ill infants.

-
